THE RED PAGE
FLEET STREET.
Closes and courts and lanes,
Devious, clustered thick,
The thoroughfare, mains and drains,
People, and mortar and brick.
Wood, metal, machinery, brains,
Pen and composing-stick :
Fleet Street now, but exquisite flame
In the nebula ofice, ere day and night
Began their travail, or earth became,
And all was passionate light.
Networks of wire overhead,
Conduits under the sea,
Aerial message from strand to strand
By lightning that travels free,
Hither in haste to hand
Tidings of destiny;
There tingling nerves of the world’s affairs
Deliver remorseless, rendering still
The fall of empires, the price of shares,
The record of good and ill.
Tidal the traffic goes,
Citywards out of the town ;
Townwards the evening ebb o’erflows
This highway of old renown,
When the fog-woven curtains close,
And the urban night comes down.
Where souls are spilt and intellects spent
O’er news vociferant near and far,
From Hesperus hard to the Orient,
From dawn to the evening star.
This is the royal refrain
That burdens the boom and the thud
Of omnibus, mobus, wain,
And the hoofs on the beaten mud,
From the Griffin at CJhancery Lane
To the portal of old King Lud—
Fleet Street, diligent, night and day ;
Of news the mart and the burnished
. hearth;
Seven hundred paces of narrow way,
A notable bit of the earth.
John Davidson (in The Pall Mall Magazine).
FLEET STREET.
Beneath this narrow, jostling street,
Unruffled by the noise of feet,
Like a slow organ-note I hear
The pulses of the great world beat.
Unseen beneath the city’s show
Through this aorta ever flow
The currents of the universe—
A thousand pulses throbbing low.
Unheard beneath the pavement’s din
Unknown magicians sib within
Dim caves, and weave life into words
On patient looms that spin and spin.
There, uninspired, yet with the dower
Of mightier mechanic power,
Some bent, obscure Euripides
Builds the loud drama of the hour.
There, from the gaping presses hurled,
A thousand voices, passion-whirled,
With throats of steel vociferate
The incessant story of the world.
So through this artery from age
To age the tides of passion rage,
The swift historians of each day
Flinging a world upon a page.
And then I pause and gaze my fill
Where cataracts of traffic spill
Their foam into the Circus. Lo,
Look up, the crown on Ludgate Hill 1
Remote from all the city’s moods,
In high, untroubled solitudes,
Like an old Buddha swathed in dream,
St. Paul’s above the city broods.
Arthur H. Adams (in “ London Streets ”).
THE YOUNG AUTHORS' CLASS
CAN the art of authorship be taught?
Is it desirable, or possible, to train a
youth to be a novelist, to bring him
up to the hazardous profession of a
writer of stories ? With her business acumen,
America thinks the scheme is practicable, and,
while leaving the education of novelists to
chance, has already got to work upon the
profession of short-story writing. Whether
the prevailing tenuity of the usual short
story in the American magazines is the
result of, or the excuse for, this scheme may be
left to individual judgment; but the United
States is desperately in earnest. If the art
of the short story can be taught, the United
States means to produce a race of authors ;
several of the universities possess professors
in the art, and classes of embryo authors are
already hard at work. “ Courses in the
short story ” are an integral part of the
training of the American literary student,
and the demand for this instruction has pro-
duced at least a dozen text-books. A recent
article in this page gave some personal advice
to the beginner in short-story composition.
Let us supplement this by the opinions of
Evelyn May Albright, Instructor in English
at the Ohio Wesleyan University. Evelyn’s
opinions are embodied in The Short Story:
its Principles and Structure (Macmillan).
In scope and style, if not in subject-
matter, the Ohio Wesleyan Instructor points
out, the modem short story is as nearly akin
to the drama as to the novel. The emphasis
given to situation rather than to a sequence
of events, the preferences for crises, and
the use. of impressionistic methods are
derived from the technique of the drama.
Evelyn gives much information for the bene-
fit of the short-story class, and illustrates her
argument with quotations from the generally
puerile efforts of her students. However, her
main principles are well based, and the book is
worth study. Evelyn calls attention to the fact
that many people are tempted to begin on a
full scale too far back in the history of the
story, instead of stationing themselves at a
good central point, where the early begin-
nings can be seen in dim perspective as
small as they really are for story purposes.
The story should be conceived, not as a mere
continuous run of events, but in a few
striking scenes, more or less closely joined.
The right cut in the line of action is near the
main incident, before and after. The main
incident may thus ba expanded, narrated on
full scale as it deserves, and the preliminary
action reaching back into the commonplaces
of experience will seem to be diminishing
in perspective. This artificial proportion
acquired by foreshortening is one of the
main mechanical distinctions between short-
story and mere narrative. The writer should
shun the introductory par. The short-story
conclusion should not be descriptive nor
expository. Rather it should be made of
typical narrative material, preferably a de-
cision or an act narrated by the author, or
suggested by the speech of the characters.
The novel aims to show growth of
character. It portrays a certain period, or
the whole, of life—but with the aim of por-
traying growth. The short-story has to do
with change in character—the cross-road,
rather than the main road travelled : charac-
ter at a crisis. Owing to its limitations of
space, it must present characters artificially
isolated from family, from past history, from
the distant future. Of course there is no
such thing as plot in real life. Most experi-
ence does not fall naturally into plot arrange-
ment. So it is the story-teller’s business to
make up in his art for the deficiencies in life.
For the author’s aim is to fashion his material
in such a way as to reveal his mealring, and
; no art can be made without arrangement
of material with reference to purpose.
Evelyn does not consider the love-element
essential to the success of the short-story.
Brander Matthews said : “If we were to
pick out the ten best short-stories, we
should find that fewer than half of them
made any mention of the love of man for
woman (the chief topic of the novel). The
short story, being brief, does not need the
love-element to hold its parts together.”
And how does this Ohio Wesleyan/ In-
structor bring up her little brood of budding
short-story authors ? She kindly provides a
course of suggestions for assignments of
stories and constructive exercises in the art,
from which—for the benefit of Professors of
English at Australasian universities —we
extract a few examples of extemporaneous
class-work.
(1) Write a beginning, introducing a character
in the midst of a story, and rapidly suggesting the
preconditions.
(2) End a story which is read aloud up to the
denouement (e.g., Maupassant’s “ The Coward ”).
Try to follow out the plot probabilities, and get
the inevitable ending if there is one.
(3) Frame an appropriate title for a short and
simple story read aloud by the instructor.
(4) Write an impressionistic description of a
face, so as to suggest the main features of the
character or disposition.
(5) Make two characters from a story previously
written converse on an assigned topic of current
interest.
Evelyn also provides a reading list of good
examples of short stories, classified under
Motive, Plot, Unity of Tone, Character,
Dialogue, Setting, Fantasy, Love Stories,
Pathos and Tragedy, Humor, and The
Author’s Attitude. In these lists—probably
owing to American megalomania—Balzac
occupies equal space with Joseph Dodge
Daskam, Hardy with ‘‘o. Henry,” Maupas-
sant with Doyle, Poe with Mary Wilkins
Freeman. The Bible adds one to the list—
the sfiort-story of Ruth.
,
However, all this may seem to the be-
ginner hardly helpful. What he wants is a
short cut to the manufacture and the sale
of his commodity. And America, the em-
bodiment of business, comes to his aid with
How to Write a Short Stor]/, by Leslie W.
Quirk (Editor Publishing Co., New York
City). There is no sentiment about Leslie
W. Quirk. He is commendably frank, even
distressingly business-like. “ I take for my
theme,” he says, “the writing of a short-
story that will sell.” He is interested solely
in “the saleable short-story,” which he sub-
sequently defines as ‘ 1 nothing more or less
than a good plot draped enough to soften the
naked crudities without hiding the general
beauty of form.”
Nowadays a good short-story is a cash asset.
The demand is steady, the market unlimited, and
the prices good. ... Says Frank A. Munsey :
“The great field to-day is for writers of fiction.
There is not half enough to go round. Publishers
all over the world are reaching out for both short
and long stories. Good ones are extremely diffi-
cult to find, but the supply does not begin to equal
the demand.”
So now to work, and hustle !
If you want to write a short story, and doubt if
you have anything worth the telling, go to bed
early some night, get up with the sun the next
morning, and take a long walk. Now, with the
smell of nature in your nostrils, let your imagina-
tion run “as wild as a spook on a, spree.” Sup-
pose- that oloud up there were an air-ship, with a
kidnapper aboard, and suppose the boy who had
been stolen were the King of Spain. Can’t you
work out the details of what might happen ? Or
suppose that girl over there should come to you,
silently and mysteriously, and place a roll of
greenbacks in your hand,-with the words, “To
pay for your burial.” How would it end? All
this smacks of the sensational, I grant, but 1 am
going at this theme in a practical, manner. I
believe your first story will be sold because of its
plot. Nine first stories out of ten are.... Write love
stories. “ All the world loves a lover,” and editors
are human. But make your love story one of
action. Make it dramatic; melodramatic, if you
will. But wake up your reader; startle the
editor, and make him read it in spite of himself.
Fully three-fourths of all the stories submitted
nowadays are rejected because of weak or trite
plot-interest., . . Outline, in your mind, some
situation, improbable, inconsistent, inexplicable.
Then fit in the characters and incidents necessary
to make it altogether probable, consistent, ex-
plainable. ~. Have a page from a diary, telling of
a man’s undying passion for a woman, flutter to
her feet in some deserted place, preferably from a
balloon, an air-ship, an exploding shell far over-
head, or what you will. There’s material for an
unique love story.
Easy, isn’t it ?
However, Quirk has no illusions.
Even when you have supplied your plot in this
fashion, and written your story, it will not be a
literary production. It will be just the “stuff”
of a hack-writer, just a “ pot-boiler but pot-
boilers sell. . •
Every student of literature should paste
that pregnant apothegm over his desk. Pot -
boilers sell, and Quirk can tell you how
to manufacture thein. For instance, so
much depends upon the introductory sen-
tences, from the view-point of the editor,
that fully three-fourths of the stories sub-
mitted are never read beyond the first page.
The (American) editor’s method is simple :
He scans the introductory paragraph, and if
he finds that the writer plunges into his story at
the very outset he is interested enough to skim
over the entire first page. If nothing is found
that condemns the story, the editor now turns
to the last page, aiid studies the conclusion. If
he finds that weak, the story is returned without
further reading; but if it ends with a quick,
sharp turn, or in a manner that suggests rapid
dramatic action somewhat earlier ini the story, he
dips into the middle and glances over the other
pages; not systematically, but in a hop-skip-and-
jurup manner. Then, if the first promise is ful-
filled, he leans back and reads the story clean
through.
Quirk is fertile in original beginnings of
short stories :
Why not have the girl watching her faithless
lover, ten miles away, through a telescope ? Why
not have the heroine dressed in brightest crimson
and living in a convent ? Why not have the girl
wondering if the man she just pushed into the
cistern is dead ?
Well, why not ?
And suppose you can’t invent plots ?
There’s the convenient plot-broker advertis-
ing in The Editor : “ I buy, sell and exchange
story-plots.”
At present (he remarks) I have on file over 100
short-story plots of different classes; probably
there is one in the lot that is just what you want.
I guarantee their originality. Sit down now and,
write to rrfe ; tell me just what kind of a plot or
story-idea you want. I’ll answer by return mail
and give you ideas of what I have in the line that
interests yon. If you like the sketch of the plot,
buy ifc; if you don’t like it. I’ll take your wore
for it instead of your money. I throw in an
attractive title with every plot I sell.
And this plot-broker is dirt cheap. His
prices (with titles thrown in) range from one
to five dollars. What author would allow
this trifling expenditure to bar his road to
fame ?
But, having written your short story, you
have to dispose of it. Quirk ranks ‘ ‘ the
ability to sell a story nearly as high as the
ability to write one,” and he advises you to
know the market through and through.
You must study the magazines, and carefully
consider the particular magazine that your
story would suit. But, despite its curt
business methods, this little book will repay
study by the beginner. And The Editor,
a New Work monthly magazine, contains
more of these business f‘ tips.”
For a post-graduate course in our Authors’
University we may take as text-book the
rather technical treatise upon Authors and
Publishers: A Manual of Suggestions for
Beginners in Literature, by G-.H.P., and
J.B.P. (Putnams, per Lockley Library).
The initialled authors are obviously members
of the publishing firm; and the book, re-
vised and enlarged, is now in its seventh
edition. Putnams’ set forth the case of
the publisher with fairness. The subjects
treated include the various arrangements for
publication, the value of the literary agent,
and the questions of advertising and copy-
right. For the consolation of the rejected
author, Putnams’ point out that one-half of
the books published each year in the United
States fail to return their cost, and that one-
half of the remainder bring no profit, leav-
ing the cost of supporting the publishing
machinery of the country to be borne
by the publisher’s share of the profits
of one-fourth of the books issued.
Putnams’ disapprove of the literary agent,
on the ground that his chief aim—with
the laudable view of securing to himself
better commissions—consists in getting his
client to change his publishers with every
successive book. Thus the cumulative
momentum of publishing effort resultant
from the books being published in a uniform
binding and style and under one publisher’s
imprint is lost. Books thus scattered among
various publishers do not get the advertise-
ment that results from the cumulative
advertisement of one publisher. Each
book is “ pushed as a separate effort,
whereas the sole publisher of an author’s
work has an interest in emphasising the im-
portance of the author apart from his books.
Such books then have a chance of becoming
44 works,” and that is the first stage toward
1 4 standard literature ” and fame. Putnams’
also point out that no literary agent has
ever been tempted to start. as a publisher,
the inference being that there is a surer
profit secured from authors’ commissions
than from, the more speculative gains—and
greater risks—of the publishing business.
For “ the history of publishing is a record of
erroneous judgments, and publishing falli-
bility is an unavoidable factor in the conduct
of publishing undertakings.”
It seems an unaccountable omission on the
part of the American universities that they
offer no courses for the student who wishes
to become a publisher. Apparently pub-
lishers just happen.
For the Australian kiddie, Tarella Quin and Ida
S. Renton] have thoughtfully provided the real
thing. Gum Tree Brownie , and other Faerie Folk
of the Never-Never (G. Robertson, Australia) is a
delightful book of child stories, with the true
Australian flavor —for surely the continent is big
enough to provide its own fairies? —illustrated, in a
way that will bring joy to any child, by Ida
S. RentOul. There is at present no Customs
duty upon fairies; but, when so happily pre-
sented by Tarella Quin, the Australian kiddie
should need little persuasion to support the
home-grown product. Another delightfully illus-
trated book from the same publishers is .Aus-
tralian Songs for Young and Old, words by Annie
R. Rentoul, music by Georgette Peterson, and
illustrations by Ida S. Rentoul. The scope of the
subjects may be from the fact that two
of the ditties are entitled “Gobble Wobbles,”
and “ A Little Aborigine. ”■
Concluding an article upon G. B. Pshaw, in
which he quotes some remarkable eulogies of the
dramatist by the best critics of Europe, Archibald
Henderson, in the Atlantic Monthly , remarks : Of
one thing at least there can be no question : that
. Bernard Shaw is the most versatile and cosmo-
politan genius in the drama of ideas'that
Great Britain has yet produced. No juster
or more significant characterisation of this
man can be made than that he is a
penetrating and astute critic of contemporary
civilisation. He is typical of this disquietening
century—with its intellectual brilliancy, it 3 ironio
nonsense, its flippant humor, its devouring scepti-
cism, its profound social and religious unrest.
The relentless thinking, the large perception of
the comic, which stamp this man, are interpene-
trated with “ the ironic consciousness of the
twentieth century.”

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Memories.
My memories abide with me
As dreams from which I, now awake,
Within my languid reverie
The sweetest parts alone need take.
Round broken hopes I do not build
Regrets to make a double loss ;
The best of what remains I gild
Uncumbered by the waste years’ dross.
The ashes of the past to me
Are ashes to be thrown away;
But first are sifted carefully
To yield such profit as they may;
And in the volume of my years
Each leaf doth hold its scented flowers
Fragrant of joy, but dried of tears :
The essence of ecstatic hours.
Within the stress of present days,
Where gain and loss mix tangledly,
A sweetness ever with me stays :
My memories abide with me.
N.S. W.
HAMER.
Harbor Fantasy.
Harbor lights, reflecting, gleaming,
Blue and yellow, scarlet, green,
Boats at anchor, gliding, dreaming,
Glimpse of a Venetian scene;
Music fills the ferry steamers
With a glamor magical,
Only city-thralled blasphemers
Frown upon love’s carnival.
Comes a peal of elfin laughter—
Ah, ’twas love’s sweet eyes that shone!
So may passion riot—after
Come what will—oblivion !
Summer is the wooing season,
Lips unkissed soon wither, cold;
Who’d be guilty of the treason,
Save the miser counting gold ?
Hearts inspire with vague suggestion—
Oh, most exquisite delight!
Is vainglory worth a question
Drifting through Arcadian night
Soft as dreams of love ?—to-morrow
Who cares what regrets may come ?
Passion lives to-night—and sorrow
Dare not come till love is dumb.
Look, the bright aurora lifting
O’er the city minarets !. . .
Loose the tiller, dreaming, drifting,
Soon the heart all sin forgets !
Southern sons and southern daughters
Gliding down love’s firmament,
Drifting down the star-shot waters,
Tranced with fabulous content.
Harbor-lights, reflecting, gleaming,
Green and scarlet, yellow, blue. . . .
What wild thoughts be yours, love, dreaming
The enchanted evening thro’ ?
Sweet emotions, breathless sighing,
Ebb and flow like summer tide;
Look, the silver banners flying
Where the moon unveils her pride !
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.
The Song of the Sea.
Once I dreamed I had captured the croon of the
sea,
And had woven it into a song;
The rhythmical, mystical croon of the sea,
O’er deep, booming billows borne ever to me.
By snowy-shod wavelets that splashed it in glee
On the sands as I sauntered along—
That rhythmical, mystical croon of the sea
With its baffling, elusive, sweet song.
And I thought it would lull little children to
sleep,
And would ease every sufferer’s pain ;
The lullaby, rockabye hushing to sleep,
That, drawn from the heart of the fathomless
deep,
Held magic to comfort all mourners who weep
In its rhythmical, soothing refrain—
’Twould lull all the little ones softly to sleep
And would ease every sufferer’s pain.
Then I thought of the wavelets iEolus had wooed
And had won for the sport of the day ;
Had kissed and caressed and with soft nothings
wooed
Then taunted and flouted with epithets rude,
Until, with the fury of demons endued,
They had leapt to the sky for their prey,
Those gay little wavelets yEolus had wooed,
And had then flung in scorning away.
Still my song had no name, though the cry of the
child,
And the moan of the sufferer in pain,
The sob of the mourner still unreconciled,
The shriek of the tempest when passions were wild
When too-trustful hearts are by fancy beguiled,
Had found echo within its refrain—
My song had no name, although mourner and child
Had been comforted by its refrain.
Then I thought how the slow-swelling, incoming
tide
Rose responsive toDian above ;
The slow-moving, far-reaching, incoming tide,
The lover obeying the call of his bride,
Who silently leads him o’er ocean tracts wide,
And the name that I gave it was T.ovq
F ° r 1 tide ght h ° W the slow ' swellin »* incoming
Rose responsive to Dian above.
N.S.W.
MAY KENDALL.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only)
Per Annum ... ... ... £1 6 0
Half-yearly ... ... ... 0 13 6
Quarterly ... ... ... 0 7 0
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have Thu Bulletin posted
regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Hoiv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (ivith regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there ; the official
always is.
Special Entice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing ivith Australian subjects, and not exceeding
tioc columns in length, or, say, 8000 ivords, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it ivill be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

MELBOURNE AND SYDNEY.
There is, to the social student, something more
to be gleaned than satisfaction with a pretty bit
of Federal feeling from the recent opening in
Melbourne of a show of pictures by Sydney artists.
Certainly it is noteworthy that the artists of
Sydney (alike with most citizens of that city with
any commonsense) recognise in the dismal shrieks
of Mr. Wade and Mr. Carruthers as to “ Mel-
bourne jealousy” and “Victorian conspiracies”
only electioneering falsehoods of a particularly
unpleasant and unpatriotic type; that the public
of Melbourne gave the artist the proper reward
for their good sense by attending their
show freely, buying freely, and never once
hinting that the product of a Sydney brush
must necessarily be worthless; and that the
Prime Minister of Australia, head of the gang of
“Victorian conspirators,” about whom Mr.
Wade and Mr. Carruthers moan, snatched an
hour from the crowding cares of his life to officially
open the show. These facts have their import-
ance. Less obvious, at a first glance, "but of equal
value, are some other suggestions which the inci-
dent prompts. The Sydney artists primarily
went down to Melbourne so that they might sell
their pictures. Experience teaches that Mel-
bourne buys pictures whilst Sydney does not.
Sales arising from a Sydney exhibition of
this particular collection were disappoint-
ing. So an appeal was made to Mel-
bourne purses, and with a good deal of success.
Streeton, a Victorian artist (who to be “in the
picture ” as it is presented by Mr. Wade should
find a reason for this in the allegation that Sydney
is a barbarous and uncultivated city, far inferior
to Melbourne in its perception of the arts), has an
explanation which is very flattering to Sydney’s
pride. The citizens of the harbor city, he main-
tains, have such a surfeit of natural beauty that
they do not need pictures. Almost every street-
end shows a glowing bit of sea and wooded hill.
Which is very pretty but not quite adequate. Not
only in this matter of picture-buying, but in a score
of other points of social life, Melbourne and Sydney
show a strong divergence in development; and the
line of cleavage widens with each year, so that un-
doubtedly the future of the Commonwealth will
be a good deal affected by the radical points of
variance in the community character of its two
great cities.
* • » *
Had Sydney been built on Botany Bay, as was
originally planned, its development would pro-
bably have been more on Melbourne lines, with
some modification caused by climate; for the
physical characteristics of its site profoundly
affect the character of a city. As it stands,
Sydney is on the shores of the most beautiful little
sea-lake in the world, surrounded on all sides
by picturesque hills, which defy the worst
malice of aldermen to make them altogether ugly.
Melbourne is on a flat plain, stretching back from
a dull and dreary bay, and has no natural beauties
at all. The one site invited man to loaf at ease and
enjoy what Nature had provided ; the other sug-
gested the need of strenuous hustle if a worthy
home were to be created. For the same reason
that the temperate zone gives the best stimulus
to civilisation in that it promises a fairly certain
reward for energetic and thoughtful labor, but,
unlike the Tropics, promises nothing to idleness,
and, unlike the Arctic regions, does not depress
effort by impressing a sense of its futility, the site of
Melbourne gave to its population a constant tonic,
the site of Sydney to its citizens a drowsy sedative.
If it had not been that the early goldfields found
their natural port at Melbourne, of course there
would never have been a great city there. But,
after those goldfields had faded away, Melbourne,
too, would have sunk back, if it had not been that
its citizens had been taught energy by their cir-
cumstances, and set to work to make their city the
great manufacturing centre of Australia. Mel-
bourne had very little natural fitness for manu-
facturing, but the strenuousness of its citizens
compensated for that. They had already got into
the habit of thinking that all things are pos-
sible to human effort. Making a noble city
arise from a dull, flat plain had taught
them their capacity. To a large extent, the Pro-
tective tariff of Victoria was due to the frame of
mind which was created by the struggle to wrest
civic beauty out'of ugliness.
» * * *
The growth of time has accentuated the points
of difference between Sydney and Melbourne. It
shows in a score of ways. Business and profes-
sional life is less strenuous in Sydney than in Mel-
bourne. The climate and the situation suggest
ease to the Sydney man. Civic government is
more effectively organised in Melbourne than in
Sydney ; Melbourne can’t afford to be unkempt or
untidy; her good looks are dependent on con-
stant effort; whilst it is impossible to make
Sydney look really ugly. The family life is more
sedulously cultivated in Melbourne, and social
life is organised on a different basis. Practically
all the entertainments of Melbourne must be in
the homes, the restaurants, the theatres : in Syd-
ney, the harbor picnic is the favored social rally-
ing point. So there is more ostentation, more
ceremony, in Melbourne : more gaiety and happy
irresponsibility in Sydney. Any Melbourne
house of pretensions has a “music-room,” where
the girls of the family may make at least well-
intentioned efforts to amuse guests. In Sydney
the music room is rare, except in large houses,
but not the smallest home is without some
rough gear for picnicking. The Melbourne
citizen is more orderly than the Sydney, and
there is a good deal more of the tendency to
snobbery in his life. The Melbourne man’s re-
spect for all conventions is greater: the black silk
hat, which is the badge of respectability in Britain,
still reigns in Melbourne : in Sydney it is rarely
seen. There is more reading done in Melbourne
than in Sydney, more deliberate effort at mental
culture. Since the weather is so much greyer
in Melbourne, the citizens are forced back
more on their homes, which they try to
brighten with music, with pictures, and
with the observance of a somewhat elaborate
social ritual. The respect for and interest in
Eersonages is much stronger in Melbourne than in
ydney. Any Melbourne citizen can point out
on “the Block” all the big doctors, lawyers,
civil servants, merchants —their wives and their
children. The Sydney citizen knows his George
Reid at sight, and then his interest in public per-
sonalities practically ceases. These are brief
hints at comparison which will suggest rather
than indicate the vital differences between the
two great cities.
* * * *
To generalise, it might be said that the soul of
Sydney was critical, imaginative, careless; of Mel-
bourne, constructive, practical, ostentatious. In
the Australia of the future, one may imagine the
great ideas coming more often from Sydney, the
great administrators from Melbourne. Civic
patriotism will be stronger in the Southern city,
but the divine love of Freedom will flourish more
by the blue waters of Sydney harbor. Melbourne
will be always in danger of her ostentation trend-
ing towards vulgarity, Sydney of her carelessness
lapsing into shabbiness. Melbourne will be
proud of a studied, conscious grace; Sydney
will be adorable in her negligent semi-tropical
beauty. So far no other centres of the Common-
wealth have really reached the dignity of cities.
Adelaide has preserved something of the quiet,
cultured Fabian spirit of the early Wakefield
colonist, but with a larger growth will probably
approximate to the Melbourne type. Hobart, in
some respects the most distinctive of the Aus-
tralian capitals, is not Australian, but English, in
type as yet; when it feels the impulse of pros-
perity, and has a big native-born population, it
may become the chief nursery of Australia’s great
men. Brisbane will, as it grows, follow in the
footsteps of Sydney. Between the Sydney spirit
or the Melbourne spirit, Australia will divide
itself, and the fusion of the two impulses, the
compromise of the two spirits, will be Australia —
and a very sensible, pleasant, well-balanced Aus-
tralia.
THE VOICE OF A MANUFACTURER.
The Bulletin receives this letter from a man in
a small way of business :—
In a recent issue you had an article on the sweat
ing of girls in drapery establishments. These girls get from
5s. a week up (or down). And as that sum is insufficient
to support them, they are necessarily supported by rela.
tives or by someone. You blame the employer for not
paying more, and accuse him of being subsidised to the ex.
tent of the balance of the girl’s keep. But the employer
does not benefit by it. His competitor pays the same
wage, and his selling prices are ruled by the cost of
goods plus expenses and wages. If all were compelled
to pay more, either the public would have to pay for it, or
the rents would be less. I am in a small manufacturing
business. Wages of girls employed are low. If the
minimum was fixed at a fairly high rate by Act
of Parliament, so that both my competitors and myself
had to pay more, I should not mind in the slightest.
Eventually, someone else would pay the increased cost.
If, on the other hand, wages fell, so that girls were pro-
curable for nothing, the actual employer would not
benefit for long. I should be pleased to see wages raised,
provided my competitors paid the increased rate as well
as myself. But I cannot afford to pay more chan others
n the same business.
And to this The Bulletin, with some slight
reservations, says “Certainly.” The only differ-
ence between it and the manufacturer who writes
the above letter is that it believes a remedy can
be found, and easily found; while the maufac-
turer seems to regard the situation with the
hopeless eye of a well-meaning man who feels
that the fates are too much for him.
* * » *
Putting the matter into the smallest possible
space, there seem to be the following reasons for
the abjectly miserable pay of the drapery girl
and the drapery man, and many other girls and
men : —
(1) The landlord takes as much as he can get.
Right here, The Bulletin wishes to say that the
landlord (whether he represents a man to whom
the manufacturer or shopkeeper has to pay rent,
or whether he is the manufacturer or shopkeeper
himself, and the rent is simply interest paid by
himself to himself on money invested in his own
building) should, in its opinion, get 10 or 12 per
cent, on the cost of his bricks and mortar.
He should do this, because he has to get a
fair interest for his money, and he has to
allow for the fact that the capital he in-
vested in the bricks and mortar will vanish
when the building wears out (which may be in a
century or more), or when it becomes obsolete,
and consequently unlettable or unfit for use
(which may easily happen in 20 years). But a
huge proportion of rent doesn’t represent the
value of the bricks and mortar, but the value of
the ground. And the ground shouldn’t have any
value more than it had when the nigger and the
kangaroo occupied it, until a living wage is paid
to the people occupied on it. And a living wage
shouldn’t mean the price of bare, savage, miserable
existence, but the price of civilised existence.
For a girl it should at least mean the price of
decent clothes, with nice hats and boots and
gloves in moderation, with good food, a bedroom
that is airy, comfortable and reasonably pretty,
some literature, some amusements, and a margin
over to save against emergencies.
* * * *
To resume the small print:—
(2) The trader takes as much as he can get in the way
of profit.
Sometimes he doesn’t get much, and sometimes
he gets a Sheol of a lot: —
(3) As much as possible of the balance goes in the ex-
pense of cut-throat competition, because there are two
or six or ten times as many drapers, etc., as are really
required.
The trouble arises out of the fact that, instead of
the draper being made for the people, the people
seem to be made for the draper. There ought to
be just the smallest number of drapers and other
dealers who will suffice to meet the people’s ne-
cessities. Instead, there is the largest number of
drapers and other traders that can possibly live
on the game. The legend of the million or more
of angels that could dance on the point of a needle
was nothing to the fact of the number of drapers
and other dealers who try to dance on the point
of a mere fraction of legitimate trade.
(4) The employee, girl or man, gets what is left.
And the only thing that destroys the beautiful
symmetry of the arithmetical position is that the
girl will die if she is not fed, and will be arrested
if she is naked and has no place to sleep. The
girl, with her desire for food, and her need for
clothes and a bed, or at least a floor, is an
irritating and upsetting factor in an otherwise
simple problem. She has to be paid a sum which,
with wliat she can get from her relatives, or what
she can steal, or what she can owe, or what she
can obtain from strangers by immorality or other-
wise, will keep her alive and in working condi-
tion, and enable her to present a creditable
appearance behind the counter, and at least a
clothed aspect in the workroom. She is a factor
which can’t be put down on a slate, and treated
as a sum, and reduced to a decimal. She is
reduced pretty nearly to starvation, but she can’t
be quite reduced to a decimal. If she is reduced
too much she may wander right off the slate and
into the cemetery, and then all the rest of the
calculation will go to blazes. But she comes
last—always last; and she gets just as little of the
leavings as possible.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s idea of a remedy is to put this
unconsidered human item first— to declare by law
that she (or he, but the position of the she is the
worse and the more pitiful) must be paid a wage
which will not only keep her alive, but keep her
in comfort as a civilised being. Then the com-
munity may sit down for awhile and see what
happens before it interferes again. By doing so
it will gain experience and find out in what
direction it is best to interfere. Something, of
course, must happen —either a reduction in the em-
ployer’s profits (some of him can stand a reduction
and some can’t), or a reduction in the number of
employers and a saving in the waste that arises
through needless competition, or a reduction in
the absurdly inflated ground values and ground
rents, or an increase in prices, or a little of all
four. If the increase in prices is great, then
it will be up to the State to take a hand,
by fixing fair ground rents, as the British
Parliament has done to some extent in Ireland ;
and perhaps by licensing all traders, and
issuing only so many licenses in each line for so
many thousand inhabitants, so as to reduce the
waste through excessive competition; and pos-
sibly by making the processes of distribution a
State affair on Socialistic principles. As the
manufacturer, and even the middleman, are alive
and work for their money, while the ground is
dead and doesn’t work for its money, it seems a
fair thing that the ground should stand a good
part of the loss. But whoever stands it, the fair
wage should be enforced at any cost. And the
high Protectionist tariff goes with it, as a matter
of course, else through foreign competition the
fair wage may mean loss of employment and no
wage at all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The High Commissionership.
A rumor takes wing that it is proposed to ap-
point as High Commissioner a gentleman who,
though having many admirable qualifications,
is not an Australian citizen. The Bulletin
hopes that there is not a vestige of truth
in the report. Australian posts should in all
cases be for Australians, provided that there
is no sacrifice of efficiency through giving
them preference. In the case of the High Com-
missioner’s office it might be maintained that an
Australian should be appointed even if a superior
non-Australian were offering; for the moral
effect of having our one ambassadorial post filled
by a “ foreigner ” (and in this relation even a
Briton must be accounted a foreigner) would
be equivalent to an advertisement to the
world at large that there was not in all
the ranks of Australia’s citizenry one man
capable of representing his Commonwealth
abroad. In the scale against that, what can
be counted ? The consideration that by stepping
outside the ranks of Australia’s sons a rich man
could be got who would ‘ ‘ entertain magnificently!”
No more contemptible or sordid reason could be
ad vanced in a national matter. Australia does not
want to win victories in London at the
banqueting but at the council table: it seeks influ-
ence, not in the drawing-rooms, but in the legis-
lative halls. There is no reason at all why the
Australian High Commissioner should not live a
life of Republican simplicity in London and be
the njore respected for the fact that he did
not enter into competition with the Park Lane
Jews in barbarically-splendid entertainments.
Unless some better reason than that of wealth
can be advanced against an Australian citizen
receiving the post, the suggestion to appoint a
foreigner had better be dropped as expeditiously
as possible.
A Sinful Waste of Time.
The N.S.W. Parliamentary session is as yet very
young, but already the order paper is loaded with
various motions to discuss grievances or to appoint
Select Committees to inquire into claims. Con-
sider these few notices culled at random :
Mr. Broughton to move, —That a Select Committee be
appointed to inquire into and report upon the suspension
and claim of Robert Roberts, late bookbinder in the
Registrar-General’s Department ; that the Progress
Report from the Select Committee of Session 1901,
the Report from the Select Committee of Session
1902, and the Report from the Select Committee
of Session 1905, together with the Proceedings of the
Committees, Minutes of Evidence, and Appendices, be
referred to such Committee.
Mr. E. M. Clark to move,- I That a Select Committee
be appointed to inquire into and report upon the claim of
Richard Augustus Willoughby Green, in respect to
certain lands, known as Brumby’s Grant, Gore Estate,
Willoughby.
Mr. E. M. Clark to move,—That a Select Committee
be appointed to inquire into and report upon the claims
of Norman Selfe in connection with designs supplied by
him to the Government for the North Shore Bridge.
Mr. O’Sullivan to move, —That a Select Committee be
appointed to inquire into and report upon the com-
plaints of unfair treatment made by Madame Bell against
the Public Instruction Department.
Mr. McNeil to move, —-That a Select Committee be
appointed to inquire into and report upon the claim of
ex-Senior-Sergeant Vaughan for reinstatement in the
Police Force of this State ; that the Report from the
Select Committee of Session 1896. together with
Proceedings of tii ■ Committee, Minutes of Evidence, and
Appendices be refeii'ea to such Committee.
In some of the cases, that of Robert Roberts, for
instance, there have been already reports from
Select Committees practically stating that the
claims are just. But that does not get them any
“forrarder,” it seems. (The Bulletin knows of
one case where a man was ruined by the
blundering of the Lands Department, and
is now a penniless old age pensioner,
The Parting of the Ways.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Preetrade system, as established in England, and
the British Empire, are, in the long run, two incompatible
things—as they were meant to be from the first. It is
for those who think they believe in both ideals, to decide
which they must forego.— London Times.
Englishmen must consider what their folly may cost
them. Going down, as they are, in the several markets
of the world, before the advances of the great Protec-
tionist nations, such as America and Germany, they
are asked whether they are prepared to throw away
Australia. — London Jsaily Telegraph.
Thou art come, O prideful Britain, to the summit
of thy glory—
See the new and stalwart nations marching bravely
on before;
Shall the strength that once was Britain’s only
live in misty story.
Are the pillars of thy splendor to collapse for ever
more ?
Great thou art —-as swollen Carthage, in her proud
and boastful hour,
When her vaunted ships and galleys rode in all
the Punic bays;
So thou ridest on the waters —but the portents
grimly lower—
Thou hast come, O tall Colossus, to the Parting of
the Ways !
Rich thou art in ships and shekels —thou hast
raked the earth for plunder—
Hast thou buried all thy wisdom in thy victims
scattered graves?
For the lands that seethe with paupers are the
lands that soon go under—
Doomed and damned are all the nations that
subsist on hapless slaves.
Thou hast reared a joss of shoddy, fed with sweat
and grim privations,
And thy starving people serve it all their bitter,
care-worn days;
And the power whereof thou braggest rests on
crumbling, cracked foundations —
Thou art come, O lofty Britain, to the Parting of
the Ways!
Are thy kinsmen less than shoddy ? —Wilt thou
hug thy joss and perish ?
Then our paths lie far asunder and thy pulse is
beating slow ;
For our destiny is calling and a standard brave we
cherish —
O Colossus, blindly groping, must the hard-won
Empire go ?
Wilt thou choose the path of folly—going down,
0 purblind Britain,
Going down before the rivals who have spurned
thy CoBDEN-craze ?
Choose thy kinsmen or thy fetich —see, the signs
are plainly written —
Thou art come at last, O Britain, to the Parting
of the Ways !
VIC.

of compensating him for his wrongs.) The Select
Committee, in fact, represents nothing but a waste
of time, since its report can be, and often is,
ignored altogether. Now the Minister in charge
of a department should have sufficient sense of
justice to propose to Parliament a remedy in all
cases of grievance. To have to appeal from him
to a Select Committee is practically equivalent to
a vote of censure. If motions for the appointment
of Select Committees were taken in that light,
they would not be so common. As a preliminary
reform, there is a great chance for a “ strong ”
Premier to come down and tell the House that he
is going to clean up all Select Committee matters
in arrears by paying compensation in cases where
it has been recommended, as in those of Roberts
and the victim of the Lands department bungling.

The Lean Days That are not Mentioned.
W. Evans rode Apologue in the Melbourne Cup
last week, and earned, probably, quite £2soin3min.
27|sec. A jockey’s pay is thus £4336 an hour,
and if he is a persevering jockey, he will have
£34,688 to draw each evening after his eight-hours
day. Even to those who don’t know what a
jockey looks like in ordinary life, the absurdity
of this sort of calculation need not be pointed out.
Yet, within three days of the Cup last week, all
the papers gravely printed figures of this class.
True, they were not about W. Evans in particu-
lar and the weedy race of riders in general;
they had a more serious mission than that.
There was trouble in the air at Newcastle, and
in order to show what a greedy lot the miners are,
and how utterly unworthy of public sympathy,
the associated proprietors issued a statement
showing the highest and the average earnings at
various mines during the past four months ; and
the Herald and the rest of them stood aghast to
find that mere miners earned as much as 225. a
day. But it was a mistake to print those maxi-
mum figures alongside the average figures. For
instance, at the JDuckenfield the average for
October was admitted to be 10s. lOd. per shift;
but the highest was 17s. sd. Now if one man
earned 17s. sd. and the average for two men
was 10s. 10d., then the other man could
only have earned 4s. 3d. per shift. Therefore,
when a list is published, which mentions the
highest and the average earnings and doesn’t
mention the lowest earnings, and doesn’t men-
tion either that the average is only for the
days actually worked, the public is entitled
to have its suspicions. Such a list is pro-
fessedly issued to guide the public, but its only
real object can be to trap the public into taking
sides against the men. It is the confidence trick
in high places. Nor does it end at this. The
figures are for four months. Now the past four
months have seen previously unheard-of activity
at Newcastle. Collieries have been working 10
days a fortnight that for many a month previously
did not average four days a fortnight. In the
three years 1897-8-9 the average annual value of
Newcastle’s inter-State and foreign coal exports
was £857,110; for this year, on the basis of figures
so far, the total will go beyond £1,850,000. The
coal-miner knows what sort of a calling he follows ;
he knows that it is spasmodic, that a turn of the
tide may bring him down to two or three days a
fortnight. It is only the prospect of the busy
time, when he can get full work at high pay, that
keeps him a coal-miner. But now the public is
asked to forget all about those awful periods of
waiting, all about the months when, day after
day, men turn up at the pit’s mouth only to be
told that there are no orders in, and no work to
do—the public is asked to forget all that, and to
glue its eyes on misleading figures which, anyhow,
apply only to the best-paid class of coal-mine
workmen (who even then have to buy explosives
out of their wages). The Newcastle miners
may be right or wrong; but nothing can
alter the fact that the owners’ plan of cam-
paign has begun with a paltry trick and a
despicable attempt to poison the public mind
against the other side ; and it is that sort of thing
which embitters the men and makes a settlement
difficult, and emphasises the need to maintain an
Arbitration Court that will sniff with a trained
nose at all such statements before it swallows them.
The Wheel and the Tariff.
The Ltne tariff proposes to hit the importation of
bicycles with a duty of £5 per machine. The
result will be to stop the importation of made-up
cycles, and Australian men will be engaged to
build them from the separate parts in this country.
The export of bicycle parts from England has
become a practical monopoly of a Trust. This
combine, exasperated by the loss of trade, has
announced that the selling price of their parts will
be increased by 16 per cent, to Australian makers.
The price is not being raised to the Australian
agents, but they have been instructed to increase
their prices to the Australian makers, under pain
of haying future supplies stopped. At least, that
is their yarn. But Whoever is responsible for the in-
creased price, the object of the move is plain
enough. The effect will be to force up the price
of the Australian-made or assembled machine,
if the makers persist in sticking to the British
parts. What has not occurred to the British
combine is that there is any possibility of parts
being made in Australia. The Victor Motor
Works, of Double Bay, Sydney, and the Mort’s
Dock foundry, at Woolwich, Parramatta River,
have both splendid plants, capable of turning out
parts equal to the best English. If the Australian
makers were to enter into an arrangement with
these firms and the Victorian makers, who also
have the machinery capable of cutting the parts,
they would be independent of the English
makers, as far as the parts of an ordinary
bicycle are concerned. But till Australia
gets an amended patents law they will still be at
the mercy of the exasperated British monopoly, as
far as “ free wheels ” are concerned. Free wheel
hubs are patented, and, under the ridiculous law
that obtains throughout the British Empire, the
owners of any patent can refuse to supply Aus-
tralia with its apparatus, and no maker in the
country dare infringe the rights. Germany,
France, United States, Russia and Italy all insist
that, to maintain a patent right, the article must
be manufactured within the county within a cer-
tain time (generally two years) of the registration
of the patent. If Australia had some similar pro-
vision, the owners of patents would be compelled
either to set up factories in Australia or delegate
their rights to some firm that is manufacturing in
Australia. Meanwhile, there is a fine opening for
a brainy young Australian who can devise a new
kind of free wheel.

The Wretched Logic of J. Cook.
Joseph Cook is about the last of his troglodyte
crowd who simply can’t see things fiscal as they
are. G. Reid has long since recognised that Aus-
tralia is set absolutely on Protection; B. Smith
emerged the other day from a maze of polysylla-
bles to admit the same thing, and others of the
party, though they vote blindly against the
tariff, make no attempt to deny that they are
voting against the policy the people want. Only
Cook clings, with oyster-like adhesiveness and
lack of reasoning capacity, to the idea that the
late Federal elections were all a horrible
mistake, and that the electors hurled tried
and convinced old Freetraders into the outer
darkness from a sheer, overmastering love for
Freetrade. Thus in the Federal House,
G. S. Cake (Macquarie) moved (6/10/’O7) for
a general duty of 40 per cent, on made-up
clothing. Whereat J. Cook rose as one stricken
to the heart, and said, brokenly: “ What! A
proposal of 40 per cent, from a representative of a
Freetrade constituency !” and then subsided in a
hopeless and utterly shattered condition. There-
after all through the debate he was obsessed by
the painful thought that this Cake, the “repre-
sentative of a Freetrade constituency,” had voted
Protection, and it pervaded all his remarks, and
made him miserable and puzzled and incoherent.
Of course, Cook’s trouble in this regard rests
with his premises. He finds it impossible to
realise that constituencies like the Macquarie
have ceased to hold with Freetrade any more, and
he reasons on the basis that they are still the
same constituencies which returned Reidite candi-
dates such as Pumpkin Smith. But the plain
fact is they aren’t. Australia has progressed
fiscally of late years (though J. Cook can’t grasp
it), and the movement has dragged whole elec-
torates along. Cark, during the recent Federal
elections, absolutely scouted Smith’s efforts to
raise the Anti-Sosh spook, and talked Protection
broadcast. And in the final result the electors
flung out the personally popular Smith, and, by
a considerable majority, elected Care, an un-
known man, whose sole recommendation to most
was the fact that he stood for the Nationalist cause.
But Cook doesn’t see this. It’s a curious circum-
stance. In the past, Mr. Cook has had a fine
facility for seeing things that made for his ad-
vancement ; which was the reason of his successive
appearances as Protectionist, Freetrader, Socialist,
Laborite, and finally, Anti-Socialist. Mr. Cook
should see a political oculist, or he will be losing
his seat one of these days ; and that would be the
only political development he couldn’t possibly
sympathise with.

The Choice of Being Shot or Starved.
After all that we have read about the fighting
spirit which impels the Briton of the bulldog
breed to go and enlist in the army, it is interest-
ing to read in the London Nation the opinion of
Henry W. Nevinson, who for fifteen years was
engaged in passing recruits into the ranks. Savs
he :
In that time I passed more men into the regular army
than the most persuasive recruiting sergeant from St.
George’s Barracks, and I never knew one who enlisted
unless he was out of work and likely to remain so. Every
man or boy who passed through my hands into the army
was driven by hunger. ... In a new volume, called
“John Bull’s Army From Within,” Robert Edmondson,
ex-sergeant in a famous cavalry regiment, with twelve
years’ service, maintains in his opening sentence that
quite 80 per cent, of our soldiers enlist through hunger.
“ It is the army or starvation with them." My experience
puts the percentage higher.
Then Nevinson dwells on the soldier’s life—the
shameful pipeclay court-martials, ‘ ‘ less likely to
do justice than a handful of chorus girls”; the
spying military police ; the public executions,
where soldiers are paraded to see a comrade
killed; the wretched food and uncertain pay;
and “the unnatural isolation that drives men*
into practices which infect at least one in ten with
abominable disease.” No wonder that one in ten
deserts, and that the “ suicides in the Army are
nearly four times the suicides in civil life.” Yet
the only alternative to an army recruited from
men who take the choice between being starved
or shot is compulsory training. “Most nations
use force, we use hunger,” Nevinson says. And
Australia will find, as soon as it begins to con-
sider defence seriously, that it is up against the
same alternative.
Dead Men’s Money.
There is one weak spot in the Bill which J. C. L.
Fitzpatrick has put before the IV. S. Wales
Assembly dealing with unclaimed bank and other
balances. He proposes that balances which have
not been operated upon for six years shall be
advertised ; and then, if they are not claimed
within two years, they are to be handed over to
the Treasurer “ for the use of the revenue.” Sub-
sequently, of course, if a claimant can “ make
good,” the Treasurer will hand the money
over. Here was a chance to give a Public
Trustee a start, and Fitzpatrick hasn’t
taken it. Obviously a Public Trustee is
the proper person to have such moneys in his
keeping. The Treasurer will act in that capacity,
no doubt, but the fact will not be made clear—not
one in a thousand knows, for instance, that there is
already a Testamentary and Trust Fund at the
Treasury. The same officer should take charge of
unclaimed moneys in the hands of trustee com-
panies. Those cos. now, under their special Acts,
are supposed to furnish statements “ of moneys
unclaimed for five years which have been in the
co.’s hands during the preceding six months”;
but only last year the Auditor-General reported
that the provision was in one case at least
entirely disregarded—apparently because there
wasn’t a Public Trustee to look at the
matter from the public’s point of view when
the Bill was going through, wherefore the provi-
sion wasn’t made effective. Still, as it stands,
Fitzpatrick’s Bill is good ; and a Bill to consti-
tute a Public Trustee, and to hand over to him
the control of these balances, can follow, if it
doesn’t come now. And with those unclaimed
balances should be handed over the control of
intestate estates and the estates of persons who
have been put under control. Granted that the
present administration of these last may be satis-
factory, yet obviously this management of estates
—a commercial business—is not proper work fora
Lunacy Department. And if there are people
who prefer that a Public Trustee should act as
their executor, then the State should supply the
trustee, and back him up with its guarantee. The
success of the Maoriland office fully establishes
the fact that the demand does exist; for last year
1091 such estates were in the charge of the Trust’s
office, which the Dominion set up years before
it dreamed of a course of Socialistic experiments.
“ Go, and Sin Some More.”
The Law had another long think over Justice
Hodges’ quaint decision in the Aldred divorce
case in Melbourne, recently. Mrs. Aldred, in
applying for a decree, stated that her husband,
after treating her cruelly, had deserted her in 1901,
lea ving her and her children penniless. She had
a hard struggle against poverty, but she had the
luck to meet a man who befriended her, and who,
some time later, asked her to “keep house” for
him. Aldred was apparently gone to the dogs
or the Devil (via the cemetery or otherwise); so,
partly out of gratitude, she consented. Last
year, Aldred still being a-missing, she asked the
Law for a divorce, so that she might marry her
friend, and quit living an “irregular” life.
Having considered the matter at great length,
Justice Hodges opined that this wasn’t a case
where he could exercise his discretion, and
refused the decree. Last week, C.-J. Madden
and Justices Hood and Cdssen spoke at
great length in admiration of brother Hqdges’
decision, and confirmed it unanimously. Doubt-
less they thought they were carrying out the
intentions of the Legislature, but if they will
ponder over one sentence of C.-J. Madden’s
judgment they may have their doubts about it.
oaid his Honor: “The central idea of the
Marriage Act was that a tie of great value should
be broken for great causes only ; the Act was an
instrument to restrain vice, anil to use the Act so
that it should countenance that evil would be to
misuse it.” The “tie of great value” had, of
course, been utterly smashed and broken in pieces
by a wastrel husband away back in 1901; but
that was nothing to them—“the Act was an
instrument to restrain vice,” so they refused the
decree, and practically told Mrs. Aldred to
return and “keep house” for some one for the
rest of her days. That they were thereby encour-
aging irregularity was a mere detail; they were
administering the Law, and the Law they would
unflinchingly administer—with Costs. Always
with Costs.
Hugh McKay and his Protection.
The Bulletin does not in the least degree regret
the fact that it was foremost in the agitation to
secure, for Hugh McKay, manufacturer of Sun-
shine Harvesters, and his fellow makers of agri-
cultural implements, higher Protection. It is
said now, on the word of Justice Higgins,
after the most painstaking and judicial inquiry,
that McKay’s wage scale is too low to be
a decent and humane one. If The Bulletin
had had a foreknowledge of that fact, it
would still have pleaded just as earnestly
for a satisfactory range of duties. The fact that
McKay, or any other manufacturer in the line,
doesn’t maintain a decent Australian rate of
wages in no way vitiates the demand that manu-
facturers should be allowed enough Protection to
enable them to pay decent wages and yet compete
successfully against a foreign importer. That
Protection granted, it is then the business of the
Government to see that its primary purpose is
carried out —viz., the employment, on fair con-
ditions, of Australian labor. This the Government
is already doing; the inquiry before Justice
Higgins was a step in that direction. So soon as
the Government is sure of the position, it should
push home every weapon that “ the new Protec-
tion ” affords to enforce a fair wage rate. High
tariff with low wages is not at all the Australian
ideal.
Cyril Blacket writes :
In your issue November 7 appears a paragraph which,
owing to editorial condensation, has become misleading.
The Forestry Commission is still collecting evidence, and,
as far as I know, has not sent in any report; but it has
been asked hy prominent members of the building
fraternity to recommend an export tax on locally-grown
timber, whilst admitting foreign-grown timber free, with
the avowed object of making timber in Sydney cheaper
and more plentiful. The condensed paragraph makes it
appear that the Forestry Commission has actually
reported as requested—which it is to be hoped common
sense will prevent.

Dear Bulletin, —As you have more than once
pointed out, the way that Newfoundland was
recently treated over the fisheries question by the
Imperial Government may be the fate of our
infant Commonwealth over a matter of infinitely
greater importance. Of course the only effective
remedy, or preventative, is to take adequate steps
to defend ourselves against aggression ; but in the
steps leading up to such a crisis a freer Constitu-
tion might prove of enormous service. To that end
I have for years advocated the limitation of the
Imperial veto. On three occasions the N.S.W.
Political Labor League affirmed the principle,
but not enough support was secured to include it
in the Federal Labor platform. I have again
moved, through my own branch League, that the
matter be placed on the business paper of the
next Labor Conference. I am aware that no
amendment of the Australian Constitution can
be consummated without an Imperial Act; and,
of course, what the Imperial authority alone can
grant it may at will take back, and the freest
Constitution conceivable would be of no use if
suspended in an emergency when most required
to operate. But such a crisis seldom arrives with-
out ample warning, and this provision would pro-
bably avert it altogether by changing our present
wooden bonds for bands capable of stretching to
political infinity without breaking.—Yours, etc.,
Arthur Kae.
From H. «T. Preston and Co., Australian rep-
resentatives of the Nestle’s condensed - milk
people :
Re remarks B. 31/10/’O7. There are at least 10 or 12
condensed milk factories in Australia. In no other coun-
try are there as many factories in proportion to the popu-
lation, and total trade to be secured. The proposed new
duties are not responsible for the development of this
industry, and while the former duty of Id. per lb.
was in force the local manufacturers undersold the im-
porters to the extent of that duty. If the proposed
duty is carried, what further inducement will there be
for additional investment in this industry if former
selling: rates for the locally-manufactured article are
maintained? If they are not maintained the increased
duty must be responsible for the advance. Why
should the opportunity for increased prices be given
if the trade was profitable under former conditions ?
The fact that our company is opening: up factories
in Australia is the strongest argument that we can
use in support of our contention that the trade can
be profitably carried on under former conditions. The
Bulletin refers to “ dumping” surplus goods. There is no
surplus of condensed milk, the world’s supplies
being short, and this perishable commodity has
to be so carefully handled at all times that
it does not lend itself to “dumping.” If the
Id. per lb. duty was sufficient to induce us to come
here, it must be sufficient for our competitors. The
world-wide reputation for the quality of our goods is
alone responsible for the volume of our business, and
never yet can the Nestle Co. be justly accused of any
attempt to obtain a monopoly unless this term can be
applied to a measure of success obtained by fair comneti-
tion in values given.
The last remark is unfortunate : “ The world-
wide reputation for the quality of our goods is
responsible for the volume of our business.” So
this big co. starts off with an admitted handi-
cap—it has had time and opportunity to
build up a name, and a name is worth a great deal
in trade competition. Nothing more than that
need be said to disprove the yarn that what is
enough duty for this big co. with the established
name and reputation is enough for everybody
else. But for a bit more proof, though no more
is needed, take last year’s Australian imports of
condensed (preserved) milk : 10,615,0611 b. That
was with the penny per lb. duty, which Nest re's
representatives say is sufficient to keep the Aus-
tralian market for Australian milk !

Queensland politics are leading up to a big fight
between the Assembly and the House of Fossils.
Matters in connection with the Postal Voting Act
have caused a deadlock, and both Houses refuse
to budge. Fossilism knows it is fighting in its
last ditch, while Kidston knows that, if the old
corrupt Postal Voting Act isn’t wiped out,
Democracy runs a fair chance of having its
throat cut at the next elections. Fat
has also refused to pass the Wages Board
Bill. The Assembly sent this measure up again
the moment it was hurled out, and now the
country awaits Kidston’s next move with interest.
Kldston has two alternatives: (1) he may flood
the Chamber of Ancient Horrors with his own
nominees, or (2) plump for a special session, and
an appeal to the country, with abolition of the
Nominee Chamber as the main Government plank.
If Kidston elects to flood the Upper Chamber
with his Councillors, he will need to use better
judgment than he has in the past. Several of his
nominees have completely turned on certain of his
measures— Hall, Johnson, Beirne and Murphy
being about the worst offenders in this regard.

“ J.R.” : The frantic terror of the Government of
India (and some other people) at a political agita-
tion among a handful of Bengali Babus makes
those who were in India fifty years ago laugh.
There was no question of incendiary speeches in
those days, or of pelting tramcars with brickbats.
The business in hand was the mutiny of a drilled,
disciplined, and fully-equipped army of 120,000
men, with rather more than a full pro-
portion of cavalry and artillery, all trained
to the highest pitch of perfection. On
the other side were some 20,000 European
soldiers, who were completely taken by surprise,
and in most cases isolated, because the telegraphs
were cut, and the roads so beset that, for weeks
and months, up-country stations could get no
news of what was happening elsewhere. At
Meerut, where I was, we had no news from the
direction of Calcutta from the middle of May to
late in August, ’57. We had two European
regiments and plenty of artillery ; but the Mogul
emperor, who was reigning at Delhi, 40 miles off,
with 100,000 fighting men at his back, let us severely
alone till we were ready to go and seek him.
John Lawrence had been recruiting Sikhs in
the Punjaub (they were burning to avenge
on the Hindustani Sepoys their defeat 10
years before); 25,000 mixed Europeans and
Sikhs took Delhi, and the back of the
Mutiny was broken by the 20,000 Europeans
in India before any of the reinforcements sent
from Europe had had time to come on the scene.
The mutineers had everything in their favor-
numbers, surprise, equipment, command of the
roads, and a great superiority in cavalry. If ever
a colored race could beat a white one, it ought to
have done it then, and it failed miserably. There’s
a moral to this plain statement of facts, which it
will be well for some people both in and out of
Australia to learn by heart.

The scarcity of Australi an candidates for ca d etships
in the British navy is harrowing the soul of the S.
M. Herald. The Admiralty is graciously pleased
to take eight every year, if they offer, but the
offerings have only been at the rate of five a year
for the last five years. And no wonder, consider-
ing the terms. Boys to be eligible must be
between 12 and 13 years old. A fee of £75 per
annum must be paid, and £5O pocket-money
yearly provided, and, in addition, an elaborate
outfit. This must be kept up for the whole four
years’ training, and there is no pay to draw till
the youngster is appointed to a ship. Even when
he is appointed to a ship (as midshipman, or
whatever may be the equivalent rating) he will
only get £32 yearly—not enough to pa.y for his
messing, to say nothing of clothes and other
necessaries. As a sub-lieutenant, when he is 20
or more, he will get £9l a year, and he may be
lieutenant, if he’s lucky, with £lB2 a year at 25.
His education will have cost at least £750, and if
he is unlucky he may very easily find himself dis-
missed his ship, which means ruin to him in his
profession. And his training is of no use in tho
merchant service.
Generally the people who barrack for the
wholesale admission to Australia of the colored
brother are the people who don’t know the
colored brother, and in these cases there is
nothing so wholesome as a little personal contact.
This is the “ testimony” of a convert, as given by
N.S.W. S’African Commercial Agent Valuer :
Before I went to South Africa I was fully convinced
that the one thing we wanted to give up was the White
Australia idea. I was of the opinion that it was good to
work our cotton and sugar plantations with Kanaka or
Chinese or Indian, or any other kind of cheap labor. But
five years’ residence in South Africa has convinced me
that the longer we stick to the White Australia policy the
better.

Anstey, Vic. Labor M.L.A., in the Labor
Call intern perately attacks P.M.G. Sam
Mauger for his advocacy of temperance. The
Bulletin does not know what Mauger has been
saying on the subject, but if Mauger exaggerated
the drink evil, there is no reason why any Labor
member should get his hair off because drink has
been attacked. The liquor trade is strong enough
to defend itself. But, in his mistaken zeal for
the liquor trade, Anstey sneers at Mauger’s
work a,s a Protectionist and organiser of the
Victorian Anti-Sweating League. No one has
worked harder to secure employment and
fair wages for Victorian workers than cold-tea
and teetotal Mauger. Anstey’s attack, therefore,
ill becomes a Labor representative. The Aus-
tralian Labor movement is not a narrow class war,
but the movement of the majority of the people
towards higher national life and ideals attainable
by votes and not by revolution. This Mr. Anstey
does not fully understand.

A Frenchman, one Rene Goddard, in an article
on “Japan ” in the Revue Politique et Parlia-
mentaire (10/5/’O7), shows more knowledge of the
real size of the Asiatic peril, as far as Australia
is concerned, than is customary amongst a big
section of people living right here. His main
point is the growing and appalling tendency of
the modern Jap to emigrate to foreign countries
that don’t want him. The U.S.A., which only
had 25,000 of him in 1900, became afflicted with
from 60,000 to 70,000 between the years 1901 and
1903 ; and there are now nearly 100,000 of the Nip-
pon gentry in the Western States of America
alone, despite all discouragements. Canada, after
remaining free for years, has now got far more than
it wants of the small yellow men, the inrush
having started with the beginning of the century.
Hawaii, which only had 24,000 Japs in 1896, has
trebled that total since 1900. Within 10 years
42,000 of the saffron nuisances have swarmed
into the already over-populated island of For-
mosa. There were only 15,000 in Korea before the
Russ-Jap war; now that unhappy country is
infested by 150,000 Japanese, and is misgoverned
lock, stock and barrel from Tokio. The con-
clusion the French writer draws is the obvious
one that Australia—“which of all countries on
earth is the most desirable from the yellow man’s
standpoint if it would avoid the fate of Korea,
will need to be ready with guns, bayonets and
suchlike implements to prod the intruding Jap
into the sea the moment lie arrives.

AT POVERTY POINT.
Melba, sniffing the fascinating dust of the concert
arena, has found it absolutely impossible to keep out of
the same any longer. She has decided to give five con-
certs before leaving - these shores (cwo in Melbourne, two
in Sydney, and one in Brisbane). It’s great news. There
are many Australians who had marked down “ Go to
hear Melba” as the first big item of a European tour ;
and now the mountain comes to them.
“ D.”: Re Melba’s lengthy stay in these parts, a recent
Bulletin par has probably hit on the right explanation.
Nothing is more likely than that the great soprano will
begin to train shortly for Wagner parts. Hitherto she
has sung in “light” opera, sustaining roles—Juliet,
Mimi, Marguerite—which demand much singing in the
high register. With increasing age the top notes of
even the most perfectly trained artists show a tendency
to fray-—not Marchesa herself can avert that. The time
threatens when people won’t crowd the early doors to
hear Melba in the aria from “ Romeo and Juliet ” as they
used. For the Wagner parts the lady will not need her
ultra high notes, but her middle ones must be strength-
ened and made fuller. To achieve that she must undergo
much bulloeking practice, and (because she’s Melba)
there is small doubt that she will. In fleeing to
Ercildoun and Wagner, Melba is, operatically speaking,
coming in out of the wet.
One has to go right ’Ome to get really reliable stuff
about Australian notabilities. Thus the Penny Magazine
(Lo nd.):—“ Naturally, Australia was very proud of Madame
Melba, and she is very much attached to her native coun-
try. Not long since, she returned there, and gave a series
of charity concerts. At one of these she realised £lO,OOO,
and her'father, Colonel Mitchell, was present. Madame
Melba bought three boxes herself at £5OO each, and one
of these she presented to her father, in order that he
should hear her sing.”
The Milo biograph, now showing at Sydney Tivoli,
reveals that master of advertisers, Alec Laing, at the door
of the London Pavilion, arrayed like Solomon in all his
glory. The films in themselves are a tribute to his genius.
Pansy is seen with ’em all on at a riverside open-air
restaurant, whence she motors to the Pavilion, where
Laing and the multitude are awaiting her. The next
scene is her dressing room, where floral tributes are
delivered, and an individual so eccentric-looking as to
stamp him as a journalist is introduced to Pansy. Then
the mere men are cleared out, and Pansy’s two maids
start to get in their fine work. Her hat is removed :
her multitudinous jewels are laid, one by one, on the
dressing table ; one maid goes behind Pansy, and is
seen unbuttoning her beauteous gown, the other maid
-comes to the front to remove it from the wearer’s fair
form. Then, just as the male portion of the audience is
holding its breath, the screen turns black, and from all
over the theatre rises an “ Aw-aw 1 ” of disappointment.
Pansy, as the Velasquez Venus, shows the lady without the
paint she wore when posing in the halls in this character.
H. Vickars Rees, who “ represents ” La Milo (Pansy
Montague), writes this paper that in eighteen months
that astute damsel and her ally Cruickshank have
“ worked themselves into the command of £l5O a week at
the Alhambra, London.” Usually a pill .takes 10 years
steady advertising before success ; but La Milo’s “ Watch
Committee ” scheme—whereby in each town dozens of
local elders were roped in to report on the “purity” of
her show—has proved better than any conception of any
quack pill. In the old country Watch Committees,
coupled with the intervention “on moral grounds ’’ of
Bedstead of The Review of Reviews, are now regarded
as irresistible advertising stunts.
The closing of Bland Holt’s campaign enables manager-
in-front Christie Simonsens to make another attempt at
escape from his theatrical environment. Chris Simon-
.sens has always had a yearning for a field of industry in
which he would not be required to stand around and deal
with “ deadheads ” every night from 7.30 to 11 p.m. He
looks at life through spectacles, and they are not rose-
colored as regards Thespian enterprise. Presumably he
will break out in another sort of billet shortly. In the
meantime he is enriched by a purse of sovereigns, pre-
sented to him by a number of strictly mercantile friends
who have known him as a business man during his long
stay with Bland Holt.
“ Stagecraft”: Re “Mariner ”on “Brewster’s Millions”
and “ Captain” Stirling Whyte’s alleged lack of nautical
knowledge. There is not, and never has been, a single
green light on the yacht visible to the public. The
correct side-light (red) is shown on the port side of the
ship facing the audience, also the usual night signal of
distress (two red lamps, one six feet above the other) is
hoisted nightly. Flags were used only on the first
night because “it was so done in America and Eng-
land,” but, when noticed, were dispensed with by the
management, who knew something of aquatics, and
preferred to do the right thing to following a wrong
precedent. With regard to the sail, “Mariner” maybe
informed that “ Captain ” Stirling Whyte prefers to keep
the yacht under what little sail she carries so as to
steady her in what is, after all, only a moderate blow.
