short, cornet soloist Moore having taken a job at Too-
woomba (Q.).
Brisbane had a fly in its ointment in connection with
Mrs. Clara Butt-Rumford’s visit there. The Big Lady
left her brood behind in Sydney, whereat the Queensland
capital was annoyed. It wanted to see Joy weeping when
Ma warbled, and Toy pulling Pa’s moustache with both

told in time.
Those who know the “ helter-skelter” at various resorts
might have thought it a simple thing. A “ squat and
slide ”is its popular name. But recently it had to be
described in affidavit language in the N.S.W. Equity
Court, and this is what it appears as :—“ The said helter-
skelter of the plaintiff co. is an amusement structure,
from which visitors descend by sliding down, usually on a
mat, by gravitation, an inclined curving trough, leading
from the top of the said structure partly round and
partly away from the same.” The owners claimed that
the “ Alpine slide ” of a rival showman was really the
same thing, viz., “an amusement structure,” etc. The
Court agreed that for the present a helter-skelter is an
Alpine slide, and an Alpine slide a helter-skelter, and
granted an interim injunction. But how it will turn out
in the long run Zeus alone knows.
“S.S.”: I thought when a certain type of vaudeville
song had done with human love and female lingerie, and
had degenerated to the amorous dallying of cats, the
canoodling of bees and flowers, and the amours of the barn-
yard, we must have reached bottom with that touching
ballad recounting the story of two devoted gold fish, as
sung in “ The Geisha.” I was wrong; many lower depths
have been plumbed since then. This song has descended
past the insects, and reached still-life subjects. Now, in
“The Girls of Gottenberg,” Lauri and Fan Dango are
warbling of the love of two sausages. Two sausages in
a butcher’s shop fall in love. There ara rivals and
jealousies, lovers’ quarrels, mutual recriminations and a
sad ending. The song may be comic—l am not sure ; but
in my opinion Lauri’s interpretation of a love-sick sausage
is the best bit of characterisation he has ever done.
“Wang”: Importers are not the only kind of private
enterprisers who scream for suppression. At the early
theatre door I met one of the brigade of small boys who
yelled something that sounded like “ Hereyarprogramme-
ancomicterreadferapenny ! ” I knew it concerned the
destination of the penny, and passing it over received a
programme and a comic-to-read. The comic-to-read is
wherein the swindle lurks. Having piloted Angelina
through the struggling mass to a seat, I turned to the
comic-to-read to fill in the half-hour before the curtain
rose. My language became worse than the villain’s, for
the comicterread contained the start and the middle of
the story, and broke off suddenly where the villain was
rushing with axes in his hands to chop the rope by which
the hero was struggling (with the heroine on his shoulders)
across the bottomless chasm. And I did want to know
how many thousand feet that hero and heroine fell with-
out being smashed. But the end of that yarn was exciting
someone else in some other part of the building, and he
was probably dying to know why the fool took the girl
over the chasm in that ridiculous way.
There are points about marrying into a royal family,
after all. Toselli, the pianist, who last month took on
the jumpy contract of spouse to the ex-Crown Princess
of Saxony, has been offered £40,000 by Caspar, the Copen-
hagen impresario, to give a concert tour of the world.
This is a scoop for Toselli, and on the sound general
principle that there are immense numbers of people pre-
pared to pay big money to hear a performer on the bones,
provided he has the reclame of a royal elopement
attaching to his name, it should be a scoop also for
Caspar.
Frohman, the big N’York and London impresario, has
struck what looks like a bright idea. He proposes that
the great Transatlantic liner companies fit up theatres on
their steamers, so that there may be representations on
the voyage. There is really no reason why not. Already
these huge ships have lifts, self-contained flats, bridal
suites, billiard tables, and a hundred other things that
seemed impossible a quarter of a century ago. A ship-
board theatrical show would pay the promoter most
voyages like a goldfield pub. It would relieve the sea’s
monotony muchly, and be regarded with disfavor by none
but the travelling confidence man who boards these
vessels in quest of gold, and the travelling confidence
maiden in search of marriage lines.
“ Picard” : There recently toured a slice of Maoriland
a show that met with a large order of misfortune. For
an overture to the tour the tent carried away, and the
pole nearly swept a Chow across into Gloryland,
and he remarked “Whaffor!” After things got going,
the strong man of the show challenged the world to do
all sorts of acts, and some of the world went inside, and
a hairy human, with red in his eye and crimson whiskers,
peeled off and threatened to do things in such a forcible
manner that the strongman shot him into the night, and
he landed hard, and then got back in search of Goliath’s
scalp, whereupon that gentleman pounded him hard and
heavy, and then something in a uniform showed up, and
the crowd howled, and demanded something more for its
honest bob. So the contortionist got himself into a knot,
and, being new to the business, couldn’t untie himself
again, after which the band (which consisted of one mem-
ber) got on to the line-up board and challenged the entire
globe to play a brass instrument for £lOO, and the police-
man said the band was drunk, and took it away with him.
The lean, lank, bony man that walked the wire now
walked up to the doorkeeper and demanded his salary in
advance, and w'as dismissed. He explained that he was
thirsty, so he was reinstated, and got on the wire to do
his turn, when the tent blew down again, just as the con-
tortionist got his bones arranged satisfactorily. Up the
tent was hitched once more, and the contortionist tried
some high kicking, and nearly kicked himself out of
joint, as he had replaced some of his anatomy in the
wrong spot, or something. Business was poor, and even-
tually a benefit was arranged, which was a success finan-
cially ; but, alas! the advance-agent got away with the
money bag, and advanced hurriedly at dead of night, and
fled over the horizon, with Goliath, the strong youth,
and the rest of the show in hot chase. But they have
given it best by now, and disbanded, and the orchestra has
arrived out of quod, and is cow-spanking for a cockey.
“ Brad ” (from a Victorian country town) : A travelling
show', including a Japanese juggler, visited our town last
night. When the time arrived for Nippon’s son to do his
turn, he was announced with great reverence by the
manager. (The white performers, by the way, simply
rushed in unannounced, stood on their heads, and spun
round on sundry portions of their anatomy, then scurried
off again almost unapplauded.) The Jap approached as
far as the ring, arrayed in a gorgeous mantle of figured
silk, and attended by two of the tent-hands, who removed
his silken raiment as gingerly as two acolytes might dis-
robe a Pope. I expected to see something thrilling in
the way of juggling, but was doomed to be disappointed,
for, after going through a lot of preliminary skite, Mr.
Jiu-Jitsu threw three balls into the air and managed to
catch tw T o of them again. The ground caught the third.
For this feat the crowd clapped excitedly. After this,
Mr. Nippon sauntered to the edge of the ring, and the
two attendants dashed frantically to meet him, covered
him with the multi-hued cloak, and led him away. He
filled in the remainder of the night peacock-strutting
round the ring and ogling the feminines of the audience,
who seemed to be greatly fascinated by his antics.
London Referee says that it is difficult to get
stage supers in Paris now ; all of them are away work-
ing for biograph firms. (France and the United States
are the great centres of faked picture-film produc-
tion.) The casual stage-hand, at a shilling a night, can
get six times that, with an engagement by the month.
So theatre managers have to engage real actors for their
stage-crowds, and the said crowds have improved mar-
vellously in their acting, but diminished painfully in their
numbers.
“ Melb.”: The Bijou diverged into high game with the
play of “ The Tyrant,” which is a version of Victor Hugo’s
“ Angelo.” Of course, it has to come somewhat from the
eminences down to Oollingwood Flat, but yet it is a
mighty advance on the common melodramatic trash. Do
we not often feel that author and actors are “ poking
borak ”atus ? There is an almost irresistible temptation
to guv, and turn it into burlesque, as Lemaitre did with
“Robert Macaire.” The only drama of Hugo’s familiar
on the English stage is “Ruy Bias,” in which
Fechter was so fetching. Then there is the operatic
version of that wondrous play, “ Hernani.” Five acts,
each a great play. The most daring and stirring of Hugo’s
pieces is “Lucretia Borgia,” which inspired Donizetti.
“ Le Roi S’Amuse ” is turned into “Rigoletto,” so that
Hugo appears twice on the Melbourne boards in a week.
“Marion Delorme” is one of his intensely powerful
works. His novel of “ Notre Dame ” makes a fine drama.
What a lava-flow of invention !
Weird in their diversity are the uniforms displayed
in “ A Life’s Revenge,” now being played in Sydney.
The action is laid in Russia, and the military
gentlemen belong to the army of the Great White
Czar. Yet one (a colonel) is habited in what is, if
anything, the garb of a private in the Prussian service ;
his orderly wears the sky-blue tunic of a French hussar
with a regulation kepi; the rank and file are dressed as
hotel porters, and Prince Boris (regiment or ship un-
specified) affects in one scene the scarlet frogged tail-coat
of a Bank of England beadle, and in the next an admiral’s
frock suiting with gigantic epaulettes and the breeches
of a British foot guardsman. It is all very puzzling, and
creates an impression that the Russian Government has
bought up a job lot somewhere, and is clothing its
defenders out of a discarded comic opera stock.
“Stony”: Saw a disgusting sight the
other day—a lovely woman, robed in purple and
fine linen (I can vouch for the purple, anyhow),
who had for companion a monkey on a chain, a
wretched beast that skipped plaintively along
behind her with a miserable expression upon its
wrinkled chiv. It was a disgusting sight. Science
and theology haven’t quite decided whether man
rose or fell —whether the monkey or the angel was
his progenitor. I never saw any woman leading
an angel behind her on a chain, but the sight of
her dragging the other possible ancestor is more
than enough for me.
“ Cecil P.” : Bully dear, isn’t it about
time that you created a typical figure of Aus-
tralia? Britain has (in addition to zoological
representatives) the figure of John Bull, as well
as that of the lady sitting by the sad sea waves,
with her back hair down and holding a toasting-
fork. All we have are certain buxom young
woman, each of whom will weigh sixteen stone
before she’s forty, and your own “Little Boy
from Manly.” But the kid is no longer quite
satisfactory. He hasn’t grown an inch since I
knew him first. And Australia has. My oath !
Please ask “ Hop,” from all of us, to invent a
typical figure of Australia. Two points are
essential: it must be masculine, and it must have
a cheerful grin. If “ Hop” makes a clean job of
it, I’ll send him a real damper made by my own
fair hands.
“A.Z.” : The par re sailing ship Port Jackson
(B. 17/10/’O7) is like the curate’s egg of glorious
memory. The boys on her were not learning to
be officers, but seamen. They were drawn from
the Warspite, a stationary training ship in the
Thames. She is somewhat after the Sobraon
style, for “ ooor or destitute boys of good
character.” There is a very wide difference be-
tween the training they get and that of the cadets
on a ship like the Ulawarra. The cadets are
mostly from the Worcester, another training ship
in the” Thames, which is really a high-class school,
making a specialty of mathematics. The fees
are £65 a year, and the boys are turned out able
to pass the extra-master’s exam, as far as theoreti-
cal navigation is concerned. Two years on the
Worcester are accepted as one year’s sea service
by the Board of Trade. They then put in three
years as cadets or apprentices—in a ship like the
Illawarra, paying £5O or £6O a year—with the idea
of becoming officers in the mercantile marine.

“Radium”: Re the futile fines inflicted
on baby-murderous sellers of watered milk. Why
not put milkmen on the same footing as publi-
cans who, on being convicted for the third time
of selling watered grog, are disqualified from fur-
ther holding a license ? A short Act might well
be passed enacting (say) a fine of £lO for a first
thin-milk offence, £25 for the second, and for the
third six months’ imprisonment and disqualifica-
tion for life from holding a milk-selling license.
Spasmodic raids by the authorities effect nothing.
They only make the milk-dealer rush out in haste
and sell more water to cover his loss.
From an old officer :
Bulletin (31/10/’O7) quotes N’York Worlds corre-
spondent on the Jap invasion of Mexico ; for, disguise it
as you may, it is an invasion as real, and perhaps as
effective, as any open attack. The curious thing is that
the World has not apparently noticed the same symptoms
in British Columbia. There can be very little doubt
about the idea at the bottom of the scheme. The three
Pacific States, Washington, Oregon and California, are to
be caught between the blades of the shears, closing from
north and south. The southern blade will be
an army-corps, and the northern one perhaps
only a division, neither fully equipped. They will
certainly be without artillery, but will trust to over-
come resistance by superior manoeuvring. But they
will certainly find themselves out in their calculations,
in much the same way as they did in Manchuria, where
they thought they were going to pouch a big Russian
indemnity without reaching Russian territory. It was
the distances that beat them there, and the distances will
beat them on the Pacific Slope. The two bodies from
north and south would have at least a month’s march
before they could join forces, and if the State militia,
which is pretty well drilled and equipped nowadays, hap-
pened to meet them, it’s hard to say what might happen.
They don’t know everything in Japan.
Geelong (Vic.) apparently owns an extra
amiable lunatic, and several sleepy citizens are
eager to become acquainted with him. Early last
month 38 pairs of fowls, plucked, dressed, and
floured ready for cooking, were found in a bag in
the Botanical Gardens. The police took charge
of the poultry, and awaited complaints from
citizens suffering from poultry thieves ; but none
came. Nobody’s hen-roost seemed to have been
rifled, and no suspicious characters —easily spotted
in Geelong—had been seen about. The mystery
was put away in the proper pigeon-hole, only to be
dragged out hastily the other day when a con-
stable arrived with another bagful of birds, 50
pairs this time, all ready for the oven, as before.
The finds are getting on the nerves of the
“ foorce.” It doesn’t like to see all these juicy young
birds going to waste ; but they may be loaded, so
to speak ; and the Johns daren’t try them on the
dog, because the owner might turn up and
demand his property ; so the tucker just lies
around and invites flies and torments.
“ Loco ” : In these days of motor-car
fatalities, bike smashes, etc., speed judges are at
a premium. In my work—l drive a train engine
.—I find it necessary to have some method in my
madness. When lam struggling up a bank, and
can count the sleeper-ends as they pass under me,
I know I am down to four miles an hour, or
under. If I can count them easily, I begin to get
anxious, for a slip of the driving wheels will mean
“stick up.” When I can count the posts in the
fences (Oft. apart), I know the speed is not over
about 25 miles an hour. When I begin to lose count,
lam getting towards 30 miles. Then I count the
rail-joints ahead (in daylight)—generally 24ft.
rails. They are a guide up to 40 miles in the
hour, but not many can be counted at that speed.
After that the telegraph poles are a slight help.
I have not reached the speed when I can’t count
them —yet. I’ll describe the sensation for The
Bulletin, if I’m alive afterwards. At night, the
fire-box door opened now and then locates speed
and place. Also, the ear soon learns to fix the
speed of the engine from the “ beat,” the size of
the wheel being considered, of course.
Bill the Exploder.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ 1 v ILL,” said the old man reflectively, as he
r* x puffed in the corner —“ Bill was the
1. -J most careless man with explosives I
ever seen. One moonlight night, when first I got
t : know him, we was walkin’ home from the town-
ship, and he tripped and fell fair on his nose. He
got up with his nose streamin’ blood and his face
all scratched, an’ says t’ me :
“ ‘ That was lucky ! ’ he says.
“ ‘ What was ? ’ says I.
“ ‘ Failin’ on me face ! ’ he says.
‘ ‘ ‘ How d’ ye make that out ? ’ I says.
“ ‘ Because,’ says he, ‘ if I’d fallen on me back
we would have been blown t’ blazes ' ’ An with
that he puts his hand inter his tail pocket an pulls
out three sticks o’ dynamite.
“ Soon’s I felt better we resoomed our way —
me hangin’ on to his arm, in case he stumbled
again. I would have walked a quarter of a mile
behind, only it wouldn’t have been friendly.
“We had t’ pass round a sort of mountain
path, overhangin’ a deep rocky gully, an when
we come t’ th’ centre of it, Bill stops an says .
“ ‘ Wouldn’t it be a lark t’ chuck one o’ these
plugs down ! ’
“ 'No,’ I says, ‘it wouldidt /’
“ ‘ Oh, well,’ he says, ‘ I feel as if I wanted t’
shake things up t’-night—here goes ! ’ An' before
I could stop him, he pulls out one o’ th’ bits o
sudden death an’ tossed it over the edge.
“ I jumped back agin th’ cliff face, an waited
shiverin’.
“ There wasn’t a sound !”
“‘Well, says Bill, lookin’ round at me, ‘I
s’pose that muster fallen inter water or somethin
soft. It’s the rottenest disappointment I ever got
in me life ! ’
“ ‘ Oh ! ’ I says, ‘ is it ! ’
“‘Yes,’ he says, bitterly, *it is. When I
heaved over that stick I was promisin’ meself one
o’ the finest busts up that was ever seen. I was
fairly tremblin’ with delight, an’ I didn't care
what happened so long as things shifted. An’
what did happen ? Why, I might as well have
chucked me hat down f’r all the row it made ! ’ _
“ ‘ Bill,’ I says, ‘ I’ve met one or two fools in
me time ’
“‘Oh,’ he says, ‘you can tell me about ’em
some other time. I’m goin’t’ have another shot
look out! ’
“I jumped, an’ th’ next second there was a
roar, an’ everything in th’ gully—includin’ two
cows an’ a kangaroo —shot up inter the air an’
fell down again. Th’ sky was almost blotted out
with a dusty haze, an’ f r two or three minutes we
could hear bits of rock an’ rubbish that must
have been blowed up fifteen or twenty mile
splashin’ an’ dashin’ in th’ crik.
‘ ‘ Bill sat on th’ crumblin’ edge of th’ cliff,
fannin’ ’isself an’ spittin' an’ sayin’, ‘ IsrUt that
grand ! ’ every time somethin’ fell. I couldn’t get
him t’ come home until he was certain everythin’
was down an’ th’ show was all over.
“ He talked about it f’r th’ rest of th’ week, an’
told me how much good it had done him, an’ how
grand it must feel to be an anarchist. He said he
wouldn’t call th’ king his aunt if he could only
have a good supply o’ dynamite, an’ get a license
t’ chuck it at politicians an’ them sort of things.
“Well, after such a howlin’ success, he went
in f r it reg’lar, and whenever we heerd a noise
like th’ Day o’ Judgment away in th’ hills we
knoo Bill was out enjoyin’ ’isself. He blew up a
whole lot o’ things that didn’t belong t’ him ; but
as his claim was payin’ handsome at th’ time, he
used t’ pay up like a sport.
“ I never knew him t’ do any wilful damage but
once, an’ that was when he blew up Hogan’s bull
that had charged him th’ day afore. It was one of th’
most wonderful things I ever seen. One minute
there was a roarin’ bull, and th’ next there was a
roar an’ no bull !
‘ ‘Ah, but it was a dangerous game ! I useter tell
him he’d be blowed up ’isself some day—told him
over an’ over again ! ”
The old man sighed, lit a match, and puffed
until the bowl glowed red in the dusk.
“ And was he ? ” we asked.
“ He married,” said the old man, very simply.
KODAK.
Lord Roberts writes the preface to a
copious volume on Fontenoy, just issued. It is
by one Skrine, a retired Indian civil servant.
Roberts vividly demonstrates the present bearings
of this study. Fontenoy was part of that terrible
volume of warfare opened by Frederic the Great s
invasion of Silesia. The moral of Roberts is that
Kaiser William is going to play some similar
game. All the trouble arose from the death of
the Austrian Emperor, in 1740, and here is
another Austrian Emperor dying by inches, with
the vultures roosting on the tailboard of his bed.
Marshal Saxe, who commanded the French at
Fontenoy, wrote a book called “Reveries,” with
a singular forecast of universal military service
and Socialism.
“Cross Nibs” sends an alluring pen-
picture of life in his suburb
Was standing, last Saturday night, near Soor Lung s
fruit and produce store, waiting for a tram. Along came
a sawed-off Scotchman with a parcel of fish beneath his
arm, and large quantities of beer or other fermented
liquors in his inner cavity. Suddenly, and without any
warning, one of the most venerable bananas in the State
exploded with a sharp report against the wayfarer s right
ear. Dislodging such portions of the fruit as were objur-
ing his vision, the assaulted one turned towards boor
Lung’s half-caste son and a couple of pals who stood
before the shop front displaying the extended smile.
“Whit bletherin’ misfit,” he demanded passionately,
“banged me aboot th’ heid wi’ th’ stale frit. The
junior Lung, with ready wit, requested him to lodge his
complaint in English, or else engage an interpreter, lo
this the other made the following neat retort: ill tecnt
a hunner o’ monkey mixtures like ve before suppin ma
whusky. Nae doot y’ll ken richt weel when I bash y r
heid fur ye whit I’m speirin’ aboot.” Urged by his com-
panions, Lung now lashed out viciously and smote the
Scot upon the ear. Whereupon the latter promptly un-
wound like a released clock-spring and hooked the senu-
alien with his left and then smashed him over a pile of
pumpkins by means of a right swing. At this stage ama
standing on the footpath intervened. ‘ Eh, mister, he
yelled, “Squeezer Smith ’n’ Pockets wi .as jii fc
bunged away round th’ lane wid yer fish. The Oale-
donian departed hurriedly, leaving m his wake a curren
of robust profanity. Lung turned to the others andsaid,
“’E is’ot stuff; another wood’ner ’n’l’d ’ave met th
ambulance. That second crack smacked aU th lnsh
outer me, ’n’ yer know, ole cobbers, I couWnt expect
Chow stuff to see me through. Here, my tram ajrrivi g,
I boarded it and heard no more.

PRACTICAL TEST OF THE TRYEE AUTOMATIC
RABBIT DESTROYER MADE ON MR. CHARLES
HARVEY’S FARM, CROOKWELL.
Crookwell, 4th November, 1907;
To the Manager, Tyree Limited,
314 George Street, Sydney.
Dear Sir,—l have much pleasure in informing you that
the trial of your famous RABBIT DESTROYER took
place on Friday, November Ist. In all, three tests were
made, two in my paddock and one in Mr. J. Keith’s, and a
large number of rabbits were killed in each test. In No.
1 test, a marked rabbit was placed in the burrow, and on
opening the burrow, it was not found on Friday, as the
whole of the warren was not opened ; but on Saturday
morning, when Mr. Lesmond and myself visited the farm,
we could not find any trace of one rabbit having
left the warren, and on opening a small pocket a short
distance from the main burrow we found the marked
rabbit, together with fifteen others, quite dead. I am
very well pleased with the result of this trial, and have
no hesitation in stating’ that the TYREE RABBIT
DESTROYER is the best means of destroying the pest.
My opinion is, that if the burrows are not interfered with,
not one can escape the deadly poison, and, as they remain
in the burrow, no nuisance is caused, and no other animal 1
or bird is affected by the poison. I can confidently re-
commend this machine to all those desirous of wholesale;
destruction of this pest.—Yours faithfully (Signed)'
Charles Harvey.*

Interesting to compare the two mighty
songstresses who foregathered at Melbourne Cup
t’other day. Butt is indolent by temperament—
she shows it in her face, figure, singing method,
everything. She doesn’t hanker after Society as
such ; and has none of the qualities that make a
great hostess or leader of a Salon. Melba is a
mighty, restless worker. Also she is one of the
few women in Europe capable of getting round
her the leaders in the things that matter, and keep-
ing them there. If she hadn’t been a Melba, she
would probably have been a great woman in
some capacity quite apart from the Voice factor.
The contralto, so that she gets her effect, doesn’t
care much how she gets it. Her mighty organ is
unique, and she knows it, and for two pins
she’d decide to sing without ever privately prac-
tising again ; and give up the time thus saved to
water-melons and her family. With Butt, singing
comes a bad third in life’s sprint—both the infants
and the Rumford gentleman being whole laps in
front of Art. But Melba is the artist first and all
the time. The Voice first; Melba, owner of the
Voice, second ; the rest of the world, “down the
course,” a bad third—that’s likely the correct
order of things in racing parlance. Thus
it results that Butt sings amazingly badly
(considering she is Butt) on occasions. But
Melba never. Each note of hers, each tiniest
trill, has been toiled at with meticulous care ; and
furbished up and polished till it shines like clear
agate. Summed up, Melba is an extraordinary
woman, with a Voice attached (the Voice here is
an unessential really, since its possessor would
have been a notable in some other line without
it); the Butt phenomenon is a Voice attached to
one who is just ordinary good family woman, and
who sans voice would have aroused no abiding
interest outside the weighing room of the physical
culture establishment of her suburb.
** * *
“A Life’s Revenge ” was revived on
Saturday night, at Sydney Criterion. It is by
Walter Howard, and is in that playwright’s riper
manner—less horrific than ‘ ‘ The Face at the
Keyhole,” more robustious than “ The Midnight
Mesalliance.” The piece is not encumbered by
any unnecessary top-hamper in the way of plot.
Its venue is Russia ; which connotes ever to those
who know their melodrama conventions, a
welter of uniformed undesirables, over-dressed
princesses, floggings, snow, and serfs, with here
and there a strong cold man of probity, and now
and again a virtuous loving woman. A very
evil aristocrat is Boris Strogoroff, flight Prince
of Marishla. His assortment of strange infamies,
with their bafflement by the more reputable mem-
bers of the cast, constitute the play.
* * * *
Boris is obsessed with a maniacal deter-
mination to marry Princess Vanda Ivyala, though
she hates him and tells him so with extreme
candor. The Princess’ pick is Paul Starovitch,
alleged by the programme to be an artist. The
young fellow’s studio is shown (Act I.) with a few
samples of his work. It is a subtle indication of
Vanda’s entire freedom from mercenariness in
accepting Paul. She who could marry the per-
petrator of such unsaleable outrages has a heart
to pity and forgive, and a soul above wealth.
Boris—not, so far as appears, out of any regard
for Art, but owing simply to jealousy—has
Starovitch dragged from his studio and turned
into a serf. Then he proceeds to make love to
Vanda. It becomes plain, after a while, why
Boris fails so signally to allure. His staple small
talk consists of anecdotes of the various inno-
cents he has knouted to death and flayed
alive. These anecdotes may be lies; certainly
they sound as if they were. But at best
they aren’t pleasing lies, and they are
punctuated with bursts of epileptic laughter that
disquiet intensely. What pure young princess
wants to hear these details of a knouter’s past,
anyway? All through, Boris shows the same
hopeless want of tact. Act IV., for example, finds
him giving a party. Vanda is there; also Bert
Bailey, Edmund Duggan, and others of unim-
peachable integrity. By way of entertaining his
guests—and j ust as in these parts a man might
hire a German band or unloose a gramophone—
Boris organises a flogging. And who, pray, is the
victim? Some negligible tea-canvasser or other?
The member for the district ? The cook ? Not at
all. Paul Starovitch, no less. Absolutely the
most popular man in the house. The awful
futility of thinking to win one like Vanda by such
methods should be apparent. The end of the play
is that Boris gets what he has been seeking
for some considerable time past—a violent
death at the hands of an enraged serf.
Laurence Dunbar, inadequately disguised in a
set of black Astrakhan whiskerings, takes the
part of Prince Boris. He is most convincing, and
would have met with severe maltreatment from
several incensed Anglo-Saxon right-thinkers had
he fallen into the auditorium towards the end of
his performance last week. Princess Vanda!
(Miss Eugenie Duggan) is a gorgeous personage,
and the family jewels, consisting of tiara arid dog-
collar of diamonds, etc., worn at all hours of the
day and night, are alone worth paying good
money to see. George Cross plays Paul Staro-
vitch satisfactorily.
Carter, the American mystery-monger,
now showing at the Palace Theatre, Sydney, is
the genuine goods. It is a big achievement to
mystify for about 21 hours a large body of self-
satisfied Sydneyites. Carter works on lines
originated years ago by Maskelyne and Cook, but
is a big improvement on those mid-Victorian
deceivers. How the lady changes cabinets ;
how Carter, when inside one himself, does the
things he appears to do; how wedding rings suffer
a sea-change into guinea pigs, and guinea pigs
into bouquets—all these are circumstances that
remain inexplicable to the ordinary mind. The
probabilities are outraged with an amazing surety
and completeness. Miss Abigail Price mind-
reads successfully, and shows a touch of neat
humor here and there; and Allan Shaw does a
coin-manipulating act. Because there will always
be people who find their keenest gratification in
seeing something which ought to be in one place—
but which isn’t—transfer itself swiftly to another—
where it has been all the time; and because also there
are those in our midst who prefer seeing basins of
water evolved out of nothingness, or sensate
creatures altered into bits of wood, to any other
form of dissipation, the Carter show will succeed.
When all is said and done, this magician’s most
impressive trick is the transforming of the Palace
Theatre from an empty into a full house.
You can’t get a penn’orth in a Sydney
tramcar now without being surrounded by young
men and women with deep lines in their foreheads
and a pained expression on their tight-drawn lips:
the competition craze has them in its coils.
It was bad enough when it was simply
a matter of supplying Limericks for the penny
papers and the vendors of lolly and patent
pills. But now even J. C. Williamson
has joined the enemy ; he offers five guineas for
information as to the best means of spending
£250,000 in Australia under the same conditions
as afflicted Brewster, of the Millions. Now you
can watch the Miss from the tape counter get as
far as—
There was a young man of North Shore
Whose sufferings from measles were sore ;
But he bought Biggs’s Pills,
Now he’s done with life’s ills—
And then she starts counting her fingers, and
you know she’s come a cropper on the Limerick,
and is reckoning up how to spend a quarter of a
million before Christmas : (Thumb) buy 11 motor
cars ; (first finger), back the Sunday paper mining
tips; (middle finger), make G. Reid Prime
Minister, and then start 17 new factories;
(third finger)—and you’re at the Railway, and
she still has two fingers left. Meantime, at the
Theatre Royal, the original Brewster (Thomas
Kingston), supported by Mrs. Brough and Miss
Titheradge and the rest, do good enough work to
stand without all this buttressing, though even a
good company can’t make the scene in Brewster’s
office anything but tedious.
** * *
“ The Blue Moon,” without being any-
thing prodigious in the way of a musical-
comedy success, continues to do its duty at
Her Majesty’s—its chief duty being to put a
prolonged smile upon the face of the ticket-office.
The stage decoration is admirable, the bogus
Burmese matrimonial agent and general money-
grubber is a quaint creation, and Miss “Blue
Moon ” Murphy’s singing is the work of a true
little artist.
** * *
The Whiteleys and the Molassos still con-
stitute the large joints in the Tivoli’s backbone,
though since last week it has grown another
pretty large item—a monologuist who tells you
how the Sunday-school choir practises for the
anniversary. He adds a bright stroke to a bill
that was already as full of color as an ox-
persuader’s eloquence.
** * *
The story of “Robbery Under Arms” is
being re-told nightly by the cinematograph at
the Oxford Theatre, Sydney. To get the pic-
tures Charles Macmahon roamed the Western
Plains, accompanied by a band of appro-
priately dressed mummers ; and so soon as a spot
was reached whereon Starlight had stuck-up
someone, the apparatus was fixed, and wild,
bloodsome proceedings began, to the infinite
diversion of the natives. To get all this (a film
4500 ft. in length) cost the management well over
£lOOO. And the result now is good.
Musgrove’s Opera Co., having apparently
become tractable again, put in a satisfactory
Cup week at Melbourne Princess’s. The loss of
Greder was very noticeable in “ Faust,” inasmuch
as his successor, Riinger, reduced the Devil to a
quiet little man of no importance, but the change
was not all for the worse. Greder’s stage manage-
ment included an absurd extra scene on the
Bogeyberg, where the chorus ladies gathered
together on a sort of vice-regal platform, and
pretended to be holding out new temptations to
the depressed tenor. The revived “Faust”
omitted this lamentable farce, so the per-
formance took less time than before in
challenging comparison with the best local
traditions of Gounod’s tuneful work. On
Saturday night the co. tackled “The Fly-
ing Dutchman ” for their first time in Mel-
bourne, and on Friday of this week they will
repeat the attempt. “ The Dutchman,” although
not nearly so Wagnerian as the more experienced
achievements of Divine Richard, is not less
German in feeling than his later musical-mystical
plays. You get the solo of inordinate length, and
the long drawn-out pose-pauses, what time the
orchestra is understood to be curdling your blood,
or making your cuticle creep. And there are
some comparatively simple melodies in “The
Dutchman.” Wagner’s plans for excising
“tune” were not nearly completed when he
dealt with Yanderdecken. The choruses—notably
the Spinning Chorus in the second act —are worthy
of the keen Australian voices that sing them in
the Australian native language. Of the principals,
Miss Heinze (almost as bright as a soprano would
be in the prima donna part), Riinger and the
tremoloic Oeser are better placed than Rupp, for
the comedian baritone plays the heavy father who
introduces an embodied spook to his daughter in
a manner that ought to be quite serious. But
Rupp, in baggy striped pantaloons and a
long gaberdine, is a somewhat comic parent.
He robs the scene of its due eeriness. He
reminds one of a Jew money-lender bringing
home a Juggins to tea. The staging is not
even so effective as it was six years ago, and the
apotheosis of Senta and the Dutchman might
easily be mistaken for an octopus painted on
gauze. “Rigoletto” comes into the repertoire
this week. Mozart’s “Marriage of Figaro” is
due on Monday, and these with the Wagner
operas will keep the season going until Friday,
the 22nd.
* * * *
“The Midnight Wedding,” at the Royal,
has lost a little of its “comic relief” since the
first night, and is all the stronger for the pruning.
The comedy scenes of this otherwise staggering
drama seem intended to give the audience mental
relaxation from the excitement of business. In
between these periodical interruptions the story
puts a terrible strain upon one’s intellect. It is
loaded with elusive reminiscences of bygone
dramas, and just as one feels almost certain that
Harcourt Beatty was Don Romeo de Bazan when
he got married, on the eve of his execution, to
Juliet Mari tana in the Friar’s cell, he turns out to
be Lord Clancarty, or the Bondman, or some
other son of an unexpected father. Also Miss
Madge Mclntosh gives a highly impressive per-
formance of more characters than one during the
night. The individuality of the actress changes not,
but the situations in which she figures seem to
pop up suddenly from the caverns of memory,
and at every apparition the present writer
murmurs “Fancy meeting you!” To an old
playgoer “The Midnight Wedding” is a be-
wilderment of recollections. And perhaps it is a
play with a purpose. The author, likely as not, is
the gifted proprietor of a matrimonial agency,
where the parson lies in wait for customers. The
first act seems intended to encourage hasty unions
between young people who don’t wish to get
married at all. Anyhow, the play was produced
with a purpose, and the oofsome end of Meynell
and Gunn will be served every evening until
further notice.
The Bijou Theatre (Melbourne) on Satur-
day night last made a new departure with
Somerfield’s “The Tyrant,” the Roman play con-
taining an Italian despot, a Greek avenger, ana
three leading ladies. Other notable sundries are
gorgeous foreign soldier-men and a British war-
ship, and a stock of Jack Tars sufficient to man
a ferry boat. Thisbe is an actress of Padua,
and is loved by the Tyrant. But the
tyrant is already married, and he is also
the proprietor of a young female victim whom he
has tyrannised into a lunatic asylum—so Thisbe’s
chances of being hoisted into the honest-woman
class are slight. In any case, Thisbe doesn’t
care about the Tyrant. She cherishes a strong
passion for a certain Prince Rudolph, who with
characteristically bellowdramatic perversity, loves
Adrianne, ill-used spouse of the Tyrant. The
situation, as may be seen, is fraught with infinite
possibilities in the way of unpleasantness._ Ru-
dolph—with the best and most honorable inten-
tions in the world—is having a chat with Adrianne
when her husband arrives, accompanied by
a posse of minions. There is _ trouble.
A disguised Friar eventuates (he is in reality
the Avenger), likewise the mad girl, who seizes
the opportunity to identify the Friar-
Avenger as a long-lost brother before being
seized and taken once more to her asylum.
Shortly afterwards, Rudolph again gets into a
compromising situation with Adrianne (he is dis-
covered—with the best of intentions —locked in the
lady’s bedroom), and the Tyrant, now really irri-
tated, decides that there must be an execution in
the family, with Adrianne as star performer. Butat
the crucial moment the good old British tars dash
in, salvage Adrianne, and, in defiance of all
the equities, remove her aboard a man-of-war,
presumably to some place where her meetings
with Rudolph won’t be interrupted by coarse and
misunderstanding husbands. In a play whereof
the excitement is tense, all the performers act well.
If any merit special mention, they are the deep-
voiced Ida Gresham, who plays Thisbe ; and
sweet little Beatrice Holloway, the angel wife of
the Tyrant.
* # «
The Maoris at the Melbourne Town Hall
are disappointing. There is none of the fierce
and bloodthirsty Haka, with its fearsome yells,
one expects. For a Poi dance “in full cos-
tume ” the funniest tribe trooped on to the plat-
form. A big half-caste led the procession—and
a fat quarter-caste blocked up the keystone.
Then there was a bedecked, black-haired
lady, who looked pure Yiddish, and. a
youngster who looked plain gamin ; he might
have been a Herald boy, with his thin and
wiry limbs covered with Maori drapings. And
the Haka ! It was exactly like a poor, spirit-broken
circus tiger—with scarcely a growl even left to the
memory of warlike prowlings. It was not an in-
spiring show. Tourist pictures filled the gaps.
The Voice of Melba, which threatened to
leave Australia longing for it in vain this journey,
is to be heard in Melbourne Town Hall, Satur-
day, 23rd, and the Saturday following. Geo.
Allan (of Allan and Co.), who represents General
Desire in a managerial capacity, announces that
the regal songstress will be supported by William
Black, a baritone whom Melbourne remembers
with gladness, and Miss Una Bourne, an artist
fully qualified to play the piano, even in Melba’s
company. Also, Lernmone will be the flautist, and
Hattenbach the ’cellist.
** * *
The last fortnight of The Greatest Show
on Earth, and already Olympia is advertised
“To let; ” so if anyone is looking for a nice snug
little residence, with stabling for a horse or two,
and plenty of room for pets, well—here’s a
chance. In the meantime, hundreds of enthusi-
asts rush every night to see the Theatre-Circus ;
the elephants—patiently putting up with fool
people whom they could bash to pieces so easily
had they a mind to—quite good-naturedly standing
on their heads, or dancing on a barrel, or trumpet-
ing out a “ song” ; the horses that know the days
of the week and month and year, and other
astounding facts ; and a dancing mannikin of a
tight-rope-walker, who finds cool, clear air the
most substantial of supports.
** * *
“ Gottenberg,” at Her Majesty’s, is a
record-breaking show. The theatre has scarcely
felt the unholy peace of an empty seat since the
opening night; and on race nights and other holi-
days grumbling crowds are turned regretfully
away in hundreds.
** * *
If Melbourne has been pining for an
“Ideal Coon,” the long-felt want is supplied
by Rickards, at the Opera House, just now.
Einglas is the name of the Coon, and his claim to
realise the lofty ideal is held over for considera-
tion. Hayman and Franklin, the droll Hebrew
impersonators, are maintaining their former
appearance of Moses and Rachel; also, La Sylphe
dances witchingly as usual, and Price or Revost
continues to fall off the table with brilliant
success. Amidst a number of more or less popular
performers, the man who comes in violent contact
with the floor is the most applauded.
Quite evidently there was no local deprecia-
tion in Clara Butt stock after she went on tour.
Thursday’s one and only grand-final-return-fare-
well concert had a house of so bumper a description
that there was no room for any more bumps on
the premises. General enthusiasm over “Land
of Hope and Glory,” and other Organ-backed
Empire songs roused the infatuated crowds to
cheer like the vociferous bookmaker who is
watching a rank outsider come in. The Huge
One was clothed in the tint of a half-ripe lemon,
with a sprinkling of gold dust here and there.
The racing season was represented by the Butt
booming “Lorraine, Lorraine, Loree,” a song
wherein a woman rides Vindictive in a steeple-
chase under instructions from a loving husband,
who expects to be made a widower at the last jump.
The Rumford was in excellent voice, and the
violinist and pianist ambled along in their gentle,
harmless way. The house was fully fashionable.
The Big Lady goes to Tasmania, but returns here
—more concerts, do you think? Mebbeyes. Up
to now she has only had two farewells in Mel-
bourne, and, in a daily-journalistic way of speak-
ing, the “vast resources of the Exhibition
Building” have not been taxed at all.
** * *
Julius Knight does not seem to have quite
all his old attractiveness for the other sex, and
thus “Robin Hood”—which is a play built for
the girl-market if ever anything was—has run its
week at Adelaide Royal to houses respectable
rather than great. Miss Elbertorton is getting
her chance at last, and the lady with a Mabel
Lane figure and a Maud Jeffries voice has rapidly
become popular. As maid Marian, she is well
placed. The support is good, especially on the
male side. Clara Butt is just starting what
promises to be a triumphal week of the cult'of
art, combined with the domestic virtues; the
Tivoli is showing the finish for the Cup, per bio-
graph; and a variously computed - number of
thousands of public-school children crammed the
Jubilee Exhibition at two successive days and
nights of work-and-play performance.
“F.H.R.” : I wonder more people do not
die of heart failure the way they rush round.
See a fat woman, fashionably-dressed, tightly-
laced, saunter down towards the ferry. While
she is yet a hundred yards away the steamer
prepares to leave. She lowers her gamp, grabs a
hundred-weight of frock, frills, and furbelows,
reveals a quarter- yard of the leg which has cost so
much to hide from the rude male eye, and dashes
passionately for the wharf. If she has covered
the distance under evens she may be in time to
jump. Ear too seldom, taking it as a warning to
others, she smacks the ocean in the face. If she
misses the boat she shows up in her worst possible
form. Her hair, clothes and temper are consider-
ably ruffled, and anyone who dares to sympathise
with her is snubbed fatally on the spot.
“ Stony ” : I have always thought that
leprosy was an incurable, transmittable horror,
its victims to be avoided as one would avoid the
public hang-gentleman. I find these ideas con-
firmed in a copy of a Melbourne rag which is great
on missions and Methodism and the latest cheap
styles in clothes for women. [Paper patterns
sent free on receipt of sevenpence ha’penny in
stamps.] This paper says tne compounds of
Pakhoi, China, contain “two of the most weird
collections of human beings in the world. Leprosy
is one of the most horrible of human diseases.
The Chinese themselves recognise its horror, and
banish the lepers to a separate village. We went
through the native leper village outside Pakhoi,
and a sad spectacle it afforded.” But this isn’t
the worst horror. The paper, after taking a deep
breath, calmly goes on to remark that “now
quantities of fine silk and linen laces are made by
the wornen-lepers, and sent to England for sale ”
—under missionary supervision !
H. Bassett writes:
Re par (B. 31/10/’O7), Mr. Hasell states : “ Any foreigner
can own land in Japan, if naturalised, but not otherwise.”
Well, if he is naturalised he is no longer a foreigner.
“ Foreigners are allowed to hold shares with some
limitations, which, however, are removed by naturalisa-
tion.” Mr. Hasell was there five minutes, and knows
all about it. The foreigner is not wanted in any
capacity except as a tourist, and for the last six
years conditions have been made constantly harder
for him to bear by drastic and unfair Customs
decisions, and the utter impossibility of forcing
a Jap to keep a contract. Hautot and Co., turning over
a trade of three million yen per annum, do not close up
their business for puerile reasons. Why did the ’Frisco
flour shippers refuse to ship any flour to Japan unless the
cargo was paid for before the ship left port? Because
the merchants were nearly sure to find on arrival of the
ship in the Japanese port that the price of flour had
fallen through the machinations of the local ring, and
unless a reduction was made, the agent had a shipload of
flour on his hands.
The tourist in Japan is filled with delight at the ex-
treme politeness of the Japs, but let him stop long enough
to be known as a resident, and the change is wonderful.
Does he step from the hotel with friends and hail a rick-
shaw? They are accommodated, but it requires the com-
bined services of policeman, porter, and runner of hotel
to induce a puller to haul the local man. Is he in a
retail trade (and you can count the Europeans on one
hand who are, in any Jap city), his employees are giving
his business to rival Jap firms or “cooking” him out of
it.
Recently, there has been much laudation at the Jap
Consulate over the arrival of a passenger ship in Sydney
commanded by a Japanese captain. Why? Japanese
cargo boats have been all over the world. Why
this excitement ? Because it was a passenger boat 1
The Jap says he is strong enough now to
put on Jap skippers, and a lot of foolish people
pat him on the back, and tell him what a great man he is.
Heretofore all the Japanese boats carrying passengers
have had white skippers. The N.Y.K. line tried, some
years back, Jap captains, and no white people would
travel on the boats (except missionaries, and they don’t
count), so the English, Swedes, and Danes remained to
grace the head of the captain’s table. In every city in
Japan, Korea, and Manchuria the fiat has gone forth:
“ The foreigner must go 1 ”

PENEOLD'S
WINES
When a woman proclaims her virtue, she
has usually lost it. It’s lost things that are adver-
tised.
“ OCHO! STOP YOUR TICKLING .”
Never before, in all probability, has flippancy been
more generally mistaken for humor than it is at present;
never before has the world been so ready to listen to that
debased wit which turns to jest whatever is august and
venerable, which amuses the groundlings by its cynical
ridicule of the grave and gracious and heroic sides of life.
—Argus.
“F.H.”: I was recently privileged to
travel first-class with a perfect lady and her
young. Ma had a Junoesque figure about to
burst a rich garment of appalling design, and her
young consisted of a small girl with a snub-nose.
'The small girl dropped an aspirate with a sicken-
ing thud ; whereupon ma boxed her ears. The
kid howled, and, finding herself unpopular with the
•other occupants of the carriage, ma relented.
“Kiss me, and be a good girl.” she coaxed.
“ Shan’t,” roared the kid, aiming a kick at ma’s
shins. In the twinkling of an eye ma had her by
the shoulders, and shook her till her teeth rattled ;
then, beating her head against the partition,
screamed, between bumps : “ Kiss me, you little
wretch ! Kiss me.” I had no idea these society
women were so impetuously affectionate.
The N.S.W. Bill to provide counsel for
accused persons does not excite much enthusiasm
among professional criminals. Most of these
would rather face a jury undefended by counsel,
making it appear that their poverty and want
of friends created a disadvantage. That plea,
which generally touched at least a few of the jurors,
will in the new arrangement be impossible. In
the early days of Sydney, outside parties were
almost always ready to stump up money for the
defence of a noted criminal, and times out of
number criminals objected to the help. Their
barrister, they feared, would make it look like a
fair fight between the Crown and the accused,
whereas if it were the Crown against the accused
unaided, the sporting instinct would incline Judge
and jury to sympathise with the lawless dog.

It’s a good job “ Mr. James ” has got his
title ; else how could he expect it with the S. M.
Herald printing things like this:—
The German Emperor, after visiting King Edward, will
stay a few weeks at the Isle of Wight to recuperate.
And after all the trouble we’ve taken, too, to
explain that Edward has quite settled down.

The greatest indiscretion a public man can
perpetrate is to say openly the thing other people
are saying in private. Thus U.S.A. Admiral
Evans, on the Jap-Yanlc situation, at a New York
banquet last week
When things get a little bit strenuous and you know
that there are 80,000,000 people behind you, you like to
see something done, and it’s a bit hard if you’re not to
do it.
Which is so candid a truth that had the Japs
meant business at any stage of the trouble which
has been simmering for so long with the U.S.A.,
the big guns would have taken up the discussion
within 24 hours.
* * * *
Lodge presentations are sometimes frauds.
Brother Smith, of the Independent Order of
Ancient Honorable liastlers, a dear old fellow
whom everybody loves, was to be invested with the
Grand Gaffer’s Certificate. The hat was passed
round, and then carefully examined for stray
coins. Enough were discovered to supply a large
piece of paper covered with flowers, extinct or
imaginary animals, and some hieroglyphics, and
the secretary suggested that Brother Smith have
it suitably framed before presentation night.
Smith, who is not affluent, passed over a pound to
a picture-framer, and had the thing fixed up in an
oaken frame. On the night of the presentation
the rest of the Rastlers arose and duly presented
Smith with the picture and many panegyrics on
his virtues. But nary a word that half the cost
was contributed by Brother Smith. Still he was
luckier than Brother Brown, of the Honorable
Order of Wild Snowballs. His brethren pre-
sented him with a beautiful marble clock as a
mark of respect and esteem. The gaud was duly
presented, and after the fact had been well-adver-
tised, and all the friends of the family had trooped
along and listened to the cathedral chime of that
clock, Brother Brown received an account from
the clock merchant, which explained that the
Wild Snowballs had paid lbs. deposit, and he had
to get the other £4 15s. from somebody.
The luscious mango capitally illustrates
the great extent of Bananaland’s wealthy coast.
When the crop, which takes four
months to mature, is ripe at
Douglas, the fruit is half-grown at
Rockhampton, while the trees are
j ust bursting into blossom at Brisbane.
Other crops to illustrate the extent
of the coast-line are tomatoes and
cucumbers. The chief industry of
Bowen, the disappointed natural port
of North Queensland, is the output of
these products months before the
Sydney and Melbourne markets have
started to draw from local sources. As
amatter of fact, Bowen yields both
vegetables right through the winter
months, with the exception of a few
weeks. Port Douglas produces fine
citrus fruits well in advance of
any centre in Australia now raising
them.
“ The Cup ball was attended by the
visiting Governors and their ladies,” say the
papers. Australian dailies are terribly afraid of
saying that persons such as Governors are ever
accompanied by their wives. There was reason
for abstaining from this form of expression many
years ago, but it should be well understood that
at present, and for a considerable time past,
Governors are as conventionally reputable as most
other people. No Australian newspaper need
now be afraid to say a Governor was seen out with
his wife.
* * * *
Says Sydney News, which has a lag love
for the lash :
Wife-beating is a difficult thing to deal with, because if
the offender is sent to gaol, the wife suffers ; and, if he is
fined, the fine will fall on her to some extent. It would
seem, therefore, to be a complaint wherein a sound whip-
ping is indicated as an appropriate and equitable remedy.
But if the whipping be a sound one, would not the
brute be as much incapacitated for work thereby
as if he lay up in gaol in the ordinary way ? Why
not send offenders to gaol and make them work
there for the bruised wife and her children ?

Hobart’s Anti-Sosh Mercury has un-
earthed a new reason for busting up the Common-
wealth. Governor Strickland decided to hold

The review should be held at Hobart because it always
has been in the past.
Since it is to be held in Launceston this year, Governor
Strickland should be deposed for want of tact.
Senator Keating- lives in Launceston, and he is a mem-
ber of the Government.
Therefore, the whole thing is a ruse of the Deakinites
to interfere with State rights, and nothing but Secesh
will meet the case.

Advfc. from a Sydney paper :
TAP., first-class cook, 30, wishes to correspond
** young Lady, view matrimony. Photo exchanged,
or never mind photo. Apply , P.O.
Now, here’s a chance for Cole, or Spruce Myth,
or someone else who says he loves the Jap as a
brother, to prove it. Surely there must be one
eligible daughter in those households.
There is yet time to alter the decision that
the King’s head must appear on the new Austra-
lian stamps. Again why? Does the King want
his head on our stamps? Does Australia
want it? A King’s-head stamp can hardly
be really artistic; and it will be a little
tragedy if it is allowed to crowd out the
chances of the lyre bird, or even the koala.
The Australian isn’t a snob, though some of his
prominent personages try hard to give him that
character. It is probably the snobbery of some
official “ dressed in brief authority” who is mainly
responsible for this King’s head idea. It is on a par
with the action of the Sydney Art Society, which
came under a good deal of criticism because it wasn’t
artistic enough, and sought to silence its critics by
changing its title to the “Royal” Society. Or
that of the Australian Engineers, who have just
won consent to call themselves the “Royal
Australian Engineers.” They won’t engineer any
better; nor will they attract a single worthy
recruit with the new title. On the other hand,
they will have given some yawnful Colonial
Office clerk a chance to say wearily, “ What silly
snobs, these —aw —Botany Bay folk are ! ”
* * * *
What backstairs influence was used in the
Sydney City Council that it should have decided,
by no less a majority than 17 votes to four, to
permit the Sydney City Mission to take up a
street collection? Granted that the Mission is
what it professes to be, it is, as compared with
fully two dozen other charitable institutions in
Sydney, a tinkling cymbal and sounding brass.
In the matter of disbursements for the needy and
suffering it is absolutely insignificant when the
whole of Sydney’s charitable bodies are remem-
bered. Moreover, the Sydney City Mission
devotes a pretty large proportion of its revenue
to salaries, office expenses, and general manage-
ment. Yet the Council, by its decision last week,
has given the Mission the right to utilise the city
streets fora whole day in order to make collec-
tions —precisely the same concession which the
Council gives the combined hospitals in the
metropolitan area.
C. M. Sheldon, the Yankee lecturer and
writer of religious fiction, has broken out in a new
place. He is advocating ocean liners that “don’t
sell a drop of rum, nor permit any gambling.”
“If I had money,” says the Rev, Charles, “I
would build them. They would be most success-
ful.” Which is the way of the world—the man
who hasn’t any money knows just how to make
it. The men who have money don’t know.
That is how they come to get the money.
THE CURSE OF RICHES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. John D. Rockefeller addressed a gathering lately on
the curse of riches. “Money,” he declared, “was a
burden.” The American nation is in danger of going
“ money-mad.” “No one seems to eare for the future,
and some day there will be a terrible crash.”— Recent
Press Item.
“ This bag o’ pelf—it crushes me,” says he to Uncle Sam ;
“ Of pleasure in this mortal coil I do not get a gramme ;
I bear a cursed load by day, and never get the guerdon.”
Says Sam, “If I .
Should care to try,
Would you pass on the burden ? ”
“The future,” wenton J.D.R., “is black as black can be,
And Yankee folk—the pack of fools—they do not care
a D.
Some day there’ll come a deadly crash to tear the world
asunder.”
Says Sam to John :
“ Go gaily on ;
Till then you have the plunder.”
“ The Sins of this great Nation here —they quite undo a
man,
Who’s got enough to battle with (twelve million pounds
per an.)
My health is broken by the strain of bending to
receive it.”
Says Sam again:
“ Conceal your pain,
Or folk ma3' yet believe it! ”
“ I clearly see,” says John again, “ we're going to the
bad;
One half the earth is moneyless and half is money-mad ;
I’m always vexed to see men grab regardless of the
needj r .”
Says Sam—“ J.D.,
I’m glad you see
The curse of getting greedy 1 ”
“ I do not like to speculate if things will ever mend,
But much I fear the dollar here is all the Yankee’s end ;j
And when I preach against the Pelf—they call me
Visionary.”
At once say we—
“ Wade in, J.D.,
And be a missionary ! ”

FURNESS BORN.
BUST UP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The female revolutionaries of Russia are now carrying
infernal machines concealed in their bosoms.— Cable
message.
Up in Russia, far and frozen,
Where the bomb your window blows in,
And a well-developed tendency to lynch is,
They’ve a terror that’s alarming
In the shape, that’s reckoned charming,
Of the lady with a bust of forty inches.
With a round resilient bosom,
Man is often like to lose some
Of his brains, if he should.chance to come to clinches ;
But, in Russia, there will scatter
All his grey and cherished matter,
When he runs across a bust of forty inches.
There’s a danger daily bigger
In the plump and pleasing figure,
That’s inviting to caresses sly, and pinches ;
For, within a steel cuirass hid,
Is a pound of picric acid
In what seems to be a bust of forty inches.
Don Juans are all in pieces ;
Just a little spot of grease is
Every youth who had intentions to be naughty ;
And, to-day, each ardent lover
Breaks away and runs for cover,
When he sees a girl whose bosom measures forty.
vie.
MILTON MACGREGOR.

The Australian High Court has given an
interesting decision on a domestic point. Some
years ago a mother died leaving a baby girl
behind. The father handed the infant over to
her grandmother, paying for its maintenance.
He married again, and has a second family of six
children. Now the father wants the daughter
home. The people who brought her up are very
much attached to the girl, and she to them. The
Full Court of Victoria decided that it is a prop and
a pillar of the British Constitution and British
justice that a father cannot be parted from his
offspring, and that nothing could be imagined,
save the fact that the parent was a rogue and a
vagabond, to prevent a father claiming his child.
In this case, the father being a highly respect-
able citizen, the Court had no hesitation in finding
in his favor. On appeal, the High Court
reversed the order. Chief Justice Griffith said it
was a cruel proceeding to wrench the child from
a comfortable and happy home and throw her
among half-brothers and sisters, who would pro-
bably be unsympathetic, simply because a man
whom she hardly knew and didn’t have any
regard for, happened to be her father.
* * « ■*
A crowd was watching a procession
through Melbourne, a few weeks ago, when a
little girl strayed on to the road, and was on the
point of being run over by a lorry. But a “ bloke”
jumped forward, tossed the youngster back to a
place of safety on the pavement, escaping so nar-
rowly himself that the wheel of the lorry
rolled him over roughly. Several people ran
to his assistance, but he rose by his own
efforts, bruised and angry. “Damn it ! Just my
bloomin’ luck ! ” he said, pulling out of his pocket
a colored handkerchief, and examining its contents
ruefully. “ What’s the trouble, mate ? ” inquired
an onlooker. “ The bloomin’ salt an’ pepper’s
spilt all over me pie, an’ the eggs is all broke up
ter blazes. I’ve bin always tellin’ ’er somethin’d
’appen if she didn’t boil ’em ’arder ! ” And the
hero almost wept.

If Immigration Arthur’s enemies want to
annoy him, they should publish widely in
England selections from the law reports showing
the pitfalls which beset the seeker after a living
area in N.S.W. For instance, a selector in the
north recently was lucky enough to draw the
winning marble in a ballot for a coveted O.P. area.
Before taking possession his father died leaving
a G.P. area behind him. As the parent died
intestate the lucky gambler found himself entitled
to a seventh share in this C.P. area. The un-
successful gamblers then haled him before the
•— and , u ? n th® grounds that he was
a holder of acquired Crown Lands and
therefore debarred from taking up an ad-
ditional area. The Land Board sent the
case on the Land Appeal Court, which
annulled the C.P. The selector thereupon
engaged more expensive counsel, and went to the
uli Court, which took the view (that to the
ordinary intellect not hopelessly befogged by an
intimate study of the land laws of N.S.W was
the only commonsense one) that the Act prohibit-
ing the holder of one C.P. from acquiring another,
was passed to prevent deliberate attempts to
aggregate big holdings, -and that the temporary
holding of a speck of land in the way that the
appellant acquired his, did not rob of him of his
national right of sharing in a raffle for a piece of
land suitable for the bringing up of an Australian
family.

Melbourne Working Men’s College is
doing well. Every class is crowded. In
each trade there are applicants always wait-
ing for vacancies. But there is no money for ex-
tension, and the Bent Government will not assist..
An example, this latter, of short-sightedness.
Ihe .State s interests would surelv best be served:
by the constant turning out of expert workmen.
Here is a college at the doors of which young
Australian workers are always clamoring for
admission in order that they may learn to become*
more expert, and thus more profitable to them-
selves and their employers. And the comment of
the employer clan on the matter, is, “Let’em
clamor. Trades unions are in some instances,
assisting towards the proposed Endowment Fund.
Which seems to indicate that unionism has more,
brains than Fat.

“Peter Pan” savagely defends the;
wickedness of Adelaide: The constant references;
to Adelaide as the “City of Churches” and the
“Holy City” are getting me down, and while I
am going down I want to kick. You can no
more judge a city’s holiness by its millions of
spires than you can tell a sausage by its mackin-
tosh. Consider these few items: Adelaide
can put its silver on a “dead cert” in
the street with perfect freedom. Melbourne
and Sydney cannot. We publish betting odds ;
the Eastern States are not allowed to. The
S. Australian Government has legalised the
totalisator; neither Ma nor Victoria have been
so sensible. The populace on Saturday nights
mobs Bundle-street and insults itself, and Ada
Ward was broken-hearted over us, and we chased
’Lige Dowie out by the back door. What more
can we do to prove that we don’t deserve a pain-
ful reputation for goodness ?
ONLY FAIR.
Clerk of Petty Sessions (fixing the bail for
the drunk and disorderly): “So your sureties are

BUNG : “ Yes, ids a fair thing, mister. We fill ’em up, and then bail ’em out.”
A feature of any big race meeting that
•astonishes mere man is the indefatigable nature of
the slim, seraphic little lady in a successful dress.
To the average he-creature there is nothing so ex-
hausting as two hours spent in perambulating the
V.R.C.’s half-guinea department under a hot sun,
and in the dusty atmosphere inevitable in the
press ; but the heat and burden of the whole day
is born by his tender bud with a fortitude that is
simply heroic. Hour after hour, up and down,
down and up, and round about, the graceful, fragile
little thing will carry her burden of shantung or
marquisette, and her crushing head-load of floral
decorations, and never make a whimper or turn a
hair ; and yet if that same gentle soul were asked
to chop her own wood and build the new brick
kitchen she would be out after a new summer
divorce within ten minutes. There is growing up
in cynical souls a sad conviction that the weak-
ness of woman is nothing to the weakness of man
in having been deceived by it ever since the Stone
Age, or thereabouts.

The other was made up of some thirty, dirty,
white city loungers, who leaned around and spat.

Tweed River (N.S.W.) district, now
•enduring drought troubles, is a special example of
improvidence. The average rainfall on the river
runs up to over six feet per year. Yet a spell
■of dry weather has reduced the district to
sore straits, and some of the Murwillumbah
people are buying water at £2 a load. For im-
providence and neglect to put by for a rainless
•day the N.S.W. cocky is a marvel.
* * * •
A gentleman named Rock represents the
sawmill employees on Sydney Labor Council.
When the Council was debating the question of
Australian national defence and the local building
•of torpedo craft, Mr. Rock “thought the Council
should advocate peace, and did not want any
swords or fight or anything of the kind. If any
foreign invaders came here to take the country,
and the trouble could not be settled by arbitration,
they could have the country, as far as he was
■concerned. He had nothing to fight for, and
would not boost up somebody else who would
stand on a hill 10 miles away giving directions.
The Council should advocate universal peace.
The only way fighting would affect his trade
would be to keep the men busy cutting timber for
•coffins.” Does this Mr. Rock understand that if
there is an invasion of this country, the odds are
that it will be by an Asiatic army that will cer-
tainly resort to horrible atrocities on helpless
women and children ? If the danger were of con-
quest by a white and civilised foe, Rock’s remarks
would be bad enough ; but when it means racial
•obliteration by a savage and repulsive enemy of a
lower type, they are simply disgraceful.
Sydney City Council’s “tip” at Moore
Park is again on the rampage (it’s always a
nuisance, but in spring it is an extra-special
nuisance). Last week, Dr. Armstrong, city health
officer, inspected the tip, and found it in an “ un-
satisfactory condition. . . . The garbage recently
deposited had not been sufficiently covered with
sand, and much of it was exposed, offensive, and
swarming with flies." Let’s see—didn’t the City
Coun’cil decide, some time last century, to destroy
the city’s garbage by fire ?
* * * *
The quiet calm of an up-country
(N.S.W.) court was broken last week when a
case of alleged wife-desertion was being heard.
The wife alleged shortage of clothes as part of her
complaint, and whilst she was giving evidence
the husband left the court, went home, and
shortly returned with all his wife’s apparel. He
strewed ball dresses, opera cloaks, coats, and
skirts, etc., about the court-room on chairs,
tables, and even the dock, with the triumphant
air of finally answering the complaint. But he is
said to have brought all the articles to the court in
one portmanteau. Now no woman’s clothes
should go in one portmanteau.

“ Hypatia’s ” reflections :
Upon receiving an invitation—" What shall I wear ? ”
says woman ; “ Will there be anything decent to drink? ”
says man.
Drink is a huge joke to man ; to his women folk, mostly
a tragedy.
Few women marry for love, generally because they are
expected to.
Woman attracts man by her ignorance of the world ;
man attracts woman by his knowledge.
Women say nasty things ; men do them.

“ F.M.” : I want to know why a woman
may not be as proud of her physical development
as a man, without having flung at her, by the
Judkinses, the accusation of immodesty. In
some cases, I dare say, the woman has a definite
object: she wants to capture a man ; but it may
also be said that to many a finely-developed
young woman a man’s desire of her comes as an
infinite mystery. Yet the man will call her im-
proper should her innocence allow her to spread
herself on a postcard as a mermaid or something
nice like that. And why ? Surely she may
Sose as Venus if Hackenschmidt may pose as
lercules.
THE VICTOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To work the mystic will of God
The shining stars and moon and sun,
And the vast systems of the earth,
Their everlasting- courses run.
The human creatures of His craft
Have, ever since the world began,
Stalked through the cavern of the earth,
Moved by the baser will of man.
TAS.
C. L. RANDALL.

THE WILD CAT
COLUMN
For the first time for 34 years the Bank of
England has put up its minimum discount rate as high as
7 per cent. At the same time the Bank of France, which
went through last year’s troubles without turning a hair,
raised its rate from 3J per cent, to 4 per cent. The
trouble comes from the United States, and sprang out of
over-trading and frantic speculation. Things established
on that fever basis had, of course, to turn sooner or later,
and then the rush-out became just as frenzied as the rush-
in had been. The Trust cos. felt the pinch first.
These cos. are occasionally respectable. But
not always. Concerns —insurance cos., for instance
—which cannot gamble with their own money, pass com
on to trustcos.; and the same people gamble with it there.
The doubtful character of many of these Trust cos. natu-
rally laid them open to an early demand for gold.
Then Canada, which always keeps a good deal of its so-
called liquid surplus in the U.S., rushed in demands.
Owing to the recent American boom the ordinary com-
mitments ahead are vast; and to be sure of meeting
them, reputable concerns preferred to have gold in a
Chicago meat-tin, or any other place at home, rather than
in a bank. So the drain of gold from Europe began over
again.
Now tbe English gold position isn’t nearly as
strong as some of the best authorities would like to see
it. The Bank of England, besides doing most ordinary
banking business, is the banker for other banks. Those
banks have assets at the Bank of England, and reckon
them among their liquid reserves ; and the Bank of
England reckons the same assets among its own liquid
reserves. So one lot of liquid reserves has to safeguard
two lots of liabilities. The system is weak ; nothing could
prove that more absolutely than the jumping up of the
bank rate to 7 per cent. It means this : that the Bank
cannot offer financial accommodation to even its own
British clients at anything short of a most burdensome
rate. Over the Channel the Bank of France has kept its
rate until now at 3J- per cent. ; and even now that bank’s
rate goes up to only 4 per cent, as against the Bank of
England’s 7 per cent. The Bank of France certainly has
the right—really nowadays as a last resort—to pay in
silver ; and that no doubt gives it more confidence. But,
whatever the cause, the result is pretty clear—the Bank
of France is doing its duty to French commerce when the
Bank of England is only prepared to do its duty to
English commerce on almost prohibitive terms.
What would happen if the Australian State
Governments had some of their big I O U’s maturing isn’t
pleasant to think about. Fortunately, they have had
three most prosperous years, and mostly have a bit in
hand. But the Governments can’t hope to strike that
sort of luck twice, or three times, running. The worst
knock now will come through the inevitable shrinkage in
prices ; in fact, metal prices have already shrunk so much
that they have left many mining cos. so much uncovered
that they are seeking refuge in liquidation. Dearer money
will, however, also mean lower prices for ■wool, and other
such products. How long the reaction will last nobody
can tell. It must be pretty plain, though, even to the
optimists who were lately telling people to plunge, that
the recent high prices were based to a great extent on
frenzy, and were as abnormal as the morning thirst of the
man who has spent a wild night among mixed drinks.
And at a time like this the reaction is liable to be just as
severe as the repentance of that unsober man. Which is
a point that ought to be obvious, yet is too often over-
looked by the people who have been enjoying boom
prices : the prices that follow a boom have a bad habit of
going just as far below normal as the boom prices went
above normal.
Meantime, why this veneration of gold ? The
situation reproduces on a colossal scale the scene at the
Barrack-street Savings Bank (Sydney) in the Australian
panic days. There was a crowd clamoring for gold.
Then up came Premier George Dibbs, towering above his
fellows, and scrawled upon a sheet of paper the pledge of
N. S. Wales —“ I will pay.” It was as good as gold, and
settled the whole trouble. Roosevelt may play the part
of George Dibbs yet, and then they will wonder what all
the fuss was about.
Though the voice of the farmer has been heard
in the land raising the familiar cry for more concessions,
and though this year’s wool clip will fall short of last
year’s, and though the metal markets have had a bad fall
and weak mines have shut up, the Australian Treasury
returns still wear a smile that stretches all across their
face. The figures that follow show how the States’
revenues have moved up in the first four months in each
of the last four financial years, except in the case of S.
Australia, which is tired, and only makes up its figures
once a quarter

Probably these figures show things at their very best. In
most cases railway revenues will now shrink, and land
and some other returns won’t grow any larger. More-
over, the Australian Customs tariff, though it has made
a full Treasury for the States so far (and got curses for its
pains), is steadily getting to its real work of promoting
Australian industries and making the Commonwealth in-
dependent of imports.

Commonwealth refunds (Customs) and railway revenue
have been almost exactly equal factors in swelling the
N.S.W. revenue : from the Commonwealth the State has
got £273,619 more than it did last year, and from the
railways £273,756 more. The two account for more than
the total net increase, so there has been a small net re-
duction in all other lines. Queensland’s very substantial
improvement is almost wholly due to the railways ; their
totals for the two years have been : 1906, £569,918 ; 1907,
£687,401 —a gain of £117,483. S. Australia is £96,794 to
the good in railway revenue, thanks mainly to the way
the Barrier is pouring out its metals in order to catch the
boom market. But to get railway revenue costs the
States money ; the Commonwealth refunds are all net
revenue, free of charges.
