The others—Victoria and Tasmania—trust their Bents
and Urquharts till the end of the financial year.
In 1906 N. S. Wales had at the end of October a
deficit of £412,118; this year a surplus of £155,691. The
improvement therefore amounts to £567,809. But the
Public Works Fund fake in N.S.W. upsets all such com-
parisons. This year (four months) £312,496 has been
charged up to that fund, which is fed from the alleged
surplus ; and most of the £312,496 ought to have been
paid for out of revenue direct, which would have in-
creased the ordinary expenditure and spoiled the look of
the pretty surplus. Besides this £312,496 charged to the
Works Fund, £427,645 of borrowed money has been spent,
and the Wade Government proposes to get to work and
pawn things much faster in the future.
Queensland charges up its interest only once a
half-year. At the end of October, 1906, it had £651,696
saved up to put on Uncle’s plate ; this year it has
£688,536, or £36,840 more. But, then, Kidston doesn’t
manufacture bogus surpluses through a Works Fund.
While N. S. Wales professes to have spent only £7036
more this year than last, Kidston, of Queensland, owns
up to having spent £87,154 more, largely on repairs,
works, etc.
S. Australia seems to be in for another good
year. It is about £89,000 to the good on the first
quarter, though the first quarter usually ends with a
deficit.
Westralia’s October revenue wasn’t enough to
pay the month’s bills by £40,411, and the total deficit is
now £221,383. A little thing like that, however, doesn’t
worry it. It just says, “ Oh, but we’ve got a sinking
fund,” and remarks that it hates interruptions, and what
are trumps. It forgets that to keep pace with the de-
pletion of the goldfields its sinking fund probably ought
to be 50 per cent, larger than it is, and that, if so,
Westralia is no better off with its sinking fund than some
other States are without any at all.
* * * *
Postmaster-General Mauger has been sniffing
the correspondence of one of the Tasmanian banks, and
suspects dead-birds and other prohibited goods. His idea
is that, as this moral generation may not use the post to
reach Tattersall, the bank has turned itself into a Tatter-
sall agency. Recognising that it would be a pretty
serious thing to shut down on any bank’s correspondence
without warning, the P.M.G. is asking the bank two or
three questions before he decides to go on strike.
Matters are not likely to reach that crisis, however, be-
cause even for its best customer no bank could afford to
risk the endless complications that might arise through
the stoppage of its mails.
* * * *
The Vie. Savings Bank now gets a step nearer
the common or garden variety of bank by undertaking to
pay up to £2 on demand at any branch on presentation
of pass book. But a depositor can only make one such
draw a day.
* * * *
From “ the town where oysters are 3s. 6d. a
dozen, and poor at that” (Oct. 3): “Well-informed per-
sons in London say the drop in copper from £llO to £6l
was greatly helped by the huge quantities of ‘ scrap ’
and second-hand copper raked out of every odd corner to
supply the demand early in the year. One firm told
writer it scraped up 600 tons from everywhere last
February, and did not sell at less than £lOO a ton. No-
body can check the amount of ‘ scrap ’ copper constantly
coming on the market, and that’s where copper statistics
all this year have been untrue and misleading. The Aus-
tralian press regularly quotes the weekly metal circulars
of interested dealers, and their forecasts of the early part
of the year make comic reading to-day. Copper looks
now like bottoming at about £61.”

The half-year’s profit is at the rate of no less than
£l5 10s. lOd. per cent, per annum on all shareholders’
funds—capital and reserves combined. True, it is men-
tioned that there have been “ some exceptional recoveries
from old accounts” ; but, on the other hand, Consols and
other such securities have been written down out of
profits “to market value or under.” The fixed policy of
this bank is to write assets down hard. Thus the pre-
mises account stands at only £251,836 ; the amounts
shown above as written off have mostly been used to
reduce this account. Compare that with, for instance,
the A.J.S. Bank, whose premises stand at £366,742 —
£366,742 to handle £4,088,084 of advances (book value),
while the ’Asia, with £251,836 of premises, handles
£15,840,960 of advances. No doubt a great deal depends
upon what one expects a balance-sheet to be. There are
people who say that a sheet which under-values assets is
as misleading as one which over-values assets ; and there
is little doubt that the ’Asia’s premises are worth more
than the figure they stand at. But it is a fine fault in a
balance-sheet, anyhow ; it is an extension of the common
and generally-approved policy of writing furniture down
to nothing. The policy explains why this bank’s figures
as to profit return are so high compared with most
others ; for this bank, by reason of this policy of writing
down, hides much of the reserves which some others show.
For instance, if instead of writing off £204,000 in the past
six years that amount had been added to reserves, the
earnings on the basis of capital and reserves for the past
half-year would have been at the rate of £l4 Is. 6d. per
cent, per annum instead of the £l5 10s. lOd. per cent,
which the accounts show as they now stand.
What the recoveries amount to there is, of
course, no way of telling ; but there is nothing in the
balance-sheet figures to account for any very large in-
crease in the normal profits:—
April, 1906. April, 1907.
Notes .. .. £543,748 £504,671 Dec. £39,077
Bills, etc. .. 2,924,503 3,291,936 Inc. 367,433
Deposits .. 16,964,281 16,622,967 Dec. 341,314
Liquid Assets .. 7,661,127 7,466,103 Dec. 195,024
Advances .. 15,484,095 15,840,960 Inc. 356,865
Premises .. 280,660 251,836 Dec. 28,824
Advances have increased by £356,865, and deposits have
decreased by £341,314, but the two items taken together
would not explain very much of the spring in profits from
£137,795 to £227,463. To the recoveries have, of course,
been added the phenomenal monetary conditions at both
ends of the world during the half-year. Australia had.
plenty of money just when London wanted to borrow it,
and the rare circumstance put Australian banks on a-
remarkably good wicket. What, however, seems worth
noting is, that the figures as to profit published 12
months ago are, probably, a much truer guide than those
now issued. A word was said just now about an increase
of advances and a decrease of deposits, and the movement
has naturally reduced the liquid assets. But the propor-
tion is still very solid ; over 36£ per cent, of the debt to
the public is in coin, public stocks, etc., and those stocks-
have been written down “ to market value or under,” and
the recent market value is an unusually low level. The
profit, calculated on the basis of shareholders’ total funds,
has moved in recent years in this way :
Oct., 1903.. £ll 3 3 p.c. Oct., 1905 ..£9 13 9 p.c,
April, 1904.. 10 19 9 „ April, 1906 .. 912 3 „
Oct., 1904 .. 10 1 9 „ Oct., 1906 .. 10 11 11 ~
April, 1905 .. 914 0,, April, 1907 .. 15 10 10 „
But again the recoveries have to be taken into account.
The bank has the use of about £7 of the public’s money
for every £1 of its own funds ; and that is regarded as-
about the limit of safety. Finding themselves in much
the same position, two other of the bigger banks
increased their capital lately, and, by either charging no
premium at all or only a small one, practically put up
their dividend while keeping it at nominally the same
rate ; and there was a lot to be said, from the share-
holders’ point of view, in favor of that, for dividends that
run beyond 10 or 12 per cent, are apt to invite trouble.

The last few lines show in the handiest way the results
of the cleaning up. In 1905 there was a partial over-
haul, and £12,044 was provided as a depreciation
suspense account, and is included above in the
amount shown as written off in 1905 ; the balance
of the writing off in that year represented a reduction of
reserves, and apparently covered losses on realisations.
On the past year’s revaluation it was found that £34,918
ought to come ofE the assets, including the £12,044 already
provided. Prom all shares 3s. 6d. was therefore written
off, which provided the £24,393 shown, and left the
capital at £83,227. During the past year sales of certain
properties were rushed through to clear them out of the
way, and the rush meant a loss of £llBl even on the
written-down values. The fact suggests that the writing-
down has been none too emphatic ; on the other hand, it
is quite possible that the co. was prepared to make some
sacrifice in order to promptly get rid of unproductive
properties. The gross receipts from interest and rent
last year were £13,202. On the written-down values this
shows about £6 ss. per cent.; and that isn’t so high either
as to suggest that the assets have been written down
any too hard. The deposits and other liabilities amount
to £127,990. There has been a small increase in deposits
during the year; and the change in the appearance of
the accounts has certainly justified the extra confidence.
As against the £127,990 of debt to the public, the co. has
£28,566 of liquid assets ; and against the £99,424 of debt
not covered by liquid assets, there was at August 31
£193,806 of non-liquid assets (mainly advances, proper-
ties, etc., written down); so the depositors have nearly
£2 of security for £1 owing to them, and that is a margin
which ought to give them a satisfied feeling. As for the
shareholders, however, their capital is still poorly pro-
tected, and dividends ought to be kept low while a more
substantial reserve fund is built up.

This co. furnishes very little information. Its paid capi"
tal is £lO,OOO, and that and its reserves are supposed to
be put up for the protection of beneficiaries under the wills,
etc., of which the co. has the management. But the co.
has debts amounting to £11,606 (the total is generally
about £ll,OOO or £12,000), and that amount or there-
abouts is represented on the other side by advances.
The co. therefore apparently borrows money and lends it
out again, and the capital has to safeguard the borrow-
ings, and protect the depositors in case of a slump in the
securities. No trustee co. ought to be allowed to do this
business. Its capital and reserves ought to be maintained
for the protection of the interests of people in connection
with whose affairs it acts as trustee.
After giving notice of intention to strike on
Tuesday, the Newcastle coal-miners agreed to take no
action for three days, to see if matters could not be
arranged by what is referred to as an “independent
tribunal.” There is, of course, an independent tribunal
already in existence—the Arbitration Court—and.the very
people who have been persistently weakening the influence
of that body are the ones who are squealing loudest now
that they are face to face with the alternative of arbitra-
tion. No doubt it is unpleasant, when you want to dq
one thing, to be told by the law that in the interests of
order and good government you must do something else;
still, that is exactly what the law is for. And if the
Government, of which the present Premier Wade has
been the chief legal member for some years, had stood
firmly by the Arbitration Act, instead of listening to
the foolish clamor against it and allowing it to be pecked
at and defied all round, the present grave situation Would
not have arisen.
At the beginning especially, this dispute at
Newcastle was essentially one for such a body as the
Arbitration Court. The point was simply whether the
world’s market would stand a 12s. selling' rate or not.
The men said it would. And without a doubt they have
some evidence in their favor. On the very morning when
the three days’truce was announced, the Sydney Tele-
graph, a paper that is opposing the men’s demands and
snorting at them as preposterous, printed this
“ Soaecity of Coal.
“ Worth a Guinea a Ton.
“The scarcity of coal is already a burning question
with shippers and merchants, many of whom were this
morning offering as much as a guinea a ton.”
If merchants and shippers can afford to offer that, the
men certainly have some grounds for believing that they
ought to be paid on a higher basis than an 11s. selling rate.
It would be proper work for such a body as the Arbi-
tration Court to ascertain whether there was more evi-
dence on the other side. The owners say there is, and if
there is enough evidence to satisfy them they should be
game to undertake to satisfy a Judge also.
When the proprietors considered the question
of putting up the selling rate from the present 10s., the
majority favored 11s., while the minority favored 12*.
The miners were encouraged to believe that some of the
majority simply wanted to be squeezed a little ; in other
words, that they wanted to throw the responsibility for
the 12s. rate upon the miners. The miners, therefore,
began to squeeze. Unfortunately the American trouble
became acute about that time, and a gloom began to
spread over the industrial outlook. Even proprietors
who had thought a 12s. rate possible began to have
their doubts about it. But the men’s agitation, having
been created, wasn’t so easily killed. As usual, other
troubles cropped up. The payment for small coal is one
—a solid grievance that —and the use of electrical
coal-cutters in mines suspected of being gaseous
is another. The latter grievance was intensified
by the locking out of about 300 men at Hebburn.
These things make a settlement more difficult; but they
don’t render it impossible. Neither miners nor owners
want a strike, and that is the most hopeful feature of the
situation. But when it is over—even assuming a peaceful
settlement —the public will be a monumental ass it
it doesn’t reflect how near it got to a great disaster
simply through listening to the chatter of sometimes
interested and often irresponsible fools who have been

Two patent portable traction scoop engines
are being built to the order of Messrs. McLelland' and
Cooper for cutting the main irrigation channels in the
Waranga Basin District of Victoria. The engines will be
placed on opposite sides of the channels and will pull and
tip the scoops in their own direction, and so make the
channel and form the embankment. There are two
engines of the vertical compound type, and, along with
winding drums, friction clutches, worm gearing, and
other appliances, are mounted on a mild steel framework
carriage which projects above and forward over a portion
of the locomotive boiler. The boiler is mounted on
driving and steering wheels. The main haulage engine
is 160 t.H.P. and the smaller one 85 I.H.P. The Austral
Otis Engineering Company Ltd., South Melbourne, are
the manufacturers.* [D.]

the Broken Hill Junction North, so far as it can be got

Six months ago, when this incorrigible old patient was
called up to show its tongue, its shares were valued by
the market at £310,000. The total had previously sailed
far above that ; but it is down now to about £200,000.
The Junction North has for nearly 20 years been
always just on the eve of doing great things.
The co. has been formed and reformed until
there is none of the old Adam left (as a matter of fact,
the original co. was mostly Zebina), but all it has
actually done in,the way of dividends is a trifle of 4s.
per share, the last distribution being something over
10 years ago (April, 1897). The past half-year certainly
shows much better results. But, then, the boom prices
of metals must be remembered.
* * * *
Broken Hill Junction North last half-year
treated 29,745 tons of ore, and produced 4835 tons con-
centrates, averaging 58 per cent, lead and 29'10z. silver.
The lead was thus 2804 tons. The gross profit was
£17,726, which was equal to £6 6s. sd. per ton of lead.
Take the average for lead at £2O ss. for the half-year,
then the costs amounted to all the silver plus
£l3 18s. 7d. per ton of lead ; and that is
simply an “impossible” figure, remembering that this
calculation is based on gross profits, and that silver
brought a much belter price during the half-year than it
is doing now. With the new section of the mill com-
pleted, the costs may be reduced ; mining costs may also
comedown when the frantic rush for miners eases off on
the Barrier. But for the Junction North to. do any good
at all with even average metal prices, a very substantial
reduction will have to be shown.
* * * *
Broken Hill mine managers have not arranged
a uniform system of working out the metal recoveries, so
the value of the published figures as a guide in compar-
ing the effectiveness of the various concentrating mills
is discounted. The South and the North, whose
recovery figures always have nice clean faces, and
sit at the top of the class, work their crude
assays back from the concentrates and tailings,
averaging the slimes. This system, though open to abuse,
has much to recommend it. It was adopted in the first
place on account of the difficulty—some say the impossi-
bility—of arriving in any other way at a true average value
of a large tonnage of crude ore. Other mines, intending
to be more conscientious, take samples of the crushed
ore at intervals as it passes through the mill, and make
assays, the results of which are afterwards compared
with the assay of the concentrates, and the recovery is
worked-out that, way. To “ fake.” returns by this latter
process would require a deliberate falsification of figures.
With the back-working method—including the danger-
ous process of “averaging” the slimes—an enhanced
recovery may be secured without hurting anyone’s
conscience. .
* * * *
The Zinc Corporation is the Job of Broken Hill,
so numerous and ghastly are its trials and tribulations.
Out of the ashes of its disastrous British mine plant arose
a phoenix, but it never sprouted a feather to fly with.
Such an experience would have spelt ruin to an ordinary
company, but the “Sink” Corporation simply paid
the British the thousands it was liable for under the pur-
chase-of-tailings contract, and a few extra thousands for
not carrying out the contract as provided, then pulled
down the plant, demolished its drying furnace, and
levelled to the ground the spacious building'
originally designed to accommodate six magnetic
machines, which were bought but never used,
The ruins of this plant have taken weeks to convey
to the South Blocks Hill, the site of the big main
works; but apparently these ruins are of no value now
they are removed. The new plant is well advanced, but
even after the 16 Elmore units get to work the corpora-
tion has a long way to go before it can expect to realise
profits.
The Pinnacles, which now comprises the
Pinnacles and the Pinnacles Consols, a few miles outside
Broken Hill, was very badly treated for 15 years. Like a
willing horse, it was run to a standstill. Every bit of ore
that could be reached without much expense was taken,
and the mine eventually left in such a state that much
money has been needed to put it in proper trim. So far
it has proved itself more of a silver than a lead mine, and
the recent drop of 4d. an ounce in the price of silver has
had the effect of stopping the despatch of carbonates,
which are now being put through the mill with the
sulphides. The Elmore process, which was here installed
for the first time on the Barrier, isn’t proving exactly the
thing it was alleged to be ; but whose fault that is has
not yet been settled. Thirty-five tons of concentrates
per week isn’t anything like the stated capacity of the
two units, but that is about the best the plant has done
to date.
* * ♦
Broken Hill South Blocks made a profit of
£3453 for October out of 7381 tons of ore ; that is, 9s. 4d.
per ton. The concentrates (1357 tons) assayed 62J per
cent, lead; that meant 845 tons. Consequently the
profit was at the rate of £4 Is. 9d. per ton of lead. The
average for lead for the month was £lB 13s. per ton ;
therefore the costs absorbed all the silver and £l4 11s.
3d. per ton of lead. Silver certainly wasn’t at a high
price during the month (a shade over 2s. 7d.);
but nothing higher than that can be relied upon
as a general thing. Under those circumstances
the outlook is blue, unless the co. can do very
much better work. A mine that can’t produce
lead for less than £l4 11s. 3d. has a very bumpy path
ahead. What the future of the metal may be nobody,
of course, knows, but he would be a bold man who
would venture to say that it was going to average
£l4 11s. 3d. It hasn’t averaged that in more than four
years during the past 25. The public has only itself to
thank for rushing South Blocks in the way it did. It
had the claim’s past history to guide it: it also had the
report of Wainwright, of the South, who was called in to
advise about a treatment plant. The claim will make
profits when good markets rule ; unfortunately, however,
it hasn’t any rich ore to fall back upon to see it through
when the other sort of market has to be faced.
* * ,* *
N. S.W. isn’t so sure of getting the electrolytic
refinery works, promoted by Mt. Morgan and the
Hirsches, as it was a few months ago. Captain Bichard,
of Mt. Morgan, and the other advisers of the co., chose
Port Kembla as the best site ; and the thing seemed fixed.
But just when the N.S.W. State Government was screech-
ing loudest about the sinfulness of the Australian
Government in “ taxing enterprise and industry,” the
hypocritical State Government itself was making such
demands from the Refining Co. that the latter turned
away from Kembla and considered a proffered site at
Pinkenba, near Brisbane. The trouble springs out of
the Kembla harbor and other dues, which are excessive,
remembering that this co. will have to fight hard for a
while for existence. In spite of that, the co. may have
to go to Kembla, because Pinkenba is so far from coal.
The Government knows Kembla’s natural superiority, and
is evidently relying upon that to squeeze heavy charges
out of the co. And, of course, the miners of Australia,
the people who will supply the co. with ore to smelt and
metal to refine, will have to pay.
* * •* *
Things have taken a slight change for the better
at the Harden (N.S.W.), and the end may not corne as
soon as seemed likely a few months ago. One advantage
Harden holders and the public have : they know exactly
where they are, so far as the management can tell
them. Though the prospects are not bright,
the shaft is being sunk below the 300 ft. level
with the object of testing the shoots lower
down. Meantime, after paying for development,
October showed a profit of £1545. A slime plant is just
about finished, and, if it comes up to the mark (the slimes
—about 3000 tons—are worth about 265.), the co. will be
able to work in some of its lower grade ore, and ought
then to see ahead till the end of next year. After that,
pretty well everything deuends on what the next level
says.
At the Grafton mine, Cangai (N.S.W.), 100 and
odd tons of copper contained in matte await the bullocky
system of transport. Fortunately a new road, that will
avoid so many crossings of the Clarence, is being made at
the expense of the good old Grafton taxpayer. While
the copper market was sky-high the co. could have made
its own road and still been a few thousands to the good.
The mine has three reverberatory furnaces, and the
question of carting enough wood to keep the three going
isn’t coo easy even at this early stage. At present one
furnace is used to smelt the mine’s output of 60 tons a
week, and another furnace re-treats matte, bringing the
copper contents up to 70 per cent.
* * •
Long Tunnel Extended, Walhalla, puts a lot
of faith in the north ground, though that blew out two
cos. and took over £150,000 to prove that it was a duffer.
What really is more to the point to-dav is that Cohen’s
reef has been got 2ft. thick by the Extended at 2800 ft.,
with the usual “ good gold” showing. That expression
may mean anything, though, for the “good gold” in
Cohen’s reef runs in seams, and when you are on it, with
plenty of needle pyrites in the diori'te to top it off, the
average dirt is rich. Again, too, the underlie is only sft.
in 100, as the reef was hit in the crosscut at 401 ft. out.
This further knocks the Long Tunnel’s 17-in-100ft. underlie
theory on the head, and proves that in mining, just as in
other things, it does not do to count profits before they
are won. Had the underlie kept on at 17ft. in 100, the
Extended would have nothing to do now but to consider
itself a has-been. As it is, there is at least two years’
work in sight. Yet, as the reef makes west, a bit is eaten
off the length of the stone inside the co.’s lease, and the
shoot of gold, by pitching towards the boundary,
threatens to affect yiplds. Even without that shrinkage,
late returns have not been too rosy.
* * * *
There is not much to cheer the shareholder in
the Union, at Diamond Creek (Vic.), just now. The co.
went on the mission of trying to prove that the old miners
who left the show about 30 years ago also left payable
gold behind. This idea got some support from the fact
that the bottom of the old pump was found to be plugged
with candle ends. That was taken to be proof positive
that the men had let the water rise because they did not
want anyone else to know what treasures they had left.
Judging by the dreary pull that the reopening of the mine
has meant, those old miners stuffed the candles there be-
cause they thought the light of the claim was about
blown out. Still, what stone was got last half-year went
over Bdwt., and that would pay if there was enough of it;
but the bottom level has been a failure, and the co. now
relies on hitting the South Union shoot and a block of
ground between Nos. 6 and 5 levels. The mine has good
management, it is in the class of country where a good
shoot may be found, and as it has never been made a
Stock Exchange fake, it deserves better luck and decent
support.
* * * *
Melbourne broker OH ver, who was to the front
over the Mt. Hope float, has fluttered out into the day-
light over the Peter’s Gully, Heathcote (Vic.). The last
time he beat his wings against the bars was in the bad
days of Mt. Lyell. His altruism has now led him to
heckle Alec Campbell, who, having got rid of Broken
Hill Junction North troubles, is trying to toss the Peter’s
Gully caber beyond the bear mark. Campbell sent a
decent miner up to Heathcote to push the shaft down to
200 ft. ; but, because a bit of gold got at 175 ft. was left till
the sink was finished, Oliver seems as sad as a turtle-dove
that cannot find a mate. But Campbell would not
budge ; and those who bought shares when the gold was
cut are cursing their luck, and are finding out that- even
the striking of gold does not always spell a Stock Ex-
change jig.
* * * *
One of the Bright pioneer dredging shows is the
Maori Queen, which Langford and his friends biffed'for-
ward until £5O was paid for shares. The Queen is now
well on the dividend list, after many vicissitudes.
Twelve months’ work shows that nearly 200,000 cubic
yards can be lifted in that time. Last year’s operations
gave 1159|0z., the return panning out at about 250 z. a
week. The wash return was 2’816 gr., or sfd. per cubic
yard. Costs ran into £63 per week, inclusive of re-
newals ; with a dredge like the Maori Queen’s, this is fair
work. Cutting the work up into hours, the dredge won
178. 6d. at a cost amounting to 12s. 3d. per hour. The
co. has got hold of 130 acres next door, formerly held by
the Excelsior Co., which could not make ado of it. Cash
is wanted for a second dredge ; and judging by results
now that things are in order, there ought to be no trouble
over getting the needful.

‘ ‘ W atchd og ” writes: —“ For some years the Iron,
Steel and Metals Manufacturing Co. has been before the
public, its aim being to demonstrate that iron and steel
can be manufactured direct from the virgin ore at a
saving on the present universal system by which
the ore passes through various stages of purifi-
cation. Its efforts as time passed on without any
practical result, save the production of a small
quantity from the laboratory, at first excited curiosity,
then impatience, and finally ridicule. The recent despatch
of a circular to the shareholders, signed by a substitute
for the secretary, and undated, promising the ‘ long-
deferred demonstration’ within a fortnight, brings to
light that a very burning question in the minds of the
directors is the price of the shares. If the co,, during its
years of existence, has made no practical progress with
the development of its working scheme, it has certainly
done very well in this matter of keeping up the quota-
tions of its shares, and in getting them placed at large
prices, principally with people living at a distance.
“A perusal of the last balance-sheet, with a little
knowledge of the history of theco., will excite the envy of
many a modern financier. Starting originally with the
* patent rights ’ at cost (£15,000), it has still those rights,
plus a plant and machinery which might fetch, say,
£lOOO, as against 6000 shares held by the public, selling
at £2O each—in all, say, £120,000.
“ History is alive with instances of endeavors
to extract sunbeams from cucumbers and diamonds
from coaldust, but so far without success; and,
whilst this latest fad is seeking- for ‘ another fort-
night’s trial’ (from no fixed date), it would be just
as well to quietly consider if it is possible that
this may be in the same category. Although the com-
pany is very hard up for funds, and has put out or is
putting out amongst its friends the balance of its unissued
shares in wholesale blocks at £lO each, to be retailed to
the public later on at £2O or so, I see no evidence in
its balance-sheet of assistance from its banker. I would
like to see the look in that hard-hearted man’s face if the
company asked him for an unsecured overdraft of the
modest sum of £5OO, notwithstanding that its market
value is £120,000, and its real value, according to the pro-
moters, as many millions. Granting, finally, that the
forthcoming trial is successful in turning out steel from
raw ore, the real question at stake—viz., whether it
can be done more economically than by the old process—
is left untouched.”
* * * *
“ Janor ” : N. S. Wales is not the only State
which has had a fair-sized hole knocked in its money-box
by the prospecting vote (B. 24/10/’O7). Victoria’s figures,
though not so bad as those of the Ma State, are yet
rocky enough to brand the vote as a waste of good coin ;
for the money spent has not benefited mining to any
great extent, while it has given rise to much log-rolling.
The sums of from £lOO to £2OO granted to co-operative
parties of miners are mostly inadequate to bring the
claims to productiveness. Specified amounts are, how-
ever, allotted to each mining district, and to obtain the
best results for politicians (not miners) it is necessary to
chop the amount into as many chunks as possible. As a
rule, decent miners are “off” the Government aid,
because they can’t get enough to give them a reasonable
hope of paying off the debt; hence much of the vote has
been absorbed by wasters and hangers-on of mori-
bund fields, who are quite content to button
up on the 255. a week—and damn the Government!
Victoria’s great need at present, in the mining sense, is
the opening up of new fields. Most of the thoroughly-
riddled and hopeless old ones want a rest. There are
hundreds of square miles of mountainous country un-
explored. The miner who scorns wages is mostly broke,
and cannot tackle the job. It is work for the Mines
Department; but while that remains a hive of quill-
drivers with a nominee figurehead there’s no hope. The
whole show wants re-organising and made into a live and
hustling institution, with a boss like Delprat or Sticht,
whose object would be to produce metals ; and then the
useless circular branded H 59607, informing correspon-
dents that “ your letter has been received and will be
answered some day,” would get a well-earned rest.
* * * *
The report of the Great Tower Hill Gold Mines,
Ltd. (W.A.), covering nearly 2J years, is rather doleful.
The preference share issue, made in November last year
in order to provide further capital for development, was
responded to, only 810,497 shares out of
1,100,000 having-been subscribed for. The intention to
continue sinking the main shaft to 500 ft., and break
new ground in other parts of the mine, had therefore to
be abandoned for the time being. At the meeting held
in London on Sept. 27, the chairman (ex-Gov. Jerry Smith)
told shareholders that unless more of the preference
shares were taken up, the co. would either have to aban-
don the mine, or go for a reconstruction. The accounts
show that during the time the Tower Hill mill was
running, 67,200 tons were treated for,bullion valued at
£83,663. They also show a working loss of £164.
* *
Murchison Associated Gold Mines Ltd. (W.A.)
has held another meeting in London, and, as usual, share-
holders were buoyed up with yarns about visionary possi-
bilities. The chairman announced that, out of 600,000
shares in the reconstructed co., 452,460 had been sub-
scribed for, and £17,000 raked in. He said he was
confident of getting- off 100,000 shares out of the 150,000
still unsold, and wound up with the assurance that the
co. had never stood in a more promising position. But
really, isn’t it about time this co. performed as well as
promised ?
* * * *
The Coolgardie Gold Syndicate held a meeting
in London a few- weeks back, when Orlando Law presided
and talked about “shares which should be worth pounds
instead of shillings.” This co. was formed late in 1894,
and by W'orking off a litter of wild cats on J. Bull it
managed shortly after to divide amongst its shareholders
more than double the capital invested. Since then its
luck has been dead out. The co.’s chief interests now
comprise the Esperanza Westralia (which, despite its
name, is a hopeless derelict) and shares in the Great
Lucknow Consols and the Loddon Deep Leads. The
chairman hadn’t much to say in favor of the Westralia
Esperanza, but he opined that the Loddon Deep Leads
(Vic.) would shortly pour out gold, and predicted that
great success was sure to be met with in the Lucknow
(N.S. W.) if shaft-sinking w ere continued. A despairing
shareholder, who had had enough of the syndicate and
its interests, wanted to wind up there and then, but the
meeting decided to supply some more rope.

As an inducement for Parliament to sanction the
construction of the Mt. Magnet-Black Range railway,
the Westralian Premier recently tabled a return showing
the total gold production of the East Murchison field to
the end of last June : —82,2120z. from 55,389 tons treated ;
1041 oz. alluvial, and 16960 z. dollied and specimens.
Total, 84,9490 z. The returns from the Black Range dis-
trict alone should easily exceed these figures within two
years from date. The trouble, however, is that you can’t
tell the future of a mine by its past, and it is only the
future gold output with which the railway is concerned.

Wilgie Mia Color Co. was recommended four
months’ exemption at Cue (W.A.) the other day in respect
to its ochre property at the Weld Range. An affidavit in
support of the • application set out that the co. had
acquired the area for the purpose of working certain
deposits of colored earth and pigments, and that experi-
ments were being carried out to determine w’hether such
deposits are of any commercial value or not.
Mills’ Day Dawn United is capitalised at the
highest figure on Charters Towers; there are 300,000
shares, selling lately at about 15s. The half-year closed
with a profit of £51,450 on an output of 25,451 tons,
worth £4 os. 6d. per ton ; practically the profit and ex-
penses evenly divide £4 per ton between them. Chairman
Mills reckons on a regular output of nearly 4000 tons a
month, w'ith quarterly Is. dividends. It is obvious the co.
is not going to pile up a- reserve ; it can’t pay out £45,000
in six months and put anything substantial in the stock-
ing. Mine prospects have improved by the extension
of the eastern shoot to the New Brilliant Freehold
boundary ; the reef splits up into footwall and hanging-
wall sections—which is w’hiy the two leases to the east,
the New Brilliant Freehold and the East Mexican, have a
speculative value. If these shows benefit, Mills’ United’s
eastern shoot must be extensive, w'herefore canny people
have been buying Mills’ United. But still, some of these
people obviously are not too sure about it, and slip out
if shares rise. They haven’t forgotten what happened to
Brilliant Central and Queen Cross. Mills’ United certainly
doesn’t seem likely to peter out with dramatic sudden-
ness, but it is by no means a moral that it will still be
wearing the glossy pot-hat of prosperity at the end of four
years.
According to a calculation made on the spot,
Mt. Molloy (Q.) will have close upon £7OOO after pro-
viding its railway, if the estimates pan out all right. On
June 30 the liquid assets (including fuel and flux, which
certainly are not very liquid, but don’t amount to
much) totalled £67,386. From that deduct—Dividend
(July to October), £10,500 ; losses on operations during
same period, £4OOO ; sundry creditors (June 30), £7446 ;
total, £21,946. This leaves £45,440. Machinery absorbs
£3OOO, so that £42,440 will remain to build the line (esti-
mated to cost £36,000) and provide for development and
any errors there may be in the estimate.
* * * *
Warden Cherry, of Cooktown (N.Q.), reports
that the Mt. Windsor tableland reminds him of the rich
Atherton scrub land. The place is about 40 miles from
Mt. Carbine, and the Mt. Molloy railway will bring it
more in touch with civilisation. It is tin country, and
the Warden has ordered the Chows to clear out, as the
field is required by Europeans. The Queensland Mining
Act restricts the Chinaman considerably ; the way he
scooped up the shallow alluvial gold on the Palmer —a
few miles from Mt. Windsor—has not been forgotten.
The bit of alluvial tin left by China is probably of little
account, but fortunately China hasn’t been able to shift
the good land and fine running creeks. About 30
European tin-scratchers are on the field at present.
* * * ■»
At Smith’s Creek tin mine (N.Q.) the yield of
oxide per ton has fallen off. Either the drives and cross-
cuts put out by Captain Warren fluked on good patches,
or else the mill is now doing very poor work. Present
manager evidently blames the plant (breakdowns have
been pretty frequent), and has advised the harassed
Board to put up a 30-head battery to supersede present
appliances. If he has his way, £4OOO will go at one pop.
Whichever way you look at it, shareholders are in for a
bad time. A distinct denial of the 30-head battery busi-
ness was set going t’other day ; but 24 hours later a state-
ment that, “ if such a course is decided upon, Mr. John
Darling will advance the co. the £4OOO necessary,” was
assiduously spread. During that 24 hours the fall in
price of shares took place.

“ M.C.”: One reason for the knock-out of
copper shares is the idea that a mine producing only one
metal isn’t as good as a show with gold and silver mixed
w'ith its copper. The fact that Wallaroo 3 per cent,
copper ore is of more value than Mt. Lyell 2 per cent.
plus gold and silver is lost sight of. At Mt. Morgan we
are told that the red metal may cost over £5O to pro-
duce, but then, it is added, the gold value is over £45
per ton of blister. Quite true ; but in the absence of
Australian electrolytic refining- works (except the Great
Cobar’s, for its own ores), the foreigner takes a big bite
out of the gold and silver for the trouble of separating
the metals. It is just as well for the public to remem-
ber the foreigner’s share when the touts parade Mt.
Morgan’s copper and gold.

tees the preference dividend is not clear.
* * *
“P.”: The subject of tin and gold recoveries
and losses by dredging and sluicing keeps recurring.
Maoriland dredgers ascribe payable recoveries on the
fourth and fifth time of treatment of ground to the fact
that the rivers vary greatly in depth, according to
season, as much as 7ft. in places, thus preventing the
buckets from reaching the bottom. This gives the
dredges a varying depth of ground during a season, and
by retreating they strike a certain average of missed
ground, hence their payable results. Sounds very
feasible, but scarcely conclusive. Is there nothing
to be improved on .in their saving appliances ?
I know of an instance where no possibility of
missed ground could prove a factor in giving extra re-
return. Centrifugal plant, treating 80 to 100 yards an
hour ; run of boxes, 150 ft. long by sft. wide ; product,
gold and tin; tailings stacked on higher ground than
original position ; bottom of paddock scraped clean by
hand, and sluiced by nozzle, and every bit of dirt sent to
the tailings dump passed over this length of boxes con-
taining Venetian ripples, diamond-shaped or honey-
combed, expanded metal, broached plates, tram rails
crossed, wire covered with cocoauut matting. No
danger of the tailings being re-enriched by floods,
or any of that “piffle.” Yet four men using
the waste water from the sluice were recovering
enough tin and gold by ground sluicing the tailings to
pay the wages pf the 56 men on the mine. Another case :
bucket dredge, gold only, treating 8500yds. per week,
recovering -98 grains per yard, ground 15ft. deep,
weekly expenditure £5O (including wages, renewals,
rents and incidentals); paid £lOOO dividends during
1906. Manager ran dredge in tailings for some
time ; retreatment exactly same as original. States
he can get enough gold by second treatment
to pay all expenses. If it were possible to recover this in
one operation, would add £2600 per year to dividend.
Probably for capable management in poor ground the
above is a record. Same manager is of the opinion that
not a gold dredge in Victoria gets a recovery of 50 per
cent, of its gold, Class of gold recovered by second treat-
ment is exactly the same as on first recovery.

Be “J.P.” and the client
who omits calling on the auctioneer for the pro-
ceeds of stock sales, heme’s a Jimmy Tyson yarn
in point. In the roaring days the original of
Boldrewood’s cattle-lifting yarn in “Robbery
Under Arms ” took a mob of fats from one of
Tyson’s Bokhara properties, and headed for the
goldfield. With few fences to negotiate, this
master bushman got his prey through with but
one inspection—and that by a blackhoy merely.
But the nigger spoke of the pitchfork-branded
cattle he had seen. Tyson heard, deduced, and
acted with a rush, as was his wont. The pitch*
forks came to hand at Gulgong, and were duly
auctioned at diggings prices. But when the vendor
c>lled for the proceeds of sale, his agent told him
that Mr. Tyson had already collected, and had
left a message complimenting him on his careful
droving, and promising him a full settlement
when he turned up at that station for another lot.
“ STICKING TO
THE END
A “ breaking-in” incident.
“Moon”: Called at a N.S.W. country
groggery, the other day, and in the effort to slay
time while waiting for dinner, had a look in at the
kitchen. The Chow cook, an unmistakable con-
sumptive, was deftly erecting an apple pie. He
placed the dough covering in position, patted it
on to the dish with his paws, trimmed it with a
knife, and prettily ‘‘ patterned ” the edges with
the claw on his right thumb. Then, raising one
corner, he placed his mouth to the opening, blew
a full blast into it to make the pie swell up, quickly
glued down the edge again, and placed the horror
in the oven. I bought biscuits and cheese for
dinner, but my appetite had left. The cheese
looked too yellow.

&aid, “ you are not going to put them in it, are

and a little un to the cask an’ it’ll boite like Hell

“ Agricola ” : It is not by his tan that he
is to be recognised, nor by his felt hat, nor by his
slop clothes. There are men in Sydney —wharf-
laborers, quarrymen, railway-men, and the fruit-
growers from Parramatta, congregating about the
markets—who are as brown as Bill Back o’
Bourke. Yet they are scarcely bushwhackers,
even though they wear felt hats .all on one side;
Bill Bush doesn’t wear slop clothes any [more. He
gets them tailor-inade, and ridels 30 ! mileS to the'
tailor. Moreover, he wears thefia about two
flights a week to a “darnce,” and tennis
matches' every Sunday, and they don’t' look
as if they didn’t belong to liini. Bill Bush in
Sydney is simply a sunburnt man in Sunday
clothes on a working day, when the city sunburnt
man is wearing bowyangs. Also, he betrays an
unusual interest in the windows and sights of the
city, which have grown stale to the denizens
thereof. After a three months’ holiday in the
bush, the city man looks almost as big a Bushie
as Bushie himself, as he pursues the novelties,
*•■**■*
gG. J.” : Looking for a horse one day, on
the outskirts of the Solferino diggings, on the
head waters o,f the Clarence, I came upon a
strange sight. With his back to a rock, bolt up-
right, stood a huge old-man kangaroo, sorely
badgered by a dingo slut and two half-grown pups.
The fight had evidently raged for some time, for
all the animals showed signs of great distress.
The old dog sprang at the kangaroo’s side, but was.
met in mid-air, and flung violently back. Nothing
daunted, she rallied again, and made several
feints, while one of the pups sneaked round and
savagely attacked in the rear. The kangaroo,
desperately dashed forward, seized the other pup,
ripped him completely up, dropped the quivering
carcase, and sped straight for the gorge. Half-
way there the slut dashed in, and tore the ’roo’s
flank. The kangaroo then got clean away, and
bade fair to reach the gorge; but, alas! he
hopped straight into the sunken track, and.
the dogs were on him again, worrying him.
fearfully. Finally, he shook them off, grasped
the remaining pup, leapt down the gorge, and
dashed into a shallow water-hole. An interven-
ing log for a few minutes prevented my seeing
what took place there ; but, when they next came
into view, the pup was drowned, and the slut had
the ’roo by the throat. Still feebly fighting, the
kangaroo tried his utmost to work back into
deeper water, but in vain ; his life blood was all
but spent. Soon they staggered back to dry land,
the kangaroo fell, gave a few slight kicks, and
then lay still in death. The slut seemed nearly
done too ; her mouth was covered with foam and
flecked with blood, her sides heaved violently, and
she was scarcely able to stand. She went back
into the water for a drink, sniffed her dead pup,
howled long and dismally, and, seeing me, trotted
slowly off, stopping every now and then, looking
round, and again howling. I could easily have
shot her then, but lacked the heart to do so.

“ William Maloney ” : Have looked in
vain for mention of a “quitter” by those
authorities who occasionally air gems of current
slang in The Bulletin. The term means one
who is something between a cocktail and a turn-
coat, and who has also some of the qualities of a
traitor and a renegade. Thin as a tariff lie is the
shade of meaning that divides a “ quitter ” from a
“ shyster,” and yet it exists. The Outbacker, by
the way, who lumps a swag now briefly refers to
that encumbrance as a “ knot.” If the same in-
dividual can sport a bike, he calls it a “web,”
probably because of its spidery appearance.

“The Prooshan ” on the local Smiths : —
The Smiths are a source of worry to postal officials,
especially in country places about Christmas time, when
coaches are late and mails heavy with festive swag. I
observed a youth with a bunged eye elbow his way up to
a delivery window, recently, just at rush-time, and 23
parcels to sign for, and 13 over-weights to deal with.
“Anythin’ for Smith?” “What Smith?” “Jack
Smith.” “No.” “ Anythin’for’Enry Smith?” “No.”
“ Anythin’for old’Enry?” “Henry who?” “Smith.”
“No.'” “Anythin’for Bill Smith?” “No.” “Anythin
for W. Smith, at Sandy Gully?” “No.” “Anythin’ for
old Mrs. Smith, up Camp-street? What, ain’t there no
parcel ? By cripes ! She’ll perfume over that. Got any-
thin’ for me? What, no parcel at all? Ain’t there no
mouth orgin? Look agin. What’s that? (pointing to a
long packet in a pigeon-hole). Well that’s flamin’pure,
enny’ow. And they’re dependin’ on me for the borl at
the crick to-night, too. Mightenter shook it outer the
ban-. Well, you can tell Art. Smith, please, I got his
mare • and, say, I took her up to ’Enry Smith’s, and left
word for old Bill to get her and take her down to Bob
Smith’s, so’s he could put ’er over the river on t’ old
Smith’s till he calls for ’er. And tell ’im I got the spurs,
and I’ll leave’em down at young’Enry Smith’s for’innf
he warnts ’em.” Then he backed off, remarking “ it was
goin’ to be a pooty ’ot Crisamus.”

“Erolb”: Be “Curlew’’and his taran-
tula (B. 24/10/’O7). Have watched a good deal
of fly-hunting as practised by tarantulas (and
very pretty and effective hunting it is), but never
saw any one of these gentle creatures wait for a
fly to walk down its throat, as described. The
tarantula who followed that method would soon
become a negligible circumstance in the fly-
hunting field, owing to starvation. Here’s the
usual mode of procedure. Mr. T. meanders along
about an inch or so until Miss F. is some 9in.
distant. The T. stops and considers. Presently
he takes a casual kind of a stroll to the right (or
left), and stops again. Once more he becomes
lost iP thought. Next he permits himself a little
jaunt to inspect the scenery on the opposite side
of the lady, who all the time continues cleaning
and furbishing herself with much self-satisfaction.
T. never makes any definite move in her direction
at any time. Simply he tacks, gives a little run,
stops, then tacks and runs again. But each stop
brings him a trifle nearer his prey. He gets
within 4in. Miss F. is now eyeing him askance,
and becomes careless as to the state of her back
hair. Then whizz, biff, and the unhappy female
is gone, and Mr. T. is resting with a look of
repletion in his eye on the spot she lately in-
habited. Have never been able to see exactly
what happens in the last act of this tragedy. Does
the tarantula leap, fly, sprint, or what ? I know
not. But whatever it is he does it fast. Cer-
tainly “Curlew” is right about the tarantula’s
use as a flycatcher. By-tlie-way, has anyone ever
actually known of a genuine case of tarantula-bite
in this country ? Second-hand evidence barred.
“Franz G.” : Have noted with interest
the efforts of “Barb,” “Namoi,” and others, to
describe the bush girl. They don’t know her.
The following, by one who does, is a faithful, and
withal unbiassed, portrait of the lady as she really
is. She is young when born, and grows up
gradually, if she does not die. She is tall, short,
medium ; fat, thin, medium. Her hair is thick,
fine, thin, coarse, red, brown, yellow, and all other
shades peculiar to the female sex. In rare cases it
is piebald. She is ugly, pretty, pretty ugly, and
d—d ugly. She does wear hobnail boots; also
patent-leather shoes. She swears like a trooper,
also she blushes if one says blank in her presence.
I have met her (this was a fairly old girl) clad in a
man’s overcoat and slouch hat, an inverted clay
pipe in her mouth—from which issued dense
volumes of thick smoke—a bullock whip in her
hand, and a team of bullocks plodding steadily
along to the deep mellow rhythm of her blas-
phemy. I have also met her in her own home. I
have seen her paintings, heard her singing, also
violin and piano playing, and noted various other
refinements about her—and here % indeed, she
excels the average town girl. Taken by and
large, the main characteristic of the bush girl is
that she is just ordinary girl—unless, indeed, she
happens to be extraordinary girl. Which isn’t
often.
“Larrikin”: As a scrub exterminator,
the goat is coming into favor. Three hundred
lately passed through from Dolby to Milmerran
(Queensland), where they are going to play the
pure Angora and eat down the scrub.

“ Won Hi” writes urging that “ J. Panni-
kin,” “Edyson ” and other (as he alleges) purely
urban Abo experts be stringently dealt with :
Turn them out and let them get a real grip on bush
ite. Turn them out to observe the laughing jackass in
his loose box. Does either “Jimmie Pannikin” or
“Edyson” know for certain how many legs this creature
has ?
Turn them out and give them an opportunity of study-
ing the gentle goanna on its domestic hearth.
Turn them out so that they may listen to the voice of
the tiger snake as he swings from tree to tree, or gimbles
through the dank, dark fronds of the silverbarked billa-
bongs prior to nestling for even-song' in the fork of some
beetling anabranch. Let them hunt the slippery paddy-
melon as he crawls into his hole in the marshes. _ Better
warn them, though, to beware of the brute’s poisonous
fangs, aijd let them not approach him sideways, as many
a poor fool new-ehum has done before. Warn them to
shun the Mallee creeks, lest they get caught on the boom
of the deadly bittern, which may then be suckling its
young.
Turn them out, and let them see, mark, learn, and
subsequently disseminate the knowledge thus gained
among their Sydney and Melbourne neighbors.
Turn them out to grass somewhere for six weeks
(stamps for all exes, herewith), and may they return to
civilisation with something like a genuine knowledge of
bush life as it really is.
EASILY REMEMBERED.

take my number! "
“ Samarai ” : Bishop Stone-Wigg an-
nounced lately that, “owing to the poverty of
the soil,” Papuan natives cannot make gardens
oftener than once in 10 years on the same spot.
This is another illustration of how misguided the
good, well-meaning man is. The fact is that the
natives would rather burn off virgin forest than
keep the weeds out of cleared land. In Papua, as
elsewhere in tropical parts, the weeds are the
cultivator’s curse ; but they only grow where the
timber is cleared. After working a plot for a
year or two, the natives therefore move on, and
return when the second growth (which, by the
way, is poor stuff) has cleared the weeds. Ihed
they'burn off the new timber and use the land
again. This second growth takes, say, eight to
12 years. It isn’t, then, because Papuan land is
poor that the natives leave it for eight or 10 years ;
it is simply in order to allow the second growth
of timber to get rid of the weeds that come with
the first clearing. No doubt the bishop has had
his gaitered leg pulled by some of the older
missionaries, who are covertly opposed to white
settlement in the Possession, and try to block it
in a dozen ways.
M‘TAGGART’S MENAGERIE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
M'Taggart was a careful man,
A cautious, calculating Scot.
He ran a store at Fryingpan.
• Unfortunately, he was not
A Rechabite,
But took delight
In brimming bowl and frothing pot.
He was a dealer, bold and braw.
1 His equal was not in the town
To split a monetary straw ; ’
• He’d trade a horse to make a brown.
■ ■ And all around
There was not found
A chafferer to take him down.
When Mack was out upon a spree,
Half-measures were not brooked. Alas,
He took his pints full length, and he
; Had nips no shorter than the glass.
So, green with fright,
He saw one night
Quaint beasties skipping on the grass.
But fear not long' with Angus bode.
The morrow’s morn he took his stand
Before his barn, that faced the road—
A boy, tq represent the band.
Hit on a tin,
And blew like sin
A broken horn at his command.
“ Roll up ! Roll up ! ” M'Taggart said.
“ It is the grandest show on earth.
A shullin’ is the charge per head,
And twenty shullin’it is worth.
There’s fishes here
And birds sae queer
They’ll fill ye all wi muckle mirth.”
The people rushed to see the sight.
They sought exhibits vainly, though,
For nought was there to please or fright,
Except a barn-door hen or so.
Old Mack, in fact,
Wi’ simple tact,
Had put his jim-jams up for show !
VIC.
E. DYSON.

“ Haul fkipid : Agree with “Nulla”
(that L,e Soeuf, of Zoological Society,
ijs wrong in attributing the dying off of the koala,
to “ people killing it for fun.” The fact is there’s
no fun in killhig a bear —it’s hard work. Bear
cause of the creature’s diminution (in many locali-
ties, at any rate) is a species of tapeworm that
infests its pancreas and thereabouts. The Zoo-
O'gical people will need to get to work on those
worms right away, if they want to save our bear.

“Yarrum” : Have lived much in tick-
infested parts of N.S.W., and have always kept
valuable dogs, but never lost one with the pests.
To make the dog “proof,” give him a couple of
ticks to swallow with his food when a puppy. A
blackfellow sold me the wrinkle for a stick of
tobacco, and I would like to pass it along. Should
a dog get down with ticks, boil some bracken, and
give him a' little'df the liquid. He will be a
cured dog next morning. > ‘ ' " ‘ ' a

Certain districts in N. S. Wales likejvis,e furnish,col-
lections Of aboriginal names with similar terminal
syllables. Out Cootamundra way is Gobarralong,
Bolong, .Tugiong, Cudgigong, Cumalong, (faffing,
Binalong, Brookong, Wyalong and others. The
final “ong” in nigger dialect meant camping
ground. On the South Coast in the early days,
there were several places with names terminating
in the aboriginal word “ boola” (meaning sheetof
water). Many of these have been superseded By
foolish Anglicised appellations such as Frog’s
Hollow, Whipstick Gully, Bocky Hall, Bill’s
Garden, etc., but in corrupted form Merimbula,
Bombala, Pambula, Wolumbula and M,umbulla
still remain.

‘ ‘ Whifcepen ” discourses concerning jhis
dogl own an Anstraliap-bred bulldog of
enormous will and jaw power. He has a peculiar
and not-to-be denied liking for prop-men (men, to
wi,t, who come into your backyard ostensibly to
sell props, really to prey upon yonr property).
Not long ago a prop-man strolled into my yard,
ignoring the fact that I already had about 10
props, also a large-sized notice on the gate, and
had got half-way up the yard, when the dog
turned the corner leading from the kitchen. The
two made a fair race of it, the odds being slightly
in favor of the prop-man, who exclaimed, “Oh,
my God, what is this?” and relieved himself of
all impedimenta as he streaked for the gate. Am
sorry to say he beat the dog by the thickness of
the gate, which was slightly damaged by
the impact of the dog’s head. I remonstrated
with the man for coming into a yard without
first taking off his hat, but he replied only with
fulsome praise of the dog, arguing that he was a
truly magnificent animal, and so on,,concluding
hv asking for his props and bag, which he had
inadvertently dropped in his hurry to see what
was the matter with his horse. Unthinkingly, I
passed them over the fence to him ; and oh ! the
change in his demeanor ! It was shocking. He
said I and my dog ought to be shot; that he’d
poison the animal, also me and family, and went
on at a terrible rate. Thoroughly puzzled, I
started to open the gate, in order that the biill-
dog might have a little fresh air—a backyard being
proverbially close. The prop-man drove away in
what one might term, without undue exaggeration,
a hurry, cursing me and mine for generations
back. Such is the gratitude of a prop-man. I
still have the dog ; his affection for prop-men has
not abated ong jot or tittle.

“Brad”: Have just made a musical dis-
covery. Our native singing birds all modulate
in the minor key. The flute-like cadenza of the
magpie as he perches confidently on the high
gum tree to digest the early worm, the midnight
cry of the mopoke from an adjacent mountain
range, the weird whistle of the curlew, the crow’s
hoarse croaks as he stands exultingly upon a
loudly “humming” carcase, are all expressed in
the minor mode. Can any observer tell me of a
bush bird that affects a major ditty? I know
none.

“Edyson”: An excited boy threw him-
self off a sweating horse in front of the police
station at Toon, and rushing at constable Hickey,
who was standing at the door, superintending the
peace and weal of the township, breathlessly
requested him to saddle up and ride out to Tom
Coster’s at Corduroy, something calling for the
intervention of the law having happened between
Tom and his missus. The boy did not
know what had happened —it was
Coster himself who told him to send
Hickey out at once—but he was sure
it was serious, probably deadly, be-
cause Coster was trembling with ex-
citement, and had a mad eye. The
boy was positive about the mad eye.
It was raining, 'a bitter wind was
blowing, night was coming down, and
to Coster’s was a nine-mile ride, with
two creeks and three miles of corduroy
track by the way. But Hickey took
a serious view of his position as guar-
dian of Toon and district, and was a
whale for duty. He saddled up, took
handcuffs and revolver, and started
for Coster’s selection. He arrived
there, wet, muddy, battered, and hat-
less, having had two falls on the
corduroy, and lost his helmet in
Peach Creek. He dismounted at
Coster’s panels. The house was in
darkness. “Is that you, constable?”
cried a voice out of the gloom of the
front room. Hickey admitted his
identity. “ What’s amiss ? ” said he.
“Come in,” replied Coster, “and do
your duty ’tween me ’n’ me missus
here.” Hickey struck a light, and
discovered Coster sitting in his night-
shirt on one side of the fireless hearth.
On the other side sat Mrs. Coster,
silent, but resolute. Between them,
on the floor, lay an old pair of mole-
skin pants. “Well, well?” cried
Hickey, impatiently. “ It’s this way,
constable,” Coster explained; “I’ve
ordered my legal wedded wife t’ patch
my trousers. She sez she won’t. I
sez I won’t put on trousers till she
does. She sez I can go without and
be hanged to me. We’ve bin like this
for two days, so I sent fer you, bein’ determined
t’ have the law of it.” The swear Hickey swore
has the Toon record. It was nine miles from tip
to tip of its extended wings.

“Lah”: Be “Rob’s” par (B. 12/9/’O7).
I have recently left Westralia, where “ Bog yer
frame inter it ”is all the go. This place (South
Queensland), however, indulges in a slang all its
own. Everything is “blinded.” Was listening
to an argument the other day. One participant
was informed “ he would get a blinded punch that
would blinded well settle him if he wasn’t blinded
careful.” I blinded well left before blinded blood
was shed. The managers of tin sluicing and dredg-
ing concerns hereabouts are all called, irrespective
of size, “ the Big Feller.” A manager of one of
these concerns is regarded by the local inhabitants
(mostly cookies) as a little god, and as such is
worshipped.
The tale of Morrissey and his snakes, as
spread by himself in Melbourne Argus :
It took me four years’ solid graft to discover my anti-
dote, and I have proved its worth again and again. I
have given exhibitions for years at Colac. Yea, and at
various places in the Ballarat district. I would take
from 12 to 20 snakes into the ring, and throw them out.
The audience appointed a committee, and picked out
two of the snakes. One of these I would “milk” of its
poison, to show that the snakes were really venomous,
and I would let the other one bite me. Then I would tie
my ligature (which I have patented) above the
bits, scarify the part, and pour my antidote
into it. I have been bitten at least 40 times.
I don’t bar any kind of snakes. In fact, I prefer a tiger
snake, because he is more vicious, and you can always
make him bite. He is easier to handle, too, and feeds
better than the other kinds. You can get as much
venom from a 3ft. 6in. tiger snake as from a 6ft. 6in.
black snake, a 6ft. copperhead, or two 6ft. brown snakes.
Another thing about the tiger snake is that you may
have him about your body, and you can squeeze him, and
he will not bite. Of course, any of them will bite if you
strike them a sudden blow. A tiger snake will have his
poison sac full again 48 hours after striking, and will
have sufficient poison in 12 hours to kill a healthy man.
1 had a little tiger snake that killed a rabbit in 30 seconds,
and I reckon it takes a minute and a half for the poison to
assimilate with the human blood.
The doctors used to say to me: “Oh, Morrissey, you
are immune from the effects of snakebite,” but I asked
them what about George Underwood? George was bitten
500 times before he “ went out,” and then it was from
snakebite. Where does the immunity come in ? Shires
was another man on the same game ; he was bitten about
250 times, and then he “went out.” “Pegleg” Davis
was only bitten eight times before he died of snakebite.
Somebody “ shook ” George Underwood’s bottle of anti-
dote, and filled it up with cold tea, because they reckoned
no snake could hurt him. Well, they found out.
All these men “ went out ” because they didn’t take care
of themselves. lam the fourth man to take it on, and I
suppose I’ll go the same way if I don’t watch it. The
doctors tell pie that to do the thing scientifically, you
ought to know how long the snake was, and how many
drops of poison he has put into you. Then you regu-
late the amount of antidote to be injected. That is
very pretty; but if you are out in the bush, and
get bitten, you don’t stop to measure any snakes.
You want to get to work right away. If you have got a
bottle of my antidote you don’t want any complicated in-
structions, but just use it as I have said. An hour or
two’s bathing in hot water to reduce the inflammatory
swelling', and you are as right as if you had never been
bitten.
How did I discover it? I experimented for four years.
I had half an acre of ground near Queenstown (Tas-
mania), and I used to get snakes and goannas and blue-
tongued lizards, and make them fight. Then I watched
the lizards run and eat certain herbs after they were
bitten. I reckon I tried every blade and leaf of herbage
on that ground .before I perfected my cure. I can tell
now by the herbage of any place what snakes I will find
there. My antidote is con.pounded of two herbs, and if I
find these growing among the rocks I expect to find tigers
and copperheads, which live in rocky country. If the
herbs are growing'thickly near thestreams I expect tofind
black and brown snakes, which live mostly in the water.
Whenever I make up a fresh supply of antidote I test it
on animals to make sure it is the right strength before I
sell it to the public, as I have been doing for many .years.
I stand to fall heavily if my mixture is not all right. I
suppose it would be manslaughter. Anyway, it would be
very serious for me.
I never had any dread of snakes. I can’t understand
that. I reckon snakes are beautiful, graceful things, and
there is nothing you can’t teach a snake to do as well as
any other animal. The tiger snakes are the best. They
dance to music more than any of them ; though all
snakes are affected by music. I had a snake which, when
I tapped my foot and whistled, would go up the leg of
my trousers, come out of my shirt-collar, and drink
the milk I held for it. My snakes are pets to me.
When I carry them in the bag I feed them on milk ;
but at other times I give them birds, rats, mice,
and young, rabbits. 411 their food must be alive,
and lnustuiot be touchediivithiihe ibarids. ill don't khoiv;
how it is they don’t refuse milki for most milk has been
touched by-human chands, but j they will kill drink iti
When I am on the track J! think more about my snakes
than myself. I don’t care if I have to go short as long as
I can get some milk for the snakes.

“William Maloney”: Knew the one-
legged snake-charmer mentioned in a recent Bub-
LETIN par, and can inform his friends why he
hasn’t been around of late. He is dead. He was
running a show in Echuca when a black snake,-
which he had only captured a few days before on
the Moira Lakes, hit him. He finished his per*
formance before he applied his alleged antidote,
and passed out (either as a result of the bite or

“Mularula”: Be the dreariest town in
N.S.W., I wofild like to air my o\tn little opinioH
that neither Bathurst nor Liverpool have any
Show against Grong Grong. This city has, I sup'-
pose, a population of 1 fully 30 persons. There
are two pubs, and at one time there were three
stores; but the place could not support both
stores and pubs, so the stores had to go. The
baker also had to put up his shutters, and the
same fate overoook the meat emporium. The
church has been locked up for years, and the
police-station has that weary look that comes tc
police-stations in towns where man is too tired to
be wipked.

The Law has finally discovered that
the two-headed baby is thoroughly
dead, and that there can be no “ pro-
perty” in mere remains.
In the hope that the late little double-
header will now be
decently buried,
“Hop” designs the
above tombstone
without charge.
Now, there you see the difference. The Federal Parlia-
ment goes on piling up the taxes. Here Mr. Wade
has taken the tax off our Incomes under £lOOO a year.
That’s what I call reform. ■</
And, by-the-bye :
“ A mummy cat,
supposed to be 3500
years old, from a
tomb at Thebes, sold
for £3 3s. in a
London auction room
last month.”
The question is, if
there can be no
property in a pickled
baby, what about a
dried cat?

carriages have given excellent results, says the Norwegian

draw sleighs on Polar expeditions.”

would think might make them very undesirable as
gee-gees.
Eleven Little hatters;

Five went a-missing,
Still leaving six.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Pioneer James Fraser, who went to
Forbes —then known as Twaddles —with the first
rush, died there last week, aged 72 years.
As an example of talent running through
a family, “Kodak,” “ Bushwoman,” “ Bohemi-
enne,” and “ Larrikin,” of The Bulletin, are all
brothers and sisters. In the same regard, con-
sider the Lindsays—all artists.
Victorian Premier Bent’s neck requires a
20-inch collar, which has to be made specially for
him by a Swanston-street mercer. Mr. Bent
never could be successfully hanged. He would
slip the rope, as some pug-dogs can do their collars.
The Labor party’s presentation to Watson
on his retirement was made by Josiah Thomas.
Which was appropriate, because it was Thomas
who, though himself a Freetrader, proposed
Watson for the leadership when the party met
after the first Federal elections. Thomas and
Watson had spent six years together!*! the State
House.
Mrs. Weston, now holiday-making in Bris-
oane (Q.), shoulders a fair section of the white
woman’s burden during business hours. She’s
teacher of the provisional school at Badu Island
(Torres Straits), and all her 53 pupils are picanin-
nies. For 10 months of the year she is the only
white human being on the island.
N.S.W. Labor member Horne, of Liver-
pool Plains, was a public school teacher, and then
went on the land. He writes rather good verse,
is a well-read, thoughtful man, and showed a
useful gift of silence in the stage-thunder want-of-
eonfidence debate, in which two new Labor
members signalised themselves by talking for hours
and hours and hours, reeling off their election
campaign speeches.
It strikes the eye as being an offence
against the economic use of power when giant
“Larry” Harnett strides across the floor of
N.S.W. Assembly to remove some little tongue-
wagger of a trifle over sft. The massive Sergeant-
at-Arms, when called, shakes his ruffles, expands
his mountain-air chest, and swells generally, just
as if a real struggle for existence impended,
whereas experience, if nothing else, should
satisfy him that the objectionable tongue-wagger
does not mean tight in the slightest degree. This
is Larry’s 33rd year of office, and, only that he
takes exercise out of doors, his muscles would long
ago have disappeared in fat. Parliament doesn’t
give him any strenuous work.

MANAGER : “ Well, in the drinking scene in the second act, there's real beer.”
MUMMER: "Real beer! Ods fish! Real beer! I’m your man.”
Some memories of Victor Daley fell out of
a hollow log that formed part of a hut on Tolarno
station (Far West N.S.W.), the other day. The
hut was being pulled to pieces, when the remains
of a watch fell out of the hollow post—a presenta-
tion watch : “To Tom Mudge, from the employees
of J. Webster and Co., Melbourne, 1875.” A
year or two after that date, Mudge was found
drowned, and Victor Daley thereupon wrote
“ ’Twas all the Same to Mudge,” one of the pieces
that sent bushmen to The Bulletin for their
poetry. Apparently, Mudge left the watch in the
hut, where it first ran down and then rusted—but
Mudge didn’t mind.
Gentlemen, unlike noblemen, cannot be
created by Royal caprice.
Obit, at Glebe Point, Sydney, Bernedetto
Narea, daughter of the late John Dwyer, of
Ashby, Bungendore, and great grand-daughter of
Michael Dwyer, the Wicklow chieftain of 1798,
who died in New South Wales in 1825.
Howard Vernon and his little Vinia have
rung the curtain down on their “farming,” and
gone back to the grease-paints. There was,
after all, a good deal of comic-opera flavor about
their agriculture. True, the old Lord Chancellor
grew some very good beetroot; but there isn’t
much cheese in beetroot. So they have forsaken
their little wooden hut out Bankstown (Sydney)
way and gone to Maoriland.
“ J.A.C.” : The brief obituary notices of
the late Gerald Massey, which appeared in Syd-
ney newspapers, spoke of him as a poet, and
nothing else. He had other claims to distinction.
He was an erudite Egyptologist, and could
decipher the hieroglyphs and the cuneiform in-
scriptions with facility. I made his acquaintance
in Auckland (M.L.) some twenty years ago, when
he was delivering a course of Freethought lectures.
Apropos John Baptist (B. 7/11/’o7)> After
being a butcher in Goulburn-street, Sydney, he
went, in the late ’thirties or early ’forties, to
Surry Hills, and converted a wilderness of sand
into a smiling garden. He commenced to sell
kitchen garden stuff in the George-street markets
in the mid-’forties. Mr. Patrick Stanley, who for
half-a-century or so managed the business at the
markets and purchased it eventually, still hangs
out a shingle in George-street, Sydney.
Mrs. Anne Elizabeth Pattrick did not long
enjoy the wealth left her by her nephew, Tom
Law'ry, of Kirribilli. He directed, among other
things, that the bulk of his £42,000 should be
divided into two equal shares, one to go to his
aunt, Anne Elizabeth Pattrick, should she survive
him. Which she did—but by only two months.
She belonged to those Pattricks who, GO years
asro, were very well-known millers in Sydney.
The oldest waterman in the Commonwealth
has just shipped his oars in Hobart. Old
Charlie Dillon arrived in Tasmania in ’37, and
followed his calling until a few months ago. He
won a waterman’s badge in the second Hobart
Regatta 67 years back, and since that tune annexed
many other prizes. In the early days, when mails
were few and far between, he used to man the press-
boat, boarding vessels for latest files from abroad,
and, being a don at the oars, he usually out-rowed
his rivals. During his long service he had Ho
serious accident afloat, and closed his long innings
comfortably.
N.S.W. Government Analyst W, M.
Hamlet’s favorite diversion after a hard spell in
the laboratory is to put on his hat, and go for a
walk somewhere. It is an old habit, and he gave
way a lot to it aforetime in England, Dutch
Guiana, and other foreign places. Once he strolled
round the coast from Sydney to Melbourne ; once
he sauntered from Tumut to the Yarrangobilly
Caves, thence to Bega and along the coast to
Sydney ; and five times he mooched from Sydney
to the Jenolan Caves. At present lie is on a
ramble from Brisbane to Sydney, and expects to
sit down again about the end of this month.
Dr. James Eadie, the oldest doctor on the
Victorian register, and possibly the oldest in the
Commonwealth, died recently. Born at Glasgow
in 1819, he obtained his diploma there in 1842.
He was in practice in Glasgow for some time, and
reached Bendigo in its infancy. He was one of
the original owners of the famous Garden Gully
claim at Bendigo. He has left three sons, all
doctors, and one daughter (the wife of Geologist
Pittman, of N. S. Wales).
According to Adelaide Register, one
Thomas, of "Wallaroo (S.A.), has performed a
diving feat which would beat most Asiatic re-
cords. A loading steamer had spilled 74 ingots
of copper into deep water. At low tide (but still
nearly four fathoms) a basket was lowered.
Thomas dived without any costume, and in six
attempts retrieved 44 of the ingots—a quarter of
a ton of copper !
The Rev. Dr. Brown, who lately retired
from the general-secretaryship of the Australian
Methodist Missions, is the last of the early shift
of laborers in the South Pacific vineyard which
included the first Bishop Selwyn, Drs. Turner,
Nesbitt, Lawes, Chalmers, and others. Dr. Brown
was born in England in 1835, and was draper s
apprentice, chemist’s assistant, medical student,
sailor, and Canadian backwoodsman before he
started to battle with sin. His personal experience
of the colored brother in Samoa, New Britain,
Papua, Tonga, the Solomons, etc., extended over
something like half-a-century.

number of times he has been assailed in Parlia-

has lustilv proceeded. And the Judge goes on
