Among the recent applicants for a 15-acre
block of land in the Fryers district, near Castle-
maine (Vic.), was a tough citizen, Charles Boots,
In a dynamite explosion at the Diamond Cully
dredge, two years ago, when the manager was
killed, Boots was almost torn to pieces,_ strips of
jagged galvanised iron being driven into most
parts of his bodv. For weeks he was blind. Now
he can see a little with one eye, and an old-age-
pension keeps the remnants of his soul and body
together; but the stout-hearted Australian,
stumping round on his wooden leg, wants a few
acres in order to win himself a little comfort,
Pluck like that deserves all the luck it can get.
From a Flemingfcon observer ;
The movements of the multitude on the lawn resolved
into a sort of triumphal procession march for Mrs.
Clara Butt on Cup Day. Tt was Clara’s day out, and
although there was a whole army of Governors, their
families, and suites present, no vice-regality amounted to
a circumstance beside the towering Buttress. Gov.-Gen.
Northcote was betrayed into the error of his life
in permitting himself to be exhibited in com-
pany with the world’s most enormous contralto.
In the vice-regal box, before the eyes of an
audience of tens of thousands, staring stonily like
ruminating cattle, he looked a mere atom by the side of the
queen of song. Acting-Governor Madden, who is a much
larger, more ruddy and conspicuous person, was lost in
the'shuffle too, despite a strong professional effort to keep
in the limelight; and a whole bunch of Governors stood
round like attendant pigmies, and smiled starkly while
Clara mopped up the glory. If she had been willing to
sit, a long Governor or too might have had a look in ; but
Clara was erected to her full height all the time, and for
a solid half-hour there was nothing else in sight. She
was empress of the gala, and all the rest—horses, people,
viceroys and suites—were simply items scattered at her
f6 Out on the grass, too, when eventually Clara came down
from the pedestal and led a march past, the heaviest con-
tralto was the only buttercup. She suggested a majestic
elephant, caparisoned in gorgeous Oriental robes, leading
an insignificant circus parade. She marched with the
same solid dignity ; the same confident serenity sat upon
her broad brow ; and the little people trooped after her in
thousands, gazing upon her with just the awe the small
boy felt in the presence of the mammoth animal in all his
glory. It would he difficult to conceive a more painful
exhibition of bad manners than the clustering lawn
females were guilty of in their vulgar pursuit of the star.
But she was nothing disconcerted ; apparently she loved
it, and a beaming happiness glowed upon her large and
comely countenance the whole day long.
Papa Rumford was in attendance, but ooviously was
not in touch with the proceedings. His high, grey hell-
topper fa painful effort to live up to his lady) over-
weighted him, and he had just the dreary expression of a
hot tired, dusty man, into whose vitals red-hot corns are
being thrust by unsuitable boots. The little Rumfords
were missed. If papa had carried one, and mamma had
carried two, and the other 27 had followed, clinging to
mamma’s skirts, nobody would have troubled to look at
Apologue. I may have under-estimated the number ot
little Rumfords, but going by the photograph, I am led
to believe there are not more than 30.
Nellie Melba was at the Cup too, and she also was in
the vice-regal reserve ; hut although a markedly superior
songster, Melba is not in the same aviary with Madame
Butt, considered as a show bird. She hasn’t the same
fondness for feathers, and doesn’t seem to possess a trace or
that pleasant vanity for pretties which men so despise in
women but which is of such vast assistance to the sex in
subjugating man. Melba is apparently unconscious of
crowds, and takes her own way with cheerful superiority
to the precisions and nicenesses that fits in with her own
remark, “lam an artist, not an exhibition.

London Outlook (27/9/’O7) anenfc most
recently published European emigration returns :
For every person who leaves Protectionist Germany*
nine now leave the Freetrade United Kingdom, and for
every worker who escapes the blessings of British free-
trade, four voluntarily take upon themselves the curse
of American Protection.
The slow-going Imperial Government has
at last threatened to publish regulations govern-
ing the use of ships’ names. At present an owner
can call a vessel by any name he likes, though half
a dozen similarly named may be loose on the high
seas. A name such as “ Victoria ”is carried by a
respectable sized fleet, to the despair of shipping
agents, Customs clerks, and such like. In Aus-
tralia the conditions are worse still. Each
separate State has up to now gone its own sweet
way, and dozens of similarly named craft occur in
each one, and when they visit one another’s ports,
they worry the unoffending Customs people out of
all reason.
Spruce Myth’s admiration for the Japanese
is, apparently, concentred on theirjdefects. Their
many merits don’t appear to inspire his admira-
tion worth a copper. If he took a trip to Japan,
now, he would find the detestable doctrine ot
High Protection—which he abhors—in full swing.
He would discover that the Jap is going to make
things for himself, from ships to lager beer.
While Bruce is bubbling over with the brown and
yellow milk of human kindness, the Jap is rapidly
displacing all the white men whom he imported
to teach him modern manufacturing methods.
When the Japs decided, after deep deliberation,
that German lager beer was the safest civilised
western drink to copy, they didn’t proceed to Juy
lager from Munchen, made by German experts
and workmen. No. They just imported the ex-
perts, and got them to start making beer to teacti
the Japs. After they had got tired of building
steamers abroad, they imported Sliaw the ehiet
designer of the Fairfield yards, on the Clyde, and
he planned the Mitsu-Bishi Dock in Aagasaki.
The European captains of Jap steamers will
shortly be displaced by Japs. Japan has a citizen
soldiery, and a splendid navy—all in a few years.
By all' means, send Bruce for a trip to Japan.
He’d come back a better Australian than he is n
he has a sense of humor, which is doubtful.

Food works on your complexion—how it smooths

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Melbourne, November 10, 1907.
Mv Dear Abini,—
When I return to Sydney I shall fold my face
in my fore-paws like a ’possum, and sleep for a
week. Here in the hotel that I pervade I average
an hour a night. My bedroom (i had written for
it two months ago) is next two shower-bath
rooms, about two yards from a telephone, a few
more yards from a luggage lift, and within easy
distance of a mysterious swing-door. When
the baths are not running, the ’phone
not ringing, and the luggage lift not racket-
ing, the mysterious swing-door is banging.
It concusses with a loud concussion on an
average twice in five minutes from six in the
evening to (say) one or two in the morning. For
the unfortunate person who cannot sleep as many
seem to sleep—as if chloroformed —the accent
soon gets on to the second syllable of the in-
numerable concussions. And if there should
chance to be peace after midnight, the light
sleeper is startled into consciousness by the
piercing discordant creaking of the wheels of
the vehicle trundled by the man who collects the
boots. Whatever may be said in excuse for the
noises of the telephone, the luggage lift and the
baths, the discords of the swing door and the boot
barrow are inexcusable. Possibly a bit of india-
rubber would remedy the first and a featherful of
oil the second.
For the rest—and there is a lot of rest —the
Board of Health ought to take a look in and
get the carpets in some bedrooms and corridors
burnt. My friend and I are always conscious that
something more terrible than an army with ban-
ners may be ambushed in our carpets. Just
outside my friend’s door is an elegant thing in
the way of sinks. And I am now qualified
for Mrs. Fitzgerald’s circus through trying
to balance on the rim of the one chair in my
room. It is a battered old Austrian, with a vast
hole and a festoon of cane whiskers where the
seat should be. If we open our windows smuts
ruin our frocks; if we keep ’em shut —well, we
think of what might be in those floor coverings !
And the tariff at this hostel is about that of the
Australia !
Of the Melbourne well-known women of six
years ago, those with small features who have got
fat are spoilt, and those with clearly-marked and
longish features who have grown thinner are also
spoilt. Two well-knowners I found unchanged.
Janet Clarke is one. With her slight, erect figure
and long neck (so many women lose their necks
by sinking of the muscles), and in a gown of grey
net with silver and steel decorations, she was a
Circumstance of Cup Day. Mrs. Byron Moore is
the other unaltered Circumstance.
The arrival at Flemington on Cup Day of the
Governor-General and five State Governors
caused a heavy run on the God Savings Bank. It
is quite an undeistood fact that if two or more of
the potentates arrived together, the veil of the
grandstands would be rent. The catastrophe has
never occurred, possibly because the V.R.C.’s
marvellous secretary spaces the arrivals of the
mighty atoms so rigidly, and each gets his
separate ruffle of drums. The G.-G., so as to
have a worthy reception, comes last. His escort,
it may be mentioned, sat their horses like sacks of
meal.
The Northcotes’ luncheon-table on Cup Day
was such a dream of beauty that it deserved to be
set in a lyric. Lily of the valley in masses, in
stacks, in bowls, alternated with gentian-blue
delphiniums. That morning I had gone into a
florist’s intending to buy a lily of the valley posy
for my dearest enemy. The chemical Flora
behind the counter selected five sprigs and a leaf,
wired them as if the action were a religious rite,
and said, “Six shillings, please.” That price
would make any enemy too dear. So I didn’t
consider those lilies, but just faded out of the
shop.
Talking of flowers. Clara Butt must have
been considering the nasturtiums when she dressed
for Cup Day. An expert described her frocks to
me as “brown ninon over yellow, and then brown
chiffon—three skirts —and decorated with galons
in dull copper, orange, and gold.” A matter of
general comment was that Melba wore her hat
on top of her head, instead of hanging on her
cerebellum. Her example was followed on Oaks
Day by quite a number of persons who had
been wearing their hats behind them, well-know-
ing that their faces couldn’t stand the belated
publication.
A Cup frock that deserves a nibful of ink spent
on its description was that of Miss Robertson, of
Ballarat. She is an heiress, lately come into her
kingdom of shekels. It was a gold and ivory-
striped gauze. Mrs. Guy Sherwood’s was another
glad gown—gold Russian net over white and
pink, with gold-crocheted balls dingle-dangley
from points of vantage on the corsage. Miss
Essie Davis’ floral ninon over chiffon and glace
was decorated with the new shade of Copenhagen
blue that they are calling sacre bleu. Mrs. (Dr.)
Rose’s outfit was remarkable for the raised silk
flowers on a blue chiffon taffeta.
Their Federal Excellencies’ ball was raved about
by a group of Sydneysiders as if its brilliancy put
the stars out of business. I think the sight of the
two great singers, clad in silver and dancing in
the same orbit, dazzled the onlookers. Only
Clara doesn't dance—she flounders. Melba’s
gown was absolutely a precious metal frock. It
was real silver put through a roller and beaten
flat on to some material, probably marquisette.
Its shining tail lay around like coils of a dead]
silver serpent. But long before God Save threw
the guests out on to the St. Hilda Road, the
silver tail was torn to bits by the armed heels of
the men with whom Melba danced or who danced
around her. A Sydneysider asked a warrior who
blazed like Mamilius (you know the gentleman
who rides the dark-grey charger under the banner
of proud Tusculum through the Lays of Ancient
Rome) to “ take off his spurs.” He dropped dead.
But the insult was too great to allow him to stay
dead. He resurrected to remark that she “might
as well ask him to take off his coat.”
When on a day, the temperature falling from A
to Z, I snarled per telephone to Captain Share,
“ I hope you are ashamed of your climate,” I got
the neat backhander “Not my climate, please—
for I am interested equally in the weather of six
States. The Bulletin should arrange a Federa-
tion of Climates.”
Lord Chelmsford, who stands for Queensland,
is loved by people who love pictures. He is most
Artful, and he is trying to get up a picture show
in Brisbane. Mr. Byron Moore is organising one
for Melbourne, which will open in March. A
group of citizens have guaranteed £lBOO for
expenses, and Mr. Lake is now in London collect-
ing. They expect to get six or seven hundred fine
pictures. Among the loan pictures will be
examples of Watts, Burne-Jones, and Sergeant.
When Melbournians got to know that managing
director Marich, of Paling’s, and Mr. George
Allan, of Melbourne, were calling on Melba
sometimes twice a day, and often stopping to help
her eat her truffles and ortolans, it was realised that
the circumstantial evidence was conclusive. They
saw the trout in the milk. The star took a lot of
persuading, for her determination not to break
from play to work was sincere. She had no
advertising ammunition with her, for, when the
astute Marich asked her for photographs, she
could muster but four.
Women’s Work Ex. has settled down to a
steady, drawing institution. Every day Lady
Northcote, often wearing Australian or Mokoia
tweeds and Queensland sapphires and tourmalines,
goes to the huge exposition, and always finds
something fresh in which to be interested. On
the night of the Cup, Mrs. Deakin was nursing
an able-bodied baby in the cr&che. She had
been nursing it for some time. Its mother had
called early in the day, and left it, paying her
threepence, ostensibly so that she might look
around the Exhibition. But she went to the Cup
instead. The little monster had had three meals,
as well as the Premier’s wife’s care for threepence.
It’s a most comprehensive Exhibition. Needle-
work, a mile of it. The spirit of the nations is
in the needle-work they send. The Britisher has
sound sheep and butcher’s meat in it—mostly.
The Royalties depict what they’ve seen from the
beginning in real life, not pictures. The Wales
Princess’s fire-screen has homely hollyhocks; and
the Connaught Princess manages a mass of
lilac and peonies in a glass tumbler. Teu-
tonic work has a more solid, painstaking look
than that of the Latin women. Perhaps the
Saxon fingers are thicker. Missionaries in Aus-
tralia testify that not a single thimble sent here
by the Good Moravian Sistern in Germany is
small enough to suit the slim fingers of our Aus-
tralian blacks. Australian whites are said to
share this characteristic, along with slight wrists
and ankles.
In the Spanish needlework you’ll get the jessa-
mine and the orange flower, and a facet of church
architecture wrought on the fabric as light as
mist; or a pansy, a lover’s knot, a spider’s web, and
a scroll—they’ll put the creases in a poppy bud—
and suggest a landscape in the background, and
the article might be a pillow-slip. The Mexicans
are as artistic. The Swedish needlework makes
one recall the Norse legends.
A Bachelors’ Den is exhibited by Buckley and
Nunn. It’s a shame for any firm to try to put a
bachelor into such artistic and comfortable sur-
roundings. He’ll always live in single wickedness
instead of double blessedness.
Rather refreshing, among so many imported
things, to rejoice in the real Australian beauty of
some of the seeds, beans, and shells worked up
into trinkets by one of the trade exhibitors. Here
are Queensland firefly seeds, sometimes called
nardoo-beans, like sparks tipped with soot.
They are found throughout tropical Queens-
land, and have a reputation for curing in-
flamed eyes. Then there are sea - beans
from far Geraldton, big smooth beauties, dis-
playing the soft, graduated reds of wild cherries.
Queensland is the place for gemlike seeds and
beans. The best shells come from South Austra-
lia. The gatherer needs to be on the spot j ust at
the end of the winter rains. A pretty light
cowrie is found at Yorke’s Peninsula. The
pelican’s foot shell comes from the Barrier Reef.
Coffee shells are brought up by the W.A. pearl-
fishers. Collectors follow their quarry into their
fastnesses so as to get them while their inmates
are alive. The empty shells washed up on the
beach are “dead,” and have lost their gloss.
Sydney, November 12.
News of Carrie Moore wavers slightly. Re-
port has it that she enters into a three years’
engagement with an English firm ; while at the
same time comes the cheerful news that
“Miss Carrie Moore, under supervision of Mr.
Williamson, returns to Australia next Easter,”
Furthermore, we hear of Miss Moore in another
Eart —active life engagement. The mystery as to
ow all these things get into the papers has been
growing more and more unsolvable ever since the
days of Vincent Crummies.
The Women’s Political Educational League,
under the guidance of that gentle but most expert
general, Miss Rose Scott, has been discussing cer-
tain Parliamentary measures of special import to
women. They recognise that now is the accept-
able time, whilst the woman’s vote is fairly well
organised on a sex basis, to push hard upon the
legislators. Miss Scott directed special attention
to the Girls’ Protection Bill, which is to be a
shield to the poor creatures who are too weak,
witless, or vicious, to protect themselves. Cause
of complaint was found in the fact that the
Family Maintenance Bill, designed to give the
family a legal claim on the father’s estate, had
been shelved, and the Inebriates Act enforced to
a negligible extent only. An alcoholists’ hospital
would be necessary to effectively carry out the
provisions of this Act, and so the W.P.E.L.
passed a resolution begging for its establishment.
This Women’s Political Educational League (sober
and modest title, promising good things from the
first) is a model of what feminine electoral organi-
sations should be. It isn’t a “ class” affair or a
snob coterie. It doesn’t devote itself to slandering
Australia or abusing any party. But it does put
in good solid work advancing legislation which is
of special interest to women, and generally of
special value to the community.
At the Women’s Political Educational League,
Miss Windeyer enlarged upon existing opportuni-
ties of giving slum denizens access to beneficial
literature. On her showing, such opportunities
should be increased, since present ones are
properly appreciated, and producing desirable
effects. Here is an occasion for most elegant
philanthropy: If some worthy citizens would
offer decent prizes in the public schools to the
students who had read most deeply and under-
standingly during a year, it would be a fine incen-
tive to the slum kid to desert surreptitious “two-
up” for Shakspeare, or marbles for Keats.
And the well-gilded benefactor might also
present the nucleus of a library to the
local temple of free tuition. Lord Beauchamp,
with ex-Justice Matthew Stephen and a
Sydney journalist, instituted a reading competi-
tion in the public schools while he was here, and
it did much good. Their ignorance of good books
accounts in great measure for some peoples’
lack of imagination. Their unimaginativeness
prevents them having ideals. And an absence of
ideals produces horror and misery.
A historic inn has lately appeared in Court —
“The Black Horse,” Richmond. “The Black
Horse ” dates back to our most ancient days. It
was then recognised as the honeymoon house.
Some men, now octogenarians, spent their ’moons
there. In the old house was kept (and the present
licensee may have it) a visitors’ book in which
could be traced, as bridal pairs, many who are
now long dead, and have left large and well-
dowered families.
The Dangar-Anderson wedding, whereby two
old local families were united, produced the usual
cloud of reminiscencers—than whom there are few
more deadly social pests. On all hands one found
awful old men who remembered the bride’s grand-
mother in her prime, or got kicked in youth by a
male forbear of the groom, and they talk of these
dull circumstances as though they constituted
claims to fame. The person who remembers
unimportant things about unimportant people
who are dead and defenceless should be checked
either by an Act or an axe.
Mr. and Mrs. J. C. Watson returned to
Sydney by Saturday’s express. Major Lenehan,
Dr. and Mrs. Weston, and the Francis Foys also
got back from works or horses.
Miss Kate Rooney, the warbler from this city,
was married in London last month to a Mr.
William Kirkham. who just writes himself down
plain, unfrilled house-decorator. Miss Maud
Dalrymple, the Sydney contralto, is to be married
to Mr. Morison, a naval architect of Glasgow.
Lady Northcote will come from Melbourne
early in December to open the Thirlmere Home
for Consumptives.
The marriage of Mr. Harold Massey, son of
the organist of St. Andrew’s Cathedral, to Miss
Rosa Stuart, only daughter of Mr. Henry Stuart,
M.L.C., is to occur at the Cathedral on November
20. Bride-elect used to be a nurse at the Royal
North Shore Hospital, and is a charming girl,
rather of the American than the Australian type.
The Henniker Heatons left by the China, en
route to England, on Saturday.
Clara Butt took so keen a fancy to Clontarf
that she is going to abide a while there, with her
Kennerley and the little brood, on her return
season to Sydney.
The Amateur Gardener for November is out.
A number of soothing articles, suitable for flower-
lovers’ reading, are given, and “Among the
Workers” is a pleasant causerie column, from
which one finds out that well-known men and
women are first-class amateur growers of aquilegias
or hippeastrums, when they are popularly thought
to be only interested in stocks and frocks.
From my Brisbane correspondent:—
' invitations are out for a Chelmsford party—nature not
yet disclosed—on the 11th; after that, the summer
'flitting. Lady C., by the way, received a shock recently,
wheh a member of a Women’s Hospital Committee waited
upon her with the request that she should take charge of
a stall at a street fair which they are arranging for
April. She firmly declined—which was a pity, for the
spectacle of a vice-regal lady manning a coster barrow
or presiding over a hot-potato can at a populous corner
would have meant money.
Mrs. Gilbert Wilson, the songstress, will not, after all,
return to Queensland until after the summer months.
With a sister and the Nelson dame and her two chicks,
she will winter in Germany, and delve deeper into music.
Archbishop Donaldson, one of Brisbane’s unappro-
priated treasures, is off to England with Chaplain Batty
early in the New Year, for a holiday. Archbishoping
must be frightfully wearing ; it is only two years since
Dr. Donaldson arrived.
Yesterday Brisbane woke from eight weeks’ lethargy,
and “ celebrated ” by going in crowds to Ascot, where it
picked five winners out of six. The weather was one
cool smile, and some jaunty frocks were worn. Mrs.
Jim Clark turned some more of hubby’s pearls into a
striking arrangement of black and white chiffon, black
velvet, and point lace. Mrs. Lilley chose an oyster-
silk gauze, combined with point d'esprit and Orleans lace.
Mrs. Jim Robertson sported her favorite turquoise—in
chiffon taffeta and ecru lace. Miss Celia Cooper was in
green, with a pinkish hat. Mrs. Brenan (looking 10 years
“earlier” for her Southern frolic) and her chicks were
there. There was a dead heat for the King’s Cup, and a
record race for the tea rooms afterwards -which rooms
have, to our infinite relief, been considerably enlarged.
Pretty Kitty Morehead, whose wedding was deferred on
account of a sudden attack of the complaint which put
off King Edward’s coronation, is still an inmate of the
Victoria Hospital, but well on the way to convalescence.
From “Rosna,” in Adelaide :
The Art Exhibition was duly opened last week by his
Excellency. The chief interest in this show arises from
the fact the Board of Governors of the Adelaide Gallery
may, if it feels sufficiently amiable, spend £250 of the
Elder bequest on exhibited canvases. This year, the
Board did feel sufficiently amiable, and all the £250 goes
to the chosen few. Miss Rose McPherson’s still-life study
of onions, exhibited in the Paris Salon, was purchased ;
and the clever damsel herself was also elected to the
Council of the S. A. Society of Arts. Hans Heysen s
“Pastoral” and Will Ashton’s “Laundry Boats on the
River Seine ” were also secured. Victorian artist
Bernard Hall was the only “ foreigner ” to effect a sale,
although all the States except poor tired little Tasmania
are represented. Norman Carter (N.S.W.) shows “The
’Cellist,” and gains many laurel leaves thereby. The Ma
State also sends Burgess’ “L’Entente Cordiale,” and “ A
Thunderstorm on the Darling,” by W. O. Piguenit.
Florence Fuller, a Westralian damsel with Parisian study
behind her, exhibits “After the Bath,” a picture of a
nice, small child. Among a crowd of local exhibits there
is the usual good portraiture by the Hambidge sisters,
some of it bought for the Gallery ; and several original
pictures by Miss Bessie Davidson, whose clever “ Snake
Charmer ” ought to have been bought, but wasn’t, because
the Elder £250 isn’t made of india-rubber.
H.M.S. Psyche rushed up the mighty Port River t’other
day, tied itself to a post, and sent its warriors ashore
in search of fresh girls to conquer. At first the Peerage
put up its lorgnette, and viewed the visitors coldly,
because a previous naval consignment had pained it by
rude manners. However, so far, the Psyches haven’t
drunk out of their saucers or hailed the Peerage by its
Christian name, and so they are being offered strawberries
and cream.
But the British cricketers are the lions of the moment,
and Adelaide has been taking them by the lily-white
hand, and showing them the Zoo and multifarious other
local excitements. The first match was thronged with
the young and frilly, and it was such a good-looking
throng that we’re perfectly certain little S. Aus. will
grasp honors in The Lone Hand’s Beauty Quest.
Adelaide is making intense preparations for its Clara
Butting. The valiant person who gained the first coupon
arrived outside the booking office at 4 a.m.—a man, of
course ! Poor, long-suffering creature. Married, no
doubt.
Latest engagement is musical—Mrs. Fox (known in the
world of Noise as pianist Adelaide Porter) and baritone
Frank Robertson. Miss Porter won the Elder Scholar-
ship when a small damsel, and went to Britain to the
Royal College of Music ; but owing to an unfortunate
accident to her hand, she had to relinquish the scholar-
ship and give up the Noisy Art for a few years. She is
one of the innumerable reputed chums of Clara of the
Voice.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
At the last Methodist Conference in Mel-
bourne the “ star” piece of the Foreign Missions
meeting was the reading of a letter written by a
native South Sea teacher to prove that “ the days
of heroes in missionary work were not passed.”
The native teacher told how for three months he
tried to get a landing on the Luiniuwa group, but
was refused permission, and had to live in a small
boat on the bounding ocean all the time. “You
must know,” he said, “ there is no water on these
islands, and their only drink is from the cocoa-
nut. Once my Samoan friend and I were
seven days without a drink; then God heard
our cry and induced a man called Maloi
to swim out to us at midnight with some
nuts and pith.” It was a great story, and
the Methodist Conference said “Hallelujah”
with enthusiasm. Now a contemptuous person
writes from New Britain to The Bulletin to say
that it is perfectly true that the natives wouldn’t let
the missionaries establish a station there, “having
a most terrible fear of anything with the name of
missionary,” but that all the heroic part of the
business is vivid imagination. The yarn about
there being no water on the islands, about the
only drink being cocoanut milk, about the enforced
boat-habitation, about the seven days’ fast—all,
this contemptuous New Briton says, is vivid
imagination. The teacher “ lived ashore all the
time, enjoying the hospitality of the islanders,
except when he travelled from one island to
another,” and “you can get water anywhere on
the islands by sinking for it less than 10ft.” The
yarn that these islanders do without water, and
use cocoanut-milk instead, might have made the
Methodists a bit suspicious, but apparently they
were too anxious to say “ Hallelujah ” to notice a
little absurdity of that sort.
It is not alone the adulteration of
milk after taking it from the cow that wants
watching; there is also the adulteration of the
cow. For instance, the Sydney suburban dairy-
men allow their cows to roam about any empty
allotments that are adjacent, and here the
gentle “moo” revels amongst house refuse, old
clo’, etc., for the empty block is generally used
as a tip by the adjacent savages. Lately
the writer saw some fine dairy cows refreshing
themselves by drinking long and deeply from the
sewage which meanders across a big open space
at Rozelle Bay. The cow looks such a tender-
hearted, peaceable animal that one is surprised
at the mortality which is attributable to her
snowy product. No doubt, she would be surprised
to see the statistics herself.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 11, 1907.
My Dear Yarrik,—
Heard some say the crowd was bigger this 1907
Cup than in Mentor’s year. In every corner
it swirled and crushed. Carefully-thoughc-out and
horribly expensive dresses were torn and trodden
upon, and crushed and passed unlooked at. No room
to sweep those billows of ninon and chiffon and
si Ik ! The happiest of womenkind there were
sensible girls in short muslin frocks—cool and
comfy, and only wanting a laundrying for revival.
The two songstresses attracted more gaze than
vice-regaldom. Melba, with scarlet poppies in
her hat, came out early. Our own Melba is an
Australian, and keen on the sport, and for her it’s
no mere social gathering.
There were two most important absentees from
the Cup. Prime Minister Deakin refused to leave
his tariff and his House, and his tall, regal lady
devoted her unselfish self all day to the minding
of babies. Exhibition stands up like an Ex-
celsior banner to many fashionable dames nowa-
days. Mrs. Deakin and pretty daughter Stella,
8.5., were attached to the creche. They had a
lively time. Some 60 or 70 babies had to be
minded that hot day.
The wildest dress on the lawn on Cup Day was
worn by the handsome spouse of a wealthy book-
maker. It was as marvellous as a transformation
scene, and was built of gleaming green silk, and
trimmed with lace a foot deep, worked with gold
thread and all the known colors. On her head
was a tumble of foaming ostrich feathers, 30
guineas’ worth at least. Nothing so daring ever
ventured from a vaudeville stage into the open in
Melbourne before, and a man instinctively held
his ears as the costume drew near. None were so
deaf as those who could not hear that resounding
garment. A local journalist interviewed astern
official in front of the stand at one juncture, and
pointing to the turbulent dress said : “Will you
please ask that lady to go away ? We cannot hear
the band.”
On Cup Day, C. -J. Madden was an honored
man for some moments. He strode the lawn
between the big Butt and the queenly Melba. He
looked happy, the phenomenal Voice looked
bored, and the phenorhenal Artiste looked, to use
a classical term, rather narked. The trio, as
an ensemble, looked humorous. Even Mr. Madden’s
Irish wit and judicial intellect must have reeled
in the effort to be strictly equal in his attentions
to the famous throats, and keep both in com-
placent humor.
The Reggie Allen pair have been combining
duty and pleasure during Byron Moore’s triumph
(which is what race week amounts to). Mrs.
Allen’s delectable parents, the Charles Grubbs,
have been over from the Apple Isle for a spell,
and their affectionate offspring has danced de-
voted attendance upon them.
The Oaks had glorious, blue weather and gor-
geous dressing. The Steeplechase, on Saturday
—last day at Elemington—showed milder frocking
and keener sport among the women. The Beauty-
Anderson girls out-staged the departing Syd-
neyites, and figure-headed on the lawn. On
Saturday the graceful Miss Elsie trailed tussore,
cool and silky, while her sister clad her tall self in
ivory chiffon. Little Mrs. Broad —the youngest
Best chick—chummed all day with Dorothy-
Vane-Menzies, with Hubby Broad in close
attendance. The McEacharn’s hope piloted
the fair Beatrice Irwin, who has enjoyed
the races and attendant frivols hugely, in spite
of broken contracts and shattered nerves. She
hung many plumes over her pretty ear, and
gowned quaintly in Eastern silk that had red
in the gold. The Clarke clan bombarded an
imposing table—with the grey Janet lady at the
head and the blooming Miss Phyllis at the foot.
On Cup night, the dress-circle at the Princess’s
was treated to a farcical-comedy performance that
made amends for “Eaust,” with little Massimi in
the name part. A male intruder—who may have
been acting with humor aforethought—took his
place early in one of the seats dedicated to the
Von Pinschof party, and maintained his position
when the great Austrian potentate came and com-
manded him to get out. Whereupon the Von
cried aloud in his agony for der “ usshah,” but
that young man came not. Ironical voices echoed
theguttural yell for “ usshah,”and finally the only
Pinschof brought the doorkeeper upon the scene,
and then the usher joined in the procession, and
amidst the suppressed roars of the adjacent
audience the sacrilegious intruder was conducted
from the seats of the mighty by the hired at-
tendants, whilst Pinschof pushed and pressed
them from the rear for all the world like a snort-
ful steam-engine shoving a train up a hill. After
a lot of rustling, the lawful seat-holders settled
down to silence. But at the first interval the de-
feated invader of the Pinschof reserve took another
rise out of “ Von” by pausing in the gangway to
give him a profound obeisance before passing out to
the refreshment bar. It was the bow of a humble
penitent offering homage to one who “ wishes to
be recogdnised as der grade Padron of Moosik,”
and whose posings in public admit of no doubt
aboud his ambidtion.
Talking of the German opera, a letter from Mr.
Greder, addressed to The Bulletin, begs to inform
the public that he has had nothing to do with the
co. since July 2. And his lawyers at Wellington
(M.L.) are talking to manager Musgrove, he says,
re the contract which bound G.M. to Greder, and
vice versa, before the spirit of dissension entered
into the co. and its members started to suffer
from sudden indispositions.
The topped-notch of Cup week dances is, of
course, Guv’ment House ball on the night after
the race. The big grey house was wreathed and
lightened, and given the gay and festive air, with
some millions of electrics in daisy chains. The
cards were for 9.30, but the Northeotes like their
guests on ice, and there was the usual wait until
“God Save” announced the vice-regality. The
slow moving procession up the centre of the ball-
room was like the choristers in church (demure
and steady and lengthy), with aides and escort
in the van and rear, and a long Noah’s Ark
list of Governors and their ladies forming the
column. After one or two impressive Importants
had dared the sanctity of the blue-cushioned circle,
the high-aloft musicians suddenly took the affair
into their hands and stirred things up mightily
with some brilliant “Carmen.” There just had
to be a set of Lancers. It was the gayest thing
for an official opening set that I have ever seen.
Melba came late, after this Lancers, and sat
with a concerned face, talking to Lady North-
cote. The dear little daughter-in-law is ill—
suddenly ill—with diphtheria. She felt off-oolor
on Cup Day and did not go out—though hubby
and mamma-in-law did Elemington thoroughly.
Next day saw her in the hospital. Daughter-in-
law has hard luck ; not long ago she was prostrate
with an -itis.
During this heart-to-heart talk, the huge Clara
arrived and wandered up the ballroom. She
wandered about, like an unpiloted ship, waiting
her opportunity of the blue-carpet and the Glad
Hand, sizzling mentally, and wondering if Roy
or Toy or Joy couldn’t have the measles for “ this
one concert only.” Both the Warble ladies were
clad in silver—spread on them like armor. The
Melba gown of woven tissue of silver, like unto
chain mail, was perfect. It fitted every curve,
smoothly and plainly, and was glorified by a riot
of white roses from chin to toe in a garland. The
Colossal One had her coat of mail built up of
silver sewn-on scales, and, streaming from her
shoulders, was a gauze cloak of pink tied on with
silver tassels. It looked rather pantomimic.
There was a gorgeous second set after these
celebrities arrived. Just ordinary folk are getting
so used to bumping into Governors and such at
every turn that they took it quite calmly to watch
a set chock-full of the vice-regal species and two
celebrated divas setting-to-corners in the same
square. Clara can sing, and she can walk with an
air and sprightliness—but to dance ! Alas, the
elephantine prancing! Husband Bertie, by the
way, dances divinely, and is beamed on by the
Social maiden.
Sydney’s widower of wealth, Kelso King, was
in prideful evidence with our Miss Kirk—who
meets him at the altar this week. Count Morner
wore his palest blue trousers this time —those
with the silver stripe. He is such a gorgeously-
uniformed Count, the envy of the youth and
manly beauty of this city.
Our Miss Standish heiress, who married Mr.
Cox, of Sydney, has been trailing gorgeous gowns
throughout the festivities ; there was some fine,
old, ivory lace on her ball dress. Mrs. Raleigh
was all in pale, misty, sea colors with a
cool sea-rose in her wealth of hair, the
Raleigh tresses being smoothly coiled, in marked
contrast to the “extra curls” and the awful
“ puffs ” that every fashionable head frowses into
nowadays. Talking about hair, Lulu Armytage’s
erection sent one thinking of Japan, where the
women have their hair piled, and decorated,
and dressed once in a few weeks. They
sleep with their necks on little, round, wooden,
grooved pillows so that the work-of-art will not be
disarranged. The Armytage maiden has a traction-
engine wheel hair-dressing, and it has been
the same size and shape ever since the
Caulfield gaieties. One supposes that Miss Lulu
slumbers in the groove of a wooden pillow. Lady
Edeline Strickland wore a marvellous dress, an
Empire hung in heavy satin greeny folds with
roses painted pinkly on its surface ; then a great
curtain of lace, like a bed-spread (one of the
horrible things that have to be folded off before
one reaches cool plain sheets), hung from her
shoulders like a cloak. I think it’s a Fashion.
The Forrest Dame had drapings of a gauze of a
different color to the main part. The underneath
fitting part was delicate flesh pink and gave one
quite a flutter when the gauzy outer covering
moved too suddenly.
Leslie Madden enjoyed herself in a glorious
purply dress —shimmering over a dull chiffon and
silk. It was the color of a tropical midnight sky.
Young Mrs. Norman Fenner has been bringing
her pale fairness into all the gaieties; billowy
white was her frocking on Wednesday. Mrs.
Ernest Brookes made rather a stir (even in that
gorgeous crowd) with the lace of her frock, but
she trailed a chook from her black hair like a
red Indian’s head-gear.
Melba has been a burst of embroidered glory
during the carnival; but the sumptuousness of
her gowns has been overshadowed by her jewels,
as exquisite and glowing as the dreams of a
pretty girl. Her familiar pearls made several
appearances, and looked enviable enough. But
they were eclipsed by her diamonds. Apparently,
she possesses them in bushels. The brilliants in
their turn became almost contemptible beside her
opals. Truly, the lady of the Jewel Song shows
a certain subtle patriotism. Australian opals
have never had a more alluring advertisement
than her famous figure in a flower-like gown, with
a gleaming plastron of supernaturally-fired stones
—ear-rings and finger-rings of them ; brooches and
bracelets, too. It was a sight which might have
caused St. Cecilia to envy the opportunities of
Sardanapalus.
The Clapp sisters steam off to Europe by the
China, to recruit their strength and courage,
after an exhausting term spent in concocting
frocks for the Best People, or people who were
not so good but still could pay the Best Price.
The enterprising spinsters are loaded with weighty
commissions the fulfilment of which will be
sandwiched ’twixt the swagger social experiences
they’re expecting.
Miss Laura Winter-Irving and her sister-soul,
Bella Malcolm, bolted off on their long-projected
jaunt right in the middle of race-week. The
soulful Laura will probably pursue her piano
study in Vienna for a while. Just before she
departed she gave a recital at the University.
For once she didn’t faint at the conclusion of her
biggest effort; which was hugely disappointing to
all the folk who think that that swoon is more
artistic than her most charming music.
This season, the smartest catteries have reaped
a rich harvest of coin. The Naicest People have
deserted hotels, where the vulgar herd without
references beyond flowing cash may find stalls and
mangers, and taken refuge in the mansions where
Mrs. Todgers shows as a dignified matron who
calls herself—with some humor—a hostess.
There they have found real happiness. In
two of the most expensive establishments, the
lucky inmates have nothing but excitement, owing
to the distinctly feminine tactics of the most dis-
tinguished guests. In one case, a divorce action
is likely to eventuate ; in the other, a libel suit.
What more could any reasonable cat desire with a
fortnight’s board and lodgings ! At a pub, one
has to pay the menials to get a whisper of an
occasional scandal; under Mrs. Todgers’ auspices
one gets it gratis—and often.
The Queensland delegates to the Cats’ Confer-
ence have been throwing bouquets to the pride
of their State, Grace Watson. They gave a
reception at the Vienna in her honor, and a
trio of gushing ladies informed the gifted
spinster that the tropic State was intensely proud
of her—in fact, that she was its most enviable
achievement. All of which the remarkable Grace
took smilingly. The Queensland women had
some election scalps on show in their belts, in the
bifurcated shapes of their successful Anti-Sosh
candidates for the Federal Parliament. If the
poor dears really had any idea beyond tea-parties,
and took their patriotism more sanely, they might
have refrained from exhibiting these proofs of
their electoral incompetency.
The Suffragettes’ Society, which is a combina-
tion under the dominion of Vida Goldstein, is
giving a quaint entertainment in connection with
its campaign. Living pictures and posters
representing various phases of women’s work
will be the order of the evening. Tummy
Bent is to disfigure one tableau, the chief
object of which is to ridicule that unsymmetrical
and unsympathetic statesman. Poor Thomas!
Some women can’t forgive his want of symmetry;
others complain of his want of sympathy. The
legal status of women will be elaborately illus-
trated by tableaux of police-court scenes, and so
on. Miss Goldstein will tell suitable yarns to
explain the picturesof this Book of Revelations, and
numbers of women will turn up in their lowest-cut
evening frocks to the show, and go away with the
fixed idea that they’ve received some reliable
tuition in social and political economy. Somehow
the female mind revolts at anything that isn’t
associated with a housemaid, a tea service, or a
frock show. So it indiscriminately mixes politics
with a new petticoat, statesmen with Soochong
tea, philanthropy with philandering, and ends
up by mistaking one for t’other, or thinking them
all the same. _ Serious-minded members of the
sex are beginning to rebel against the fantastic
methods of established leaguers, and wail for some
authoritative and reliable direction. It remains
for some enterprising man to inaugurate a really
educational association for reasonable women.
The official opening of the new Dental Hospital
m Spring-street was a swaggeraffair, with a G.-G.
to turn the key, and a Lieutenant-Governor and a
gaud Mayor and a Premier and other importants
to chortle the agreeing Hear-hear. Lord North-
cote broke into quite a hilarious speech, and
mentioned the inspiring fact that he had taken
the chair at dental affairs oftener than any other.
He also got off various dental stories of such age
that they had reached a blooming second youth ;
they were received joyfully.
The ghostly and fascinating “Flying Dutch-
man,” at The Princess’s, drew a sensation-seeking
crowd on Saturday night. Melba front-rowed in the
circle. She was dressed in a marvellous gown—
the lines simple and absolutely perfect. It was
black and gold—the gold subdued and dull and
folded across the breast with a simple scarlet
hibiscus bloom, cunningly embroidered. Melba’s
son and Mr. Sam M'Culloch helped on the party.
A plaintive young tnan makes moan over the
instability of feminine beauty, the brevity of
’Enley-on-Yarra, and the maddening memories
that have possessed him for a whole week. “I
went to Henley, and there I found a lot of girls
who had strayed out of the Golden Age, and
covered up their shining little bodies with gar-
ments which seem superfluous, but, as river-
trappings, were most becoming. There was some
magic in the air that afternoon. Or rather, there
was magic in those promenading girls. (Believe
me, I had lunched very reasonably !) Oh ! but it
would have been better if many a lass had died
on the night of Henley Day, leaving behind her
only the deluding, distracting memory of her
single day’s divinity. By that murky flow of
Yarra water, girls I never could tolerate before
seemed adorable, and those I liked were worship-
ful. Never a glance nor a gesture did one make
that wasn’t in harmony with the green of the trees,
the glow of the flowers, and the clear deep color
of the sky. ... I’ve come to the conclusion that
the Australian girl is usually bizarre in an artifi-
cial setting. She must have a frame of sunlight,
or of moonshine, with the scent of clean earth as
her perfume, and no hampering suggestions from
a maternal mind warped with early Victorian
superstitions. She only wants opportunities to
be natural to be irresistible—like she was at
Henley. ”
The George Fairbairn couple pack up their
kit-bags and Saratogas, and leave for India in
December. Their first good reason for braving
an Indian Christmas is the first baby credited to
their gorgeous offspring, Mrs. Stewart Balmain.
The first day I went to the Women’s Exhibition,
the judges were deciding on the best wedding
cake, certain pastry, and the kitchen garden
variety of 41b. loaf. The common herd was
shooed away from the exhibit at 3 p.m. by a con-
stable, so that the five judges might give their
minds to the job undisturbed. The judges were
all elderly women, and, seemingly, underfed, and
blessed with neither complexions nor livers.
Waitresses dodged in and out with frequent cups
of tea, and at 5 p.m. the judges were still testing
the cakes. They didn’t look any the worse for
the ordeal—though the cakes did. They were
conscientious judges, and they didn’t intend to
miss one cake, even if they got dyspepsia for a
month as a result. A healthy small boy would
have settled the matter —and the cakes—in a
quarter the time.
In this week’s menu of Wimmen’s Exhibition
stodge, appears one bonne bouche. Miss Bella
Whitley gives a recital of chamber-music,
in conjunction with Mrs. Christeson. Mrs.
Christeson will sing ,some more familiar
songs from her delightful repertoire, and
a couple of new beauties by Sibelius and
Baclier-Lunda. By the way, there are great
difficulties with the translation of these songs.
In some cases the words are candid enough to be
dramatic —and, incidentally, shocking to the
wimmen. The cantatrice has to call in special
aid and Judkinise the verses out of Scandinavian
recognition.
The most striking of the frocks sported by
Tasmania’s Lady Edeline Strickland during the
Cup frivolities was a compliment to local
artistry and the apple of the isle. The frock was
of ninon-de-soie, with foundation of glace silk,
and Mrs. J. P. Laughton decorated it with hand-
painted apple blossoms. The artist had ex-
hibited several things which caught the favoring
eye of her Ex., and this commission followed.
Major Timothy Marcus Maclnerney reappears
over our horizon, after a lengthy absence in
S’Africa. With him he brings a charming wife,
won during his exile. The lady is English, tall
and graceful; with honey-tinted hair, eyes colored
like cornflowers, and lips that curl up at the
corners. The heroic Tim is heavier in bulk and
manner than aforetimes; which is perfectly cor-
rect (all proper-minded men get fat in happy
matrimony). Also, he is stated to be the Aus-
tralian representative of a syndicate which has
£7,000,000 to invest in ventures calculated to
grease the fatted pig; and if he really has that
amount of gold about him, he’d be very welcome
in America just now. Altogether the Major’s
two burdens, and his way of carrying ’em, quite
justify the joyous welcome he’s receiving from his
adoring family, and his envious friends.
There was a swagger invitation night on Tues-
day for the opening of the new St. Kilda rink,
which aspires to carry on the fashionable roll that
erstwhile cut the ice at the Glassy-harem. The
new Rink is granolithic below and roofed with
Heaven above, and its huge area is fenced in with
blue and white lattice. A number of festive at-
tendants are decorated in blue-and-white, or wear
the gold lace of officialdom. All very smart and
toney.
Pretty little Carmen Pinschof made the sad
mistake of developing chicken-pox just at the
grand finale of race time, and when her beloved
German opera was in full blast.
In the scramble for dates, Mrs. Ernest Brookes
grabbed Cup night for her dance, and many
stewed and race-wearied guests languidly flung
themselves along. Some keen maidens “cut”
the Cup on purpose to be fresh for the shivoo,
and they danped with energy, in spite of cool-
conservatory appeals from the universally tired
man.
A squabblesome party of severe adults gave a
quaint performance at a fashionable cafe recently.
The event was a birthday dinner, packed with
pomposity and wealth of softgoods. The company
played quite serenely and sweetly through soup,
fish, and entree, but clouds arose, and two of the
diners became so nervy that they left the festive
scene with distressing suddenness. It was an en-
forced lesson on ‘ ‘ The Importance of Keeping One’s
Mouth Shut.” The whole crowd of thirty-odd
guests had been trying to teach one big handsome
lady and her small, wealthy husband, whereupon
the offended pair declared “We won’t play in
your yard,” and departed without seeing Punch-
and-Judy or having a ride on the merry-go-round.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

SYDNEY.
POLITICAL
POINTS
Under the new mail contract, the Aus-
tralian Government of Victorian conspirators
against the peace and happiness of N. S. Wales
provides that British mails should reach Sydney
and Melbourne the same day. Hitherto the
loathsome Melbourne people have got their mails
a day earlier.
Sydney City Council has scored its first
win in a signboard case. Notice of appeal was
given, so the merits of the matter cannot be
reviewed. But if the present law isn’t strong
enough to give the Council absolute control of the
city signboards, it should be amended, and
amended again, until it does. Then, if the City
Council uses its power badly, the City Council
can be changed. But the city beautiful can never
come until there is one sure central authority in
such matters, and nothing is left to individual
taste or want of taste.
HELL BE THERE ALL RIGHT.
JONES: “ What a glorious vista! ”
VESTED INTERESTS: “Here, get off that cloud;
don't you know that's private proputty?”
Proceedings from time to time in N.S.W.
Legislative Assembly make it appear that one or
more members before the Speaker’s chair are
understood by their fellow-members to be in
possession of loaded firearms while taking part in
the debates. Is the understanding well founded ?
If there be a reasonable foundation for believing a
member loaded in the way indicated, is it not the
duty of the Speaker to take action to stop the
practice? It is stultifying the idea of a delibera-
tive Assembly for a member or members to sit
equipped with murderous weapons.
The same old business—after voting with
the Foreign-traders time after time on the apparel
duties, Labor member Poynton, of S. Aus.,
actually had the gall on the next item, “ undressed
feathers, 15 per cent.,” to declare that “as this
was a South Australian industry he would vote
with the Government.” In brief, Freetrade for
my neighbors, but Protection for myself! And
then, after he carefully refuses to swallow his own
medicine, Poynton, like Bill Wilks of the iron-
works, asks us to take him seriously when he says
it is good medicine.
Chamberlain’s Tablets will clear the sour
stomach, sweeten the breath, and create a healthy
appetite. They promote the flow of gastric juice,
thereby inducing good digestion.*
Maoriland Government sets the example
of differentiating between the pay of married and
single men doing the same work in the Civil
Service. Australia, wanting population as it does,
might reasonably follow the same plan and, so far
as it could be done without interfering with effi-
ciency, encourage matrimony and parentage.
The (London) Daily Chronicle star-heads
an anti-Australian slander :
WHERE £1 IS WORTH ONLY 15s.
The New Tariff in Australia Crushing the Workers.
The local authority for the above useful immi-
gration advertisement is the Sydney Morning
Herald.
The 400 immigrants who reached Mel-
bourne on Derby Day included a few of the sort
Australia needs most —mechanics retrenched from
Woolwich arsenal; and the shame of it is that we
have not the work to give them that they can do
best. Japan would have snapped them eagerly a
few years back ; Germany would take them to-
day ; Australia can only put them on the land—-
clever, experienced gun-builders to mind cows !
Last week, eight expert ironworkers,
known as “puddlers,” were specially imported,
under contract, from Birmingham for Lithgow by
the Sandford people. They arrived simply and
quietly, and are already puddling away. And
the papers which yelled about the exclusion of the
Six Hatters (who were not excluded) have had not
a word about the Eight Puddlers.
Westralian Parliament, moved thereto by
the sour Secesh grumblers, having forwarded
a “protest ’’against the new tariff to the
Australian Parliament, Senate President Gould
and Representatives Speaker Holder very pro-
perly refuse to receive it. “ The Standing Orders
of our respective Houses do not provide for the
placing before those bodies of any representations
otherwise than by petition, in accordance with the
method universally followed by all Parliaments
whose practice has sprung from that of Great
Britain, or by a document laid before the House
by command of the Governor-General.”
More effects of this ruinous tariff. Trinity
Inlet, where the ships at Cairns (N.Q.) load and
unload, looks like a Canadian River : hundreds of
logs, chained together in rafts, float near the
wharves. An increased demand for this timber
for furniture-making has sprung up; and the
coastal vessels, full up with sugar just now, can’t
get the bulkier cargo away quickly enough. The
logs afloat include silky oak, cedar, silkwood,
and others. The mournful thing is that the tariff
didn’t arrive till so much of the North Queens-
land forests got the firestick. But there is still a
fine heritage left.
The “voluntary liquidations” list just
records another triumph of glorious Freetrade in
Britain. The manufacture of vulcanite goods
used to be a biggish Scotch industry ; but Ger-
many got on to the line, and then a fight began.
German manufacturers “dumped” goods into
Scotland, but Scotland, in spite of its thistle and
its motto, couldn’t hit back, because the German
tariff was in the way ; so the Scottish Vulcanite
Co. —the last surviving manufacturer—puts up
the shutters, goes into liquidation : and Edinbro’
has to whistle for its £70,800 of capital. And it’s
a dour sight to see a Scotch town bereft of that
much capital.
Bananalanders claim that the work on the
Cloncurr.y railway line forms an Australian record.
One week 4i miles were laid.
The whole of the work has been
carried out by day labor, under
the direction of Engineer Munro,
who has treated the men much
more consideratelv, and housed
them better, than^frivate Enter-
prise would have dreamed of.
Munro, from his office at Rich-
mond, regulated the sleeper-
getters at Cardwell, on the coast,
ordered the bridge timber, super-
intended constructional work,
provided first-rate accommoda-
tion for his army of workers,
opened some time ago 80 miles of
line for customers, and generally
bossed the show. He proved
there was no need to send abroad
for a Johnson. Under the con-
tract system the work would
likely have cost thousands more,
and there would not have been the
hustle which Munro has organised.
A cable from Sydney, published in
London Times on October 4 :
It has now been ascertained that Mr. Carntthers’s
resignation was largely due to the probability that the
State Assembly would have carried a vote of censure on
his action in seizing the wire-netting consigned to the
State Government if he had remained Premier.
S. M. Herald, in its issue of November 7,
undertakes to reprobate the cable as a misleading
report. But everybody in Sydney who knows
anything knows it to be true.
One of the “hottest ’’Labor members
from Wayback (N.S.W.) was entertained at a
smoke social the other evening. The entertain-
ment consisted of beer, speech and song, and one
of the speeches was a sermon against colored
labor so blistering that it boiled what little water
there is left in the Murrumbidgee. And when he
had sat down, the chairman called on Mr. Sing
Gee to favor them with another selection, where-
upon Mr. Sing Gee (who had already obliged
with “Sweet Sixteen”) sang “Believe in Me.”
There’s a tremendous lot of make-believe about
some politicians, even of the P.L.L. brand.
From an Australian in London :
The anti-Australian tariff meeting at the London
Chamber of Commerce consisted of a few unimportant
people who act here for small Australian indentors in
Sydney and Melbourne. These people quite naturally
kick at the tariff, but the serious opposition will probably
come from Germany. That country firmly believes it is
going to be the great industrial hub of the future, and,
not being much good at colonisation itself, it wants
Britain to do the colonising, while it gets the benefits. It
is keenly interested, therefore, in blocking high tariffs in
new countries, and it will pull all the strings it knows in
Australia while the tariff debate is in progress.

Every officer goes through the ranks and rises to his
commission through the various subordinate grades.
Nothing' “comes” to the Swiss soldier; however dis-
tinguished his connections or irreproachable his character
he can work his way upward by ability only.

What humorist designed the three-globe
ornamental lamp-posts in front of Melbourne
House of Parliament? In the old State days,
when the members sat inside wrangling
wearily about the possibility—or impossibility
—of floating more loans, the pawnbroker’s
sign was blazing gaily away in front, pro-
claiming to the multitude that the House of
Impecuniosity was busy writing out its I OU.
Nowadays the Australian Parliament deli-
berates behind the three-ball illuminations,
and, as the Australian Parliament doesn’t
borrow', the joke isn’t as much of a joke as
it used to be.

comes to this office a marked copy of

the sentence “ What we fought for.” 1
However, this particular S’African,
in his anti-Semite fury, forgets the
Chows. He and his friends fought
just as much to get work for the Chow
coolies as to get profit for the Tribes.
In an affair of this kind accuracy is
everything.
“ J. Cobden-Bright ” writes :
What is the use of The Bulletin (which must
be in collusion with Victorian manufacturers)
trying to prov e that Protection does hot raise
prices? Consider this case. Last week I was
hunting for lodgings up Potts Point (Sydney)
way, and was shown over a large house by a
stately landlady, with a subdued and superior
voice. Last came the bathroom, which con-
tained a gas-heater. After pointing this out to
me, she said : “Of course, since the tariff, everything
is so dear that I charge M. for a hot bath.” “But,” I
protested, “ there is no duty on gas." She drew herself
up to her full height, and a look of mingled scorn
and pity came into her eyes, as she said “ Ohiio, not on
.gas, but there is on coal.” I said “Oh'Uand that I’d
call in later and let her know whether I was coming.

The plentiful lack of wisdom shown by
those precious anti-Sosh women who met at Janet

Bathurst (N.S.W.) has bumped trouble
over the new Local Government Act. A man
was summoned for allowing four horses to “stray”
in the public street. He successfully took the
point that his gees weren’t straying. They were
being fed. They were eating the grass "in the
streets, and were in charge of a boy, who took
jolly good care they didn’t stray. It appears that
the words “ depasturing and tethering ” dropped
somehow through a hole in the new Act, and the
point was fatal to the prosecution.
When Captain Richard, of Mt. Morgan,
was in America, the other week, he found that
Queensland copper ore was being smelted there
with N. S. Wales coke. First the ore was humped
eight or ten thousand miles, and then the coke
was humped the same eight or ten thousand
miles, and the ore and the coke were then emptied
into a smelting pot to get out the dirt that Aus-
tralia apparently hasn’t got sense enough to get
out itself. And when Richard’s co., seeing the
absurdity of this, proposed to establish smelting
and refining works at Port Kembla, near the
coke works, the N. S. Wales State Government
promptly held out a bill which meant that the
co. would have to pay 7 per cent, of its probable
revenue in wharfage and tonnage and other port
dues.
S. M. Herald is weeping at the action of
the Melbourne Customs in seizing, under section
161 of the Customs Act, a consignment of
Japanese notions, on the ground that they are in-
voiced much below their real value. Acting under
the powers of the section, the Government has
appropriated,the shipment, paying the importers
the full invoice value plus 10 per cent. This pro-
cedure of the Customs is, says Granny Herald, a
wicked and unjust power to exercise. The
Herald pictures its friend the importer, by a
combination of brains and opportunity, added to
keen business instinct, getting possession of goods
at far below their genuine value, and being penalised
for his smartness. The Herald doesn’t see that
the value of the £lOO worth of goods is still £lOO,
even if the importer gave £5O only for them.
According to her logic, if the importing fiend
knocked the owner down, and skipped to Aus-
tralia with the goods, he would have the right to
import them duty free.
Apropos the Victorian Agency-General.
When Taverner’s appointment was mooted, Bent
in his public speeches protested that Taverner
was not being sent as Agent-General, because for
some years the Vic. press and the public (includ-
ing the Reform League) had asked that the State
Agent-Generalship should be abolished. Taverner
also, in interviews in the press, protested that he
was not taking the Agent-Generalship, but was
simply going to London to arrange for the send-
ing out to Victoria of thousands of British farmers.
Now, in the Estimates appear the items, “Agent-
General, expenses of staff and office, £3600,” and
“Allowance for expenses to the Hon, J. W.
Taverner, Agent-General, £1500.” And there
is a hint that if Bent finds Victoria too Warm
for him he will become Victorian Agent-General
himself.

The nastiest jolt on the solar plexus of the
soulful Beu Tillett was swung by Billy Hughes,
M.H.R. In the middle of Tillett’s wild denunci-
ations of all things Australian, just as he seemed
about to make the welkin ring with the odor of
the decayed fish he waved at the end of a bit of
oratorical string, Hughes came along to inquire
quietly whether the imported person in the velvet
coat regarded the condition of Australia as much
worse than it was four years ago ; and, if not, how
he could explain that at that period he accepted a
paid position under Q. Agent-General Tozer to
stump the United Kingdom as immigration agent,
telling everybody what a splendid place Australia
was to go to. Tillett has not yet replied to
Hughes, Perhaps he is not able to do so, Or he
may have left the country, for, nowadays, Tillett
attracts so little attention that nobody knows
whether he is here or not.
N.S.W. Auditor-General has been having
a dig at the Public Service Stores Supply and
Tender Board. A few years back the attempt
was made to check extravagance in the
Government Departments by forming a new
Department to grapple with supplies to all the
others. The idea was good, but too much was
attempted. Had the Tender Board stuck to
regulating the supply of main lines, such as
stationery, used by everybody, it would have been
all right, but they grabbed everything. If a
lonely lightkeeper wants a wick for his lamp,
or a botanic gardener wants a pair of rose
scissors, or the man on a country dredge or a
powder magazine wants some specialised article,
he must apply to the same Johnny at the Tender
Board, and if he doesn’t apply on the right colored
form, he doesn’t get what he wants. Result is a
chronic state of curse. The Auditor-General
sums up his objections in the fact that the salaries
paid to the staff of the Board now reach £7OOO a
year. Also as the whole public service is con-
cerned not in saving their stores, but in trying to
see how much they can “ get out of” the Board,
the result is more expensive.
Although the Wade Ministry will not
have been in office three months, by the evening
of November 26 it will have two hangings on its
conscience—-both legacies of Joseph Carruthers.
By this time Wade should be about beginning to
realise that Joseph knew something when he
moved out. He had won an election on an almost
impossible programme for his party to follow
on account of its extreme Socialism. Benefit so-
cieties were to be spoon-fed, and taxation was to
be remitted wholesale in the face of a warning
from the Commonwealth Treasurer that the pay-
ments to the States would probably begin to
diminish seriously, owing to the Protective cha-
racter of the new Tariff. Also he had started the
wire-netting insurrection. Joe had in a general
way got himself into the position of the small boy
who had taken the clock to pieces, and couldn’t
put it together again.
CRUMBS!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
_ Mr. Spence, M.H.R., was much applauded when say-
fing that Australia was share-owner with England of the
[lmperial Navy.—Melbourne Herald.
The troops at home are available in case of need,
'and, after due provision has beenmade for our own safety,
'for colonial purposes.—Lubbock, English M.P.
The red marine, and the boy-in-blue, with 'his heaving
deck at foot;
The gilt commander, tired ’n’ true, and'the dtoker, striped
with soot;
The guns that threaten fore and sfit, and the salted
breakfast-horse,
The cloying incenses that waft [from dinners, brief and
coarse ;
The raw deck-hand, with'h'is discipline of the slum and
the barrack-square,
And the orf’cer bloke with the lovely skin and the perfume
in his hair:;
The flag aloft, and the weekly wash that flaps upon the
line—
This is 'the BRITISH navy? Bosh! It’s (more or less)
you re and mine !
And this is the way we’ll hold our lands immune from the
dlieh’s touch—
<©ur ‘noble navy’s great, big hands, with black death in
their clutch,
Will spread red gore on the Sydney shore (if they come
on the scene in time—
BJ they come on the scene with a score or more of gun-
boats in their prime).
And it’s not for us to worry about our empty land, nor
fret
For the navy we ought to be turning out, and the people
we fail to get ;
It’s ours to wait on Bull and bide his pleasure, and take
what comes ;
When John Bull’s safe and satisfied, at least we’ll get the
crumbs.

A CIVIC OFFICER
Fortifies his System Against Prague and
Disease by using Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills.
Mr. Samuel Williams, who has been engaged
for the past three years by the Municipal Board
of Health in connection with the cleansing of the
infected areas of Sydney, states that he takes Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills regularly, and thus
keeps his blood pure and his system in good
health. He writes: —“9 Wemyss Street, Sydney.
—For over 2| years I have been employed by the
City Council in connection with the Sanitary
Mr. Samuel Williams.
(From a photo.)
Department, cleansing
the city and wharves in
the infected areas. Al-
though I have been in-
oculated, I have always
found that the best
preventative against dis-
ease and common ail-
ments is to keep the
body well, and in this
respect there is no better
remedy than Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills. My
wife and family use
them occasionally, and
while we have them in
the house we never re-
quire the doctor or any
other medicine. You
have my permission to
use this, together with my photo.” Cleanliness is
next to godliness, but it is not alone necessary to
keep the outside clean ; the interior must also be
kept in a sanitary condition. Many a tenement
in Sydney from all outside appearance looks
clean and healthy, but a coat of paint on the out-
side does not prevent the germs of disease and
pestilence breeding in the dirty, unwholesome
inside. So with the human frame. The microbes
enter the system, and if the bowels are constipated
and unhealthy, and the blood impure, there it
finds a veritable harbor to germinate and gather
in another victim. Why is it that medical men
so seldom fall victims to disease? Well, the
reason is they consult themselves, and knowing
that the true secret of health is the regularity of
the system and the maintenance of unvitiated
blood, they take every precaution to this end.
Thousands of men and women have learnt this
secret, and like our correspondent (Mr. Williams)
use Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and fear
neither disease nor any of the common ailments
to which mankind is heir. Be your own doctor.
Learn that a pure and regular system is the only
proof against disease and ill-health. Experience
has taught many this lesson. You may learn by
their experience. Ask your friends, neighbors,
and relatives what Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
have done for them.
