SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Westralian match was not taken seriously,
and the real interest in the English cricket invasion
commenced on Saturday last when the team attacked S.
Australia. The result was a slight slump in the estimate
of the English bowling, Clem Hill making 104, and
Jennings and Claxton notching 79 and 57 respectively.
The fact that a South Australian team could run up 343
before the last wicket fell, indicated that the bowling
was not up to reputation. On the other hand, when the
Englishmen got to the sticks they proceeded to astonish
the natives, 233 being scored for the loss of four
wickets, A. O. Jones making 119. As revealed by Adelaide
doings, the Englishmen appear to be somewhat nervous
and over-cautious. English papers have published dread-
ful accounts of Australian audiences bombarding the
players with bottles, and making footballs of the bowlers
who take the local wickets too fast. For which we can
thank the vivid imagination of Mr. Plum Warner. As
these cricketers mingle with the people and prove by
experience that we do not bite at sight, this feeling will
probably wear off, and then we shall see the new team at
its best.
4 * * *
What the dailies described as a magnificent dis-
play of batting took place, at Sydney on Monday last in
the match between the N.S.W. First XI. and the Next
XIII. The Devil’s number went to the sticks first,
closing their innings with nine wickets down for 361. It
seemed reasonably safe till Trumper got going
with splendid effect, and Duff, Noble, E. F.
Waddy, and Cotter backed him up to such pur-
pose that the XI. scored 336 for the fall of
four wickets; so the game ended in a draw.
All this proves that there are some splendid batsmen in
N.S.W. ; but does it not also suggest that the State XI.
is badly in want of some bowlers who can go out to kill
occasionally? Among the First XI., Cotter was easily
the best bowler, and yet he only took three wickets for
106 runs. Among the XIII., 'Hickson was the star
trundler—and he got one wicket (Noble’s) at a cost of 25
runs.
♦ * * *
“Adelaide”: A quiet look at the English
cricket team effectually disposes of that odd, much-
boomed, idea that they are a crowd of “youngsters.”
True, there isn’t a Lilley or a Jackson or a Darling among
them, but a cricketer is hardly a kid at 30, and seven of
them are over that, with two more near it. In fact,
there are only three under 25, and (though Young
is still a Cambridge undergraduate) Crawford is
the only one under 21. (He will come of age next
month.) The captain is the senior of them all at 34
which he doesn’t look. Mrs. Jones is with him.
Major Trevor, the business manager, is short and sturdy,
very funny in private life, very serious in public—he has
just published a heavy book on “The Problems of
Cricket,” which reserves a special Hell for the statistical,
average-booming journalist. He is probably the best
speaker that has ever returned thanks for a British team.
The co. brought heavy rain with it, and had to practise on a
wicket quite English. It looks (but you never can tell) as
if Blythe would be more dangerous than Rhodes, Fielder
more useful than the last time he was here, and Crawford,
the only new bowler, possibly the most effective of the lot.
Barnes has certainly shed a good deal of his pace ; he is,
by the way, notably the most burly of the crowd, who do
not run much to weight; the smallest of them is the
nuggety Hardstaff. With the bat, the only one with
marked defensive tactics is Hobbs, who should take a lot
of shifting. Two fierce hitters, and therefore likely to be
popular, are Hayes, who has a rather long black
moustache, and looks like anything you please rather than
a professionaj cricketer ; and Hutchings, who is powerful
though not tall, and very good looking. Crawford and
Hardstaff also hit hard. In fact, the team is going
to be a good one to watch, whether at the wickets, or
saving runs in the field, where its collective activity is
remarkable. The early stage of the S.A. match does not
say much for the bowling. On a good wicket the men
who send up the slow tricky stuff seem most likely to be
effective. As an early shot, the writer places the team’s
qualities thus: Fielding 1, Batting 2, Bowling 3.
* * * *
The Melbourne Cricket Club’s attempted grab
at the largest slice of the cake represented by the
“ gates ” of the forthcoming English matches, has moved
the trustees of the ground to take more interest in their
trust than hitherto, and, after inspecting the gorgeous
new members’ stand and the new scoring-board facing it,
they said things to the club—about the three lone water-
taps where the drouthy small-boy lines-up and awaits his
turn ; about the hard, scrubby, backless seats ; and about
the shelter shed, large enough for perhaps a tenth of the
spectators. The club considers these interfering trustees
a nuisance, hut the only answer the trustees make is that,
as the ground is a public one, the public has got to be
considered, whether it suits the club or not. So the
M. 0.0. is “ formulating a scheme for new and better
seats and more shelter,” and the trustees would do a good
turn to the public by seeing that the extra accommoda-
tion arrives before the test matches.
School boy E. P. Barbour continues in fine
form, and put up a taking performance against University
(one of the first-grade teams) on Monday. In University’s
total of 248 runs, Barbour took six wickets for 86 runs in
15 overs ; and for the Combined Schools scored 37 runs
This youngster should be given a show for the State team'
Country cricketer E. Barnes was responsible for
a fine performance at Meadow Creek, near Orange
(N.S.W.), the other day. Barnes took six wickets for 12
runs ; and afterwards scored 251 in a total of 423 for
eight wickets. In one over Barnes hit all six balls over
the boundary for six each.
*' » * *
The Australian Cricket Board of Control is fully
alive to its responsibilities, and Captain A. O. Jones and
the M.C.C. team had hardly arrived in South Australia
when Joe Darling (on the Board’s behalf) confronted
them, and fixed up an agreement that the captains in
each match shall exchange lists of the teams before the
tossing of the coin. This step is necessary to prevent the
ringing-in of players under altered weather conditions.
* * * *
Poseidon’s utter failure in the Melbourne Cup
must have been due to something more than the fast
pace. He is not “another Carbine,” but he can carry
weight and run brilliantly up to 32 furlongs, and on his
Melbourne Stakes form it was odds on his beating Tartan
(at a difference of 81b.) in the Cup, if he were as good
a stayer. Probably he was off color on Cup Day. Or,
which is just as likely, he is not a genuine stayer. Last
year he had only the 3-year-old weight of 7st. 61b., and it
didn’t stop him. But he has not grown in the meantime,
and if 2 miles was as far as he cared to travel twelve
months ago, it is too far for him to carry a Carbine
weight to-day. Of course, he wasn’t ridden out to the
bitter end. Probably Tartan—who had been backed to
beat him in their places —was ridden out for the other-
wise barren honor of 4th position—nearly a dozen
lengths behind the dead-beaten Mountain King.
The alleged “popular ” character of Apologue’s
victory was not expressed in tremendous cheering from
the multitude, anyhow. No favorite for the Cup could
have been less warmly received on returning to scale.
The crowd’s first feeling was surprise at’- the appearance
of Apologue twenty lengths ahead of the top weight, and
then came the general remark that the Caulfield Cup
ought to have been a gift for the Maorilander.
To the very last moment before the race the
relative chances of Apologue and Poseidon for the Mel-
bourne Cup were discussed as though their “ form ” in
the Caulfield Cup could be accepted as true. Even book-
makers and other followers of the game gave heed to the
persistent allegations of Poseidon’s excellence. Now
that the champion’s victory at Caultield is proved—as far
as it is capable of proof—to have been due to
the unsatisfactory manner in which Apologue was
ridden, one can confidently say that the subse-
quent booming of the Caulfield hero was only
what might have been expected. In the light
of the Melbourne Cup result two things are clear enough.
Firstly, that the public were right in finding fault with
Coffey’s riding- of Apologue at Caulfield, in so far as he
never brought his mount near to the front until its
speedy stable companion was in a fair way to win. Bad
luck, or bad judgment, could have been pleaded in excuse
for the late coming of Apologue.;.but, as a matter of
fact, no excuse was given, and a dissatisfied multi-
tude naturally looked for an explanation in the
fact that a lot of stable money was on
Poseidon for the race, and on Poseidon and
Apologue for the “double,” whereas the owners of the
light-weighted horse from Maoriland hoped to win the
first Cup anyhow, and the second one as well if they
could. In short, the performance of Apologue at Caul-
field suited the stable backers of Poseidon, but the owners
of Apologue were dissatisfied. Another jockey—Evans—
was substituted for Coffey at Flemington, and the horse
wasjridden in accordance with his light weight. At Caul-
field the lightweight had been never in front of Poseidon.
At Flemington he was always in front of his stable com-
panion, and before a mile had been covered he was one of
the leading six.
What is there in the foregoing to explain the
earnest “ booming ” of Poseidon during the fortnight
interval between the two Cups? The stable connections
who had won their Poseidon money would naturally
sing the praises of that gallant quadruped. They were
like the V.A.T.C. stewards who saw no reason for
questioning the running of Apologue at Caulfield.
Everything in the garden was lovely for them. As
for the actual owner of Poseidon, there was probably
only one horse in the world that he could
fancy for the Cup, and the more he heard
of comment on the Caulfield running the more
he ‘persuaded himself that the public knew nothing
about the matter. He thought the defeat of Apologue
would be confirmed at Flemington, and the rest of the
stable (apart from the purely Apologue party) supported
this belief in a loud voice, whilst quietly recommending
their friends to back Apologue. And so the public who,
on the evidence of their own eyes, had asserted that
Apologue should have been his stable companion at Caul-
field, came round to the opinion that Poseidon might
possibly beat him again, giving him a lot more weight
than before. There was a combination of reasons why
Poseidon’s chance should be cracked-up by the people
best able to judge Apologue’s chance. Their pride in the
horse’s record inclined them to exaggerate their estimate
of his powers. They couldn’t admit any fault in the
Caulfield performance of Apologue without casting a
reflection on the jockeyship, and permitting a suspicion of
money influences to rest upon it. And having convinced
themselves, more or less, that Poseidon won the Caulfield
Cup on his merits as regards his stable companion, and was
improving every day, they were bound to think him
likely to carry his penalty successfully in the Melbourne
Cup.

♦ * * *
“0.K.” : The Cup result this year was an ex-
ceptional triumph for latest “ form,” which is always
something of a solid fact where the best horses are going
for big stakes, though a delusion and a snare when
second-raters are concerned in petty events. The three
placed horses in the Cup represented recent “form” as
follows : Ist and 3rd in the Spring Handicap, and Ist in
Rawson Stakes (Sydney Tatt.’s September meeting),
2nd in the Spring Stakes, Ist and 2nd in the Metro-
politan, 2nd in Epsom Handicap, and Ist in three
w.f.a., including the Derby (A.J.C. October meeting),
Ist in Coongy Handicap, and 2ndinCaulfieldCup(V.A.T.C.
October meeting), Ist in Derby and 2nd in Melb. Stakes
(V.R.O. November meeting). Previous to their filling the
places in the big handicap, the three horses had col-
lectively made fourteen appearances this season, and were
placed every time. The book showed 8 wins, 5 seconds,
and 1 third to their credit. Only three other starters for
the Melbourne Cup— viz., Melodrama, Poseidon and
Welcome Trist— had any recent “form” worth con-
sidering, yet there were punters by the hundred
who took comparative!}' short odds about sundry hopeless
chances in the wild belief that something unexpected
would turn up. There are so many unexpected things
to pick from every time, and not once in ten years does
one of them turn up. Within the last eight years two
Cup winners—Clean Sweep and The Victory—have
started at 20 to 1, or longer odds, but in both cases the
winners had recent “ form,” which should have brought
them to a shorter price. And against those two under-
rated outsiders in eight years, there is a list of fully 60
failures that were backed for a lot of money at the last
minute, on no “ form ”at all. Punters never tire of try-
ing to find amidst a large field of horses the “ unex-
pected ” winner which so rarely arrives.

After a long spell of bad luck, the Ballarat
trainer, Scobie, has got hold of a valuable trio of two-year-
olds in Mother Goose, Cleis and Kuban. It looks as
though “ Scobie’s best ” is once again going to be a
popular candidate in classic events, provided that he gets
out of the way of winning with the wrong one.

* * » *
Crouch, M.H.R., pressed the Postmaster-
General in Parliament for a brief statement as to why the
name of Wren remained on the black list after the name of
Oxenham had been struck off it. “ Mr. Wren had distinctly
and emphatically denied that he had used postal
facilities for illegal purposes, and Mr. Crouch desired
to know whether the department had definite in-
formation to the contrary.” The P.M.G. replied
“ Unquestionably,” whereupon Crouch subsided. Wren’s
policy, throughout his long career of law-breaking,
has been bluff, supported by allegations of “sectarian-
ism,” or “personal spite,” or “political bias,”
against anyone who treated him as a Public
Evil. The P.M.G.’s tampering with the mails in
individual cases is a hateful interference, which should
be carried to a logical conclusion. The idea of one postal
law for Tattersall and another law for Wren or any other
bookmaker is intolerable.
* * * *
James Aldridge, of Adelaide Gresham Hotel,
has been burnt out in his private house and stud farm at
Richmond Park, just outside the city. The stallion Pistol
was located within 20 yards of the blaze, hut escaped
unhurt, as did all the other live stock. The house went
in a few minutes, paintings, stud-books, and all.
* * * *
The victory of good old Proceeder in the
V.R.O. Handicap offered the multitude a chance to show
their sporting sentiment, if they had any. Apparently,
the multitude’s regards for that honest veteran did not
amount to much, It was practically a vveight-for-age
race as far as Proceeder was concerned— i.e., the handi-
eapper had brought him down toBst. lib. in consideration
of his years. This is Proceeder’s tenth year. In last
Sandhurst Cup he carried 9st. 71b., and finished third to
Tulkeroo (Bst. 121 b.) and Mira (7st. 71b.). The weights for
the V.R.C. Handicap were Tulkeroo (Bst. 81b.), Proceeder
(Bst. lib.), and Mira (7st. 111 b.), and the old horse being
pretty nearly as good to-day as when he ran Tartan to a
head for the Champion Stakes, made no trouble of out-
staying his old opponents over the tiring course. The
fact that he is a gelding with iron legs explains why
Proceeder has been kept at the racing game all this time.
A buggy horse before and after his dbbut as a racer, he
started his record at four years old by winning a Hack
Race at a small up-country meeting in Vic., and he was a
six-yearrold ere he appeared at an important course.
Since then he has been a consistent performer in
respectable company. His list of battles as an aged
animal covers more than a page in Miller’s Sporting
Pamphlet. If the w.f.a. scale made a concession, as it
should, for every year over seven, this remarkable son of
Off-Color and Progression would be a very valuable
property to his owner. As it is he can only manage to
score in a long-distance handicap at a metropolitan meet-
ing when the weight is not too heavy.
* * * *
The title of “champion” will presumably be
shifted from Poseidon to Mountain King, now that the
stalwart youngster has decisively licked the 4-year-old in
a 12-furlong- race at w.f.a. The C. B. Fisher Plate at
Flemington was the third w.f.a. and the longest wherein
the “King” has beaten Poseidon, and the seventh in
succession which he has won—not reckoning the Mel-
bourne Cup handicap. He has appeared to be close
pressed on two or three occasions, but the nags that
finished second have subsequently suggested that the
colossal colt was only pulling their legs.
» * * *
•Clarence Weber’s retirement from wrestling
left an opening for another "championship” goat the
Melbourne Athletic Club, on Friday night last. Gus
Rennert, the ponderous Sydney German ’rastler, who
made some reputation in the catch-hold style on the
exhibition ticket with the Rev. Carkeek and the mighty
Hackenschmidt, was out for keeps against the wily
Hindoo, Buttan Singh. Rennert’s display gave evidence
that an exhibitionist’s knowledge of the game is useless
against a man of Buttan’s skill in a fair-dinkum
go. Gus, with 14st. 61b., made the brown man look
weedy, though his glistening skin covered a fibrous
collection of muscle. On getting to holds Rennert pulled
his man to the mat in businesslike style and showed a
winning vein, but as the minutes drifted by he appeared
to lose all resource—if he ever had any. Facing each
other on the mat in the squatting position, Rennert was
after a barNelson,when Singh, taking a quiet nap, suddenly
woke up and upset the big fellow’s equilibrium with a
clever leg hold. It was a clear case of pulling the base
away, whereupon the mass above hit the floor with two
shoulders—a clean fall, in dmin. llsec. After an eight-
minutes’ spell, the men resumed the struggle—Rennert
cautious, the Hindu a trifle cocky. The remainder of
the affair was monotonous even to the enthusiast, as
Rennert was content to remain mostly underneath,
while the Buttan applied a series of grape-vines, leg-and-
toe holds, which puzzled the exhibitionist. Singh on one
occasion almost secured a fall with an arm-and-toe hold.
The German’s great strength, however, enabled him to
break away. Rennert essayed a couple of wrist rolls,
which failed to come off. Singh tried a “bluff” by
leaping in the air after nearly downing his man, but the
referee told him to get work. After If hour’s grappling,
with the Hindoo top-dog, they were called off and
Singh declared “champion” once again, as he had
secured one fall in the time limit. On the
other night’s form Gus couldn’t win if given a day,
except under Tivoli exhibition conditions; for then the
Buttan would consent to be pressed under in the stipu-
lated ten minutes, perhaps. An effort to place the cham-
pionship on a White Australian basis will be made by
Bain, a recent arrival from Scotland, at an early date.
Writer, however, considers Australia’s only hope is in the
return to the game by the youthful Weber. And Weber
says he is not going to return. Fifty-year-old Buttan, of
course, must presently be beaten by age, but when he
drops out there will be another Hindu to fill his place.

Schoolboy athletes, at the Victorian Schools
Championships, promoted by theV.A.A.A., at the Mel-
bourne ground, on Friday last, made a somewhat wonder-
ful showing. In the under-14-year section a long, lean
lad, one G. L. Ulbrich, scored a treble. He won the 100
and 440yds. races, and in the high jump he cleared
4ft. llin., with a bit to spare. Senator Best’s youngsters
ran first and second in the 120yds. hurdles, A. Best
defeating his brother by a yard in 17 l-ssec.—a good go.
J. Brake, with a pole, put 9ft. 3£in. underneath him—
a feat that would win five out of six Victorian
open championships. H. S. Trangmar (Ballarat) did
sft. (>|in. in the tall jump, which is considerably above the
average. S. Leverett, an under-16 youngster, covered
100yds. in 10 9-10 sec.—same time as the open 100 was won
in. The Vic. athletic nipper is pushing his barrow right
up against all-comers in amateur competition, and
it is very rarely that the schoolboy doesn’t pick
some of the plums (especially in the field games)
at the V.A.A.A. championships. The State school
sports, held on the same ground the Wednesday prior to
the aforesaid, showed the State-educated kiddie a decided
inferior in the athletic line. The private and public
schoolboy has usually the advantage of an ex-athlete as
coach, whereas the State schools are controlled, generally
speaking, by teachers who were never athletes ; and the
kiddies have no cricket and football ground incidental to
the premises.
Santos Dumont, the flying Brazilian who lives
in Paris, has left aerostats and turned his attention to
hydroplanes. Someone bet him a small fortune that
he could not make a machine capable of travelling over
or through or under the water at the rate of 60 miles an
hour, and the impulsive Brazilian accepted. To carry off
the loot he has made a fearful and wonderful contraption—
three sharply-pointed cigar-shapen pontoons of wood and
aluminium, Covered with an air and water-tight fabric,
and filled with compressed air. The centre float, which
is the main part of the machine, is 33ft. long, the side
ones a tenth of that length, and the three are held together
by transverse submerged planes. Santos himself will steer
the thing, from a bicycle saddle set well back on the
middle cigar, with a steering wheel like that of a motor
car. A 16-cylinder, 120 h.p. Antoinette engine, weighing
5001 b. only, will drive a huge three-winged aero-pro-
peller set high over the prow. When properly going the
thing will rise up and travel on the points of the three
cigar-shaped cylinders. But if Santos is not careful
about the adjustment of his aero-propeller, he won’t
travel over the water, but fly through the air. A speed
of 45 miles an hour has already been attained on a much
less elaborate machine.
* * * *
Daredevils H. L. Stevens and H. B. James are
on the warpath once more. They haven’t slept peace-
fully since a Yankee, named S. D. Waldron, haa the
impertinence to lower the Australian-made world’s motor
road record of 556 miles in a day on a low-powered car.
James and Kellow put up those figures. Yankee Waldron
rushed the distance up to 606 miles on a high-powered
car, capable of developing 50 h.p. Kellow and James
made a game effort to cut down the Yankee figures last
April, and covered 307 miles in the first 12 hours. Then
the windows of Heaven opened, and the mud vats of the
great deep were broken up, and the attempt went to
Sheol. This time Stevens and James will use a4O h.p.
Darracq, Dunlop-shod, 60 miles-an-hour car, and either
break that Yankee record or burst something.
Two Adelaide motorists, Messrs. Dutton and
Aunger, have decided to try the Adelaide to Port Darwin
run along the line of the overland telegraph. The
machine chosen is a 24 h.p. Talbot, and they hope to
average 100 miles per day, and thus run through in about
20 days. The car, which is being specially fitted to carry
a large supply of petrol, has plenty of reserve power
for ploughing the sandy stretches. The greatest diffi-
culty is expected in negotiating the McDonald Ranges
and getting across the unbridged rivers of the Northern
Territory. Still, where a bike rider could go alone ought
to be practicable for two motorists, and Albert McDonald
ran through the same stretch on a Dunlop-shod Swift
machine in 1898 in 28 days 15 hours, and didn’t seem any
the worse for the experience. And, before that, Jerome
Murif, a back-country rider who had never entered in a
race in his life, filled in a month’s holiday the same way.
The progress of the motor. It is estimated that
on Cup Day, in Melbourne, there were no less than
£120,000 worth of cars in the Victorian capital. One
garage, that of the Kellow Motor Co., went to the trouble
of working out the value of the cars it contained that
night, and the result of the tally was 80, ranging
from 15 to 45 h.p., and worth £58,650.

The programme for the Austral Wheel race is
out at last, and states that a two-days’ meeting will be
held on the grass track at Melbourne Cricket Ground,
on the 7th and 14th December, and will consist of
seven races and a novelty exhibition. The Aus-
tral Wheel race prize money is not fixed, but will be
20 per cent, of the takings. The first prize will be 60,
the second 30, and the third 10 per cent, of whatever a
fifth of the gross takings are. The Austral Wheel
meeting has fallen from its high estate, and the
total prize monej' the club is committed to, in-
cluding the £lOO minimum for the Austral race
itself, is £249. Entries close on November 23.
A fearful roar of disapproval from racing men is begin-
ning to make itself heard. But the racing men have only
themselves to blame; by their crooked games they
destroyed the public interest in the sport, and now they
are feeling the pinch.
* * » *
A syndicate, with Yankee cyclist MacFarland
pushing vigorously behind, is being formed in Melbourne
to erect an indoor cycle saucer track. The enclosure
will resemble the Salt Lake Oity track in U.S.A. —being
constructed of boards with very steep banking, and a
circumference of eight laps to the mile. The idea comes
with highly-painted praise by riders and public. Another
co. has a similar venture in hand at Newcastle (N.S.W.).
The Maoriland professional Rugby team came
to a sudden smash at Wigan, in Lancashire, where a
team of Geordies ran over them to the tune of 12 points
to eight. The cable mentions that the pace was
“tremendous” throughout, and that the Wigan team
led from the jump. The Maorilanders are more formid-
able in attack than defence, and the Geordies seem to
have rushed them from the first, and smothered the
halves. This is the team’s first defeat, although in the
match against Wakefield Trinity things were so close
that the game ended in a draw of five points each.
Apropos , our only Messenger is winning great admiration
and being hailed as the finest three-quarter in the British
Empire.
* \ * *
Interest in sculling seems to be slack in N.S. W.,
and Tresidder has practically had to ask for an extension
of time in which to find the second deposit of the stake
money. If he manages to get the second one, there are
more than grave doubts about the ability to raise the
final one, and the chances of Tresidder bringing back the
championship grow dim and remote. Maoriland is said
to be aghast, and quite unable to understand how the
home of so many mighty scullers, world champions in
their day, can have so little interest. The truth of the
matter is, probably, that Tresidder’s chances are not
fancied.
* * * •
Sydney’s steady, reliable colored bruiser, welter-
weight Joe Walcott, wrecked another reputation on
Saturday night last. A young man with the rhythmical
name of Jack Black recently arrived in Sydney with, he
said, gory honors thick upon him from the rings of
Britain and South Africa. He was a sort of antipodean
Bill Squires, inasmuch as his training impressed the
knowing ones very favorably indeed ; and some were
ready to believe him a coming champion. And, on
Saturday night, it all went up in steam and perspiration.
Reliable coffee-colored Joseph fell on him, and, though
somewhat damaged and occasionally baffled, he managed
to lead on points right through. Black seemed anxious
to make it a wrestling match, and, after several warnings,
was disqualified in the sixth round.
» * » *
Leura (N.S.W.) Golf Club has imported John
Donald, a champion professional golfer from the other
end of the world, to show Australians the tricks of the
game, and, incidentally, to boom it on the Blue Moun-
tains resort. Donald comes with a big record of successes.
* * * *
Golf is coming into conflict with the Education
Act. Caddies are as necessary for the fashionable game
as fierce-looking stockings, and as its exponents are of a
class which can indulge their vice on week days, the em-
ployment of youngsters as caddies interferes with
school attendance. It is computed that the kiddies
earned £lOO in fees during the recent big tourna-
ments at Sandringham (Vic.). Good caddies are diffi-
cult to find, and golfers hang on to their special
favorites, and are prepared to pay for the privilege
of first call on their services. Caddying takes
some learning, and consumes sufficient time to make a
hole in the lads’ school attendances. Hence when a
caddy has perfected himself in his “profession,” it is safe
to say his education has suffered. Prosecutions of
parents of Victorian caddies have resulted in fines of 25.,
and it was stated in court that some of the bo.vs have
earned 30s. a week. It can be seen that the truant in-
spectors will have a job in tackling this class of stray.

The mail reports of the Squires-Sullivan fight
contain the startling' announcement that Squires’ punch
has lost its weight. He is reported to have landed on
Sullivan’s nose, and driven in heart blows in showers, and
inu*" <^one nothing worse than spoil his opponent’s beauty.
The only bright spot in the story is that Squires was game
as a bull ant, and came up cheerfully after each knock-
down blow to take the rest of his gruel. He was spread-
eagled three times in the final round, and his seconds,
seeing that the game was hopeless, flung in the towel to
save further damage to Willie’s face. Amurkan papers
sa,y there is nothing for Willie now to do but return home
with all the credit that an unsuccessful fighter can carry
‘O r pluck, strength, willingness, and everything but
the ability to fight a winning fight with a top-notcher.
* * * *
. -A- man from Numurkah (Vic.) makes a few
insulting remarks: “Quite agree with ‘Snell’ r&
Squires (B. 10/10/’O7). The man who wrote the preceding
par knows as much about boxing as my terrier knows
about the Boer War. The real fact of the matter is, that
Sqmres, by his last fight, has asserted his right to be re-
garded as a coming champion. There probably isn’t
much difference between Sullivan and Burns. In fact,
on performances, Sullivan is the superior man. Yet it
took Sullivan 19 rounds to accomplish what Burns, three
months previously, managed in less than one. That
speaks volumes for Squires’ improvement. Squires,
practically speaking, is only now picking up experience.
He was (and is) so far away superior to Australia’s next
best that he never had to fight to beat him. As to re-
turning to Melbourne, Squires has no intention of so
doing. In fact, he states definitely he will not do so till
he is champion, and Squires is no mere blowhard. Willy
has a very repressive jaw. In my opinion less than six
months will see our William champion of the earth.”
The writer of the “preceding par’’alluded to here
rises to remark that, although the above is patriotic, it is
rot. Willie Squires was never anything but a rusher, with
a ferocious wallop and a reputation made by John Wren’s
hard-working office journalist. As long as he met
‘natural fighters,” who allowed themselves to be rushed,
the wallop did the rest. But in the U.S.A. he has met
men who simply would not be rushed, and who knew
enough to keep out of the way of the wallop. They are
trained fighters, whose first lessons are to avoid knock-
ouc blows, and their teachers are men who have reduced
the business of keeping out of the way to a science.
The whole trouble is that Willie never learned to fight—
he was a hard hitter who mistook courage, determination
and recklessness for fistic skill. Had he been caught
young and trained properly he would, no doubt, have
made a magnificent fighter ; but he wasn’t trained pro-
perly. The fact that Sullivan took 19 rounds to finish
the trouble only means that Sullivan took no risks ; but
he won. And in the States they do not give the eaten
lion any chances. Of course, as long as Squires has large
sums of money to lose he will be “ obliged,” but when
his money has gone he will get no more opportunities to
fight front-rankers.
* * * *
The first of the month saw the beginning of
N.S.W.’s trout-fishing season. The trout is one of the
rare wild products of the Cold Country that hasn’t
wreaked disaster to our suffering backblocks. All through
the Monaro—on the Snowy, Eucumbene, Badja and such
like streams —the native snares this fish in large quantities
now, and with it snares the lodger and tourists from the
city. As in Maoriland the trout is going to be a big factor |
in the prosperity of whatever districts cultivate it.
At what age and under what circumstances
may a man be reasonably presumed to have thrown
a seven ? Twenty-seven years ago a William
(then aged 46), farmer, of Queensland, cleared
out from his wife and family, taking something
feminine with him. His son, who was nine years
of age when dad went over the rim, asked Justice
Real (Q.) for letters of administration in connec-
tion with 64 acres of land which the flitter
had to leave behind in the hurry of" his de-
parture. No proof was offered that William
had died, and, as the Judge hinted, it is no un-
usual circumstance for these bucolic Lotharios to
attain the age of 73 —which is this particular
Lothario’s figure if he still lives. Still the appli-
cation was granted. Not improbably it was on
the sound general principle that if William isn’t
dead he ought to be.
The so-called “Ninety-mile Desert,” about
the S. A.-Victorian border, on the main railway-
line, is kicking against its name. Wheat is being
grown freely on it wherever the dense mallee
scrub is cleared, and a rainfall record for seven
years (the first three of them very dry) shows an
average of 19 inches. This is as good as, or
better than, half the wheat-growing centres of
S.A., within liin. of Adelaide itself, and nearly
2in. better than Roseworthy, the State’s Agricul-
tural College, so the name of Desert seems
uncalled-for.
“Huffin-Guffin”: For weeks we have
watched it rise, slowly, but surely. Nothing to
measure how fast it grew each day ; but day by
day it has grown. At first it began to peep above
the walls of the surrounding buildings. Then the
great circular, slightly-tapered column seemed to
push itself up so that it might look over the city.
No noise about its growth. Scarcely a sign of
life. A light tripod kept its apex a little above
everything else in the centre of the circle. An
occasional head of a man (growing smaller day
after day) bobbed above the edge of the round
tower. That was all. At last one day there came
a change from the plain circle of bricks. The top
was begun. We could watch the daily growth
then, as there was something to fix the eye upon.
Now it is getting the final course of ornamental
bricks, and the great smoke stack of Melbourne’s
municipal electric light works in Spencer-street
holds its head 175ft. high. Another monument
to the skill of Australian builders, and the steady
nerves of Australian workers.
Naval expert Biles is protesting over-much
that his visit to Australia is not connected with
the building of an Australian navy. Biles is
spending exactly a fortnight in Australia, which,
to say the least of it, is a hurried trip for a Scot-
tish professor on a pleasure jaunt. Part of the
fortnight has already been used up, not in sight-
seeing, but solid work at Lithgow and other
places, where work, and not fun, is the objective.
The explanation that Biles was on his way from
Bombay to Calcutta, and just dropped into Aus-
tralia on the way, is the sort of explanation that
an average householder wouldn’t like to try on
his wife, and is much the same as a resident of
Sydney saying that he looked into Hobart on his
way home from Adelaide.
“ M.M.” : A wayback Gippsland town has
just been bereft of its main interest. The same
was the State school-house, which had become so
riddled by white ants that a walking stick,
prodded incautiously into any of the upright
beams or joists, was liable to drop away into
nothingness, and be lost irretrievably. As soon
as the children left for school, such of the inhabi-
tants as had any sporting spirit whatever as-
sembled at Che grocer’s, and here a book was
made on the individual chance of each separate
child returning alive to tea that night—little Eric
Henry, the publican’s youngest, whose seat was
under a massive cross beam, being invariably a
rank outsider. Last week a breeze sprang up
during the night, and the entire structure col-
lapsed, killing a stray dog (the only occupant of
the place at the time).
IN CAMERA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Lyman Pierce, of the Y.M.0.A., says that there
can be no objection to boxing’ as conducted by his insti-
tution. It is the crowds that make a man anxious to hit
the other’s solar plexus. “When only two or three are
looking on, there is no such temptation.”
The stoush that is dispensed by Wren,
Is mostly blood and bruise and hair ;
The thousands cheer the count of ten,
The punches on the bodies bare ;
With hooks they drag the corpses out
Between each conflict fast and fierce ;
But there is nothing rude about
The stoush dispensed by Lyman Pierce.
His pugs are cultured persons ; if
They jab, they whisper “ If you please ”;
Observing “ May I?” with the biff
That sends the other to his knees.
“ Excuse me, James ; my little joke,”
They mention as he blindly rolls
Upon the floor ; and then they soak
Their knuckles red in finger-bowls.
For Lyman has discovered why
The other pugs are raw and rough ;
The crowd it is that rouses high
The instincts primitive and tough ;
They would not punch each other blind
If people were not there to see ;
And so the noble art’s refined
When seen by only two or three.
But pretty though its tinkle sounds,
In Lyman’s lute there is a rift ;
Some lads through eighty-seven rounds
Most conscientiously have biffed,
Without a gong or referee,
Without a soul to yell “ Hooray,”
They’ve dealt it out in ecstasy,
And willing fought the livelong day.
N.S.W.
MILTON MACGREGOR.

THE Beale Australian Pianos are built specially to withstand
sudden and extreme climatic changes. The timber used is
seasoned here in Australia, in the very climate and under the
very -conditions that are to be found throughout the year. As former
importers, we know the value of this, for our experience has taught
us that imported pianos, which are built during the European sum-
mer., stand this climate better than those which are prepared there
•during the winter. Further, our method of seasoning, by combined
■open-air and kiln process, has been pronounced by experts to be one
of the best and most up-to-date systems. In addition, every stick of
timber, before being used in a Beale Australian Piano, is carefully
tested in a special apparatus, which at once reveals whether it is fit
for use. Then our patent system of building the cabinet work in
cross-plies; our patented iron wrest-plank, eliminating the wooden
wrest-plank, still used in all other Pianos (iron and steel will stand
climatic change better than wood), and our special sounding-board,
which is constructed to resist the most trying heat—all ,these
combine to make the Beale Australian Piano proof against the severe
and sudden changes of weather which make such havoc of the
majority of imported Pianos. And this is why, wherever the Beale
Piano goes, it gives the most lasting satisfaction : this is why, wherever
a Piano is needed for a home in a district where the climate is
trying, the Beale Australian Piano is the best obtainable; and ,it
naturally follows that we have thousands of testimonials from ajl
classes of the community confirming and corroborating our claims,
and speaking in the most glowing terms of the
BEALE AUSTRALIAN PIANOS.

Interstate Mail
Steamshijx Lines
T is a liner of tbe Howard
Smith fleet that holds the
mermaid’s view this issue>
and the eye of the man
about to travel is invited to
note the roomy decks, the
long promenades, and the general air of space,
all of which make for comfortable travelling.
The great secret of pleasant voyaging is plenty of
room to knock about, plenty of shady spots where
one may lounge in a deck chair, and read and smoke
between well-cooked and pleasantly-served meals.
That is for the un-nautical; and most travellers
are non-nautical. For the other fellow, who has
ocean foam in his blood, the small boat that flies
skyward on the crest of every roller, and falls down
into great, silent, breathless depths between moun-
tains of water, is the one that appeals. But the
landsmen and the women love the great, steady boat
that divides the seas and merely undulates slightly
when great waves roll by, and as they are in the
great, overwhelming majority, the fleets of Howard
Smith Ltd. and all the rest of the Five Companies
have been brought to their present size and luxury to
meet their wishes.
Looking through some travel-notes recently, the
writer noticed that a man, who had travelled right
round the Australian coast in the various steamers of
the Five Companies, remarked that travelling along
the Australian coast was very pleasant, as the
Australian waters were much calmer than those
of other countries, and there was an almost
entire absence of rolling. Which is very
flattering to the Five Companies. Asa
matter of fact, the Austra-
lian seas are as wild as
any of them, and it is
not the calmness
of the water,
but the ex-
cellent de-
signing of
the ships,
that was
respon-
sible for
his increased pleasure. At various times inventive
brains have devised means of doing away with
the roll of ships. One genius elaborated a swinging
cabin which, being balanced on roller bearings and
other contrivances, inclined the opposite way to
that which the ship rolled. He said it would
abolish sea-sickness. Instead of which, it made
even the officers and stewards decidedly ill. They
felt both motions, and people who were quite
inured to one motion were broken up completely
when they found themselves double-banked by
this contrivance to keep them upright. As a
matter of fact, a well-designed ship that has
stability never does roll, and what people take
for rolling is more often than not merely the rise and
fall of the ship on the waves—an up and down motion
instead of a side to side roll. For this rise and fall there
is only one remedy, and that is increased sizeof the hull.
As ships grow larger they sink deeper into the water,
and are less amenable to the surface disturbances of
it; for, after all, the disturbance does not go down very
far. The wildest gales in the stormy Atlantic are
said not to disturb the water 300 ft. below, while the
ordinary heave and roll of the sea is only a matter of
about 20ft. So when one gets on board 4000-ton
liners like the Coovna or Bombala, there is practi-
cally no disturbance worthy of notice—if the
ships are properly designed, that is ; and the fact
that the Australian coast has earned the name of
being a calm coast, proves to people who have
watched the gales sweeping over it, that these ships
are well-designed. Which is natural enough—they
all come from the best yards in Britain, which is the
greatest ship-building country on the planet.
<23?
The Howard Smith Company have long possessed
a flyer in the Peregrine, but in the run from Sydney to
Melbourne, on Saturday and Sunday week, the big
4000-tonner Bombala proved that this fortunate com-
pany has two flyers. Pitted against some of the
fastest boats that run round the Australian coast, the
Bombala was only beaten by the huge 9DOO-ton twin-
screwed German liner Bulow by 13 minutes, and left
the rest behind. All of them were loaded up with
tourists to see the running of the Melbourne Cup,
but the Cup performance was not half so interesting
or creditable as the race in which they themselves
took part Passengers describe it as intensely interest-
ing; the Australian boat held its own for a long time,
and was several minutes ahead of the European boat.
Had the captain of the Bombala liked to push his
ship he could have made much faster time, but the
Howard Smith Co. do not allow the racing proclivities
of passengers to be indulged in at the cost of straining
the engines. All the same, it was an interesting
display, and shows that the Australian coastal marine
is composed of unusually good ships, which are quite
equal in speed, etc., to those turned out in Continental
Europe with the help of enormous State subsidies.
Needless to say, the Australian owners are not
assisted in that way.
However, in that respect, Australia seems to be
waking up. It is now proposed to subsidise certain
lines of boats running to various places outside the
Commonwealth. And there are some places inside
the Commonwealth which ought to be developed.
The Northern Territory, for instance. That some-
thing should be done to fill up that great empty
space which lies invitingly empty for the first aggres-
sor is one of the great Australian national necessities.
If the Commonwealth Government really wants to
fill up the blank, and has any ideas of how
to induce population to settle in it, there is
no earthly reason why it should not start to carry
out the good work NOW. To wait till a railway is
built is absurd. That will take years. The first
thing to be done is to get people settled there, and
to have a population that can make use of the strategic
railway when it is made. And the way to do that is
to get people settled there, and come to an arrange-
ment with the Five Companies to take them there.
The steamers of the Five Companies developed Wes-
tralia quickly and easily enough immediately there was
something there for people to go to; and they can do
the same with the Northern Territory. The only thing
required is for the Australian Government to make
it worth their while to go there, and the
Five Companies can be relied upon
to do the rest. As the North will
be artificially developed before
its time for national and
strategic purposes, it will
be necessary to give the
people who develop
it extra facilities in
the shape of low
fares and cheap
freights; and to do
that it will be
necessary that the
steamship compan-
ies who undertake
the work should
be subsidised.

The Palmist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ t OU are married,” said the Palmist,
if with an expectant eye and an air of
finality.
The lawyer’s wife said, with a start of pleased
surprise, that she was.
A large flowered cretonne curtain hung half-
way across the small room. There was a broken
pane of glass in .the window looking out on to the
narrow back-yard, and through it a gust of wind
blew and flickered the bare gas flamg.
The pecuniary advantage which the Palmist
derived from her practice of the occult sciences
was not so large as to enable her to live by that
alone. By day' she was a washerwoman. At
night she removed her white apron and insignia
of office, and arrayed herself in a frayed red satin
wrapper, took her hair out of its myriad curling
pins, and was ready to receive her clients. Out-
side her door, facing the unlovely side street, there
hung a large square of cardboard, bidding the
passer-by in uneven lettering, to “ Consult the
Palmist.” The feminine passer-by, frequently
took the hint.
The lawyer’s wife had heard of the Palmist
from her cook. She had, as in duty bound,
lectured that domestic on the folly of believing
anything such women told her,, but the cook had
stoutly persisted that it was to the Palmist she
owed her foreknowledge of her brother’s death
when his neck was broken by an outlaw brumby.
The advantage of such knowledge remained to be
proved, since it-could not avert the calamity.
But that was not the point; the fact remained, and
established the Palmist’s reputation in the lawyer’s
house.
The lawyer himself did not know anything
about it. He had once sharply forbidden his
wife to have any dealings with a gipsy door-to-
door clairvoyant, so she did not think it necessary
to tell him where she was going the night she
slipped out to find the Palmist’s house. He was
under the impression that she had gone to see her
friend, the doctor’s sister, along the terrace.
The lawyer’s wife had removed her wedding
ring, as a sort of test of the Palmist’s power's, and
she bent eagerly forward to hear her future fore-
casted, after the preliminary announcement.
The Palmist laid a square-tipped, not over-clean
forefinger (she-had been cleaning, saucepans since
her frugal tea) in the soft pink palm of the lady’s
hand.
“ I see widder’ood plain marked,” she said,
watching the other’s face. The lawyer’s wife
tried to look shocked.
“ Not till I am quite old, though ? ” she asked,
with studied carelessness.
“ Indeed no, madam, I grieve to say it is pro-
bably within the next two months. But don’t
fret yourself, you will marry agin the young man
whose eye is upon you, and be most ’appy. I see
a man ’ere, dark—tall—’ansome. . .”
The lawyer’s wife smiled. The man she was
thinking of was neither dark nor very tall, but
those were mere details. There was a man who
loved her more, she was sure, than her self-
absorbed husband had ever done, and if she were
free, she-felt he would lose no time in telling her
so. But
The Palmist shook her head in a melancholy
fashion, and a curling pin she had forgotten
wagged dejectedly over her forehead. “If you
don’t want to lose your present ’usband,” she
said, fancying that she caught a gleam of regret in
the prospective widow’s eyes, “tell ’im to be
careful of street crossin’s.”
The lawyer’s wife drew her hand away from the
greasy clasp of the seer’s fingers:
“Of course I don’t want to lose my husband,”
she said sharply, and opening her purse she
inquired the fee.
The Prophetess waved her hand grandly and
said carelessly, “ One shilling for the left ’and,
and another shilling if I read the cards.”
The lawyer’s wife decided that she would not
consult the cards just then.
She picked her way through the mud of the
main thoroughfare, her little brain busy with a
thousand fond imaginings.
Conventionally-bred and frugal-minded, she
was not the woman to dare society or the voice of
scandal and think the world well lost for love.
There had never been an action in her life at which
Mrs. Grundy could point a finger. And yet
She would not have done anything to risk her
position as a respected wife, or the loss of the
generous dress allowance which her husband gave
her. And yet
Well, the Judge’s Associate was her husband’s
friend, and women have ways of knowing. She
knew how much he cared.
If there was anything in what the Palmist said !
Two months ! A year’s mourning, of course, and
then.. . the property secured to her,’ the pro-
prieties satisfied. . . and love at last, such as she
could appreciate. . . the love of words and
caresses. ..
She had always been a good wife to the lawyer.
No one could say she had not, that was one
comfort.
She stepped on to the tram-line to cross the
street, her fair head bent, her unseeing eyes on
the gilded future. She had hastily thrown a cloak
over her dinner-dress when she went forth to seek
the Palmist that night. The wind caught her hat,
and as she raised her hand to her head to secure
the hatpins, her trailing skirts tripped her, and
she fell. Motor No. 493 was coming at a rush
down the hill. The motor-man saw something on
the rails, and threw all his weight on to the brake
handles, but the pace was too great on the incline,
or he was half-a-second too late. The car stopped
dead with a jar, and above the screams of the
women on the front seats came the long shrill cry
of the lawyer’s wife.
* * * *
In the private ward there was the strong reek
of iodoform mingling with the sickly, choking
smell of ether.
Only her head moved restlessly from side to
side of the low pillow. Her body was quite still.
Her fair hair was neatly plaited on each side of
her small head. It was a wonder the nurse had
troubled, the doctor thought, since she must die
before the dawn, but order is the first law in
hospitals. So when her husband came in to the
room to see her, he thought she looked much as
usual, except for the faint greenish white which
succeeds the anaesthetic in most cases.
He laid his long fingers lightly on her damp
forehead. ’ '
“Dear,” he said softly, “are you any.easier
now ? ”
She opened her eyes with a frightened look,
and closed them again. She could not bear the
tenderness in his eyes. She had never realised it
before. Was it because he thought she was going
to die ? Her lips moved dumbly. She was not
reproaching herself. She had always been a good
wife. She meant to go to God with that sentence
on her lips, and if he was a conventional God he
: would, of course, understand. But her husband
had never understood her ! She did not want his
love now ;it was too late. She wondered where
the other man was. How stupid ; of course he
could not outrage the proprieties by coming here !
People would expect that she would wish to be
alone with her husband. She must die as she had
lived, in the odor of respectability and in her hus-
band’s arms most likely. Why hadn’t she thrust
aside the barriers long ago and lived and loved her.
brief life as she willed. If .she had known—if she
had only known.
A sudden fury shook her morphia-dulled nerves-
and brought back a racking agony as she remem-
bered the Palmist. Surely it ought not to be her-
self who was lying there. She raised her head a
little on the pillow, her eyes dilated, and her lips
twisted with her effort to speak.
The lawyer put his arm gently under her head.
“ You wanted to tell me something ? ” he said
softly, and bent to catch her whisper.
But when he heard it he did not understand,
and thought that she was wandering, for these
were the last words she spoke before she died :
“ She lied —the Palmist lied to me.”
M. FORREST.
An interesting document has 'just come
the way of Adelaide Public Library Board. The
London Colonial Office recently rooted out for,
conflagration purposes the. old papers in connec-
tion with the ' Colonial Land and Emigration
Commissioners, a Board appointed in 1840 to deal
with, among other matters, emigration to the
colonies. The' Board was abolished in 1878.
Among the ancient stationery was found the
Royal Commission issued to the S.A. Colonisa-
tion Commissioners, July 19th, 1841, and the
Commissioners’ seal. The relics have been handed
over to Adelaide Library.
A correspondent from Nagasaki, Japan
(28/9/’O7), gives some sidelights on the “ perfect
civilisation ” of England’s brown ally :
Although the minor eccentricities of Japanese civilisa-
tion need not be too seriously taken as representative of
the genius of the civilisation itself, yet the almost uni-
versal indifference in matters of decency sharply differ-
entiates the Jap from any European folk.
A more revolting feature of Japanese civilisation is its
heartless and cruel immorality. The social system that
permits and encourages among parents the sale of inno-
cent daughters, against their will, to a life that forever
condemns the child to a condition of the most inhuman
slavery,.is too infamous jto be accorded a place among the
customs of a high civilisation. Yet it is State-sanctioned
in Japan.
The utter lack of chivalry for womanhood, and the
general indifference of the strong toward the sorrows
and misfortunes of the weak, arrest the attention of the
foreign resident. In other countries, should a pedestrian
be attacked by a robber on the public street, the victim
would not be disappointed in expecting the assistance
of every passer-by,. In Japan it is not so. Every
Japanese would look upon it as none of his business.
Only the other day a foreigner was going down the street-
in Yokohama (one of the largest cities of the empire)
about eight o’clock in the evening. As his jinricksha
passed an open lot he heard a woman scream. His ’rick-
sha men wanted to ignore the woman’s cries, but the man
refused, and ran to the rescue. He discovered a young
girl in the clutches of a Japanese, and beat theffirute off.
Having relieved the girl, the foreigner was bent upon
capturing the fiend who had attacked her, but failed.
No less than 30 citizens came up to see the fun, and all
calmly stood by and refused to assist in arresting the
criminal. Recently, in a Kobe tram Car, because the con-
ductor, who was a mere boy, insisted on being paid the
fare, two drunken roughs fell upon him and beat him
mercilessly, while a whole car full of men sat by without
offering- any interference. This sort of thing is a serious
reflection on the civilisation that can tolerate it.
A further astonishing characteristic of Japanese civili-
sation is the “ thug ” system. This is, of course, a relic
of the savage customs of feudal times, when certain
hands of roughs, called “soshi,” were hired by discom-
fited warriors, or by citizens who had been insulted, to
avenge their wrongs and get even with their detractors.
The “soshi” were, to the common man, what the re-
tainers were to the feudal lord —his avengers in time of
need, only they were open to hire. Even to-day if any
man wants to punish his enemy he in ay employ these
thugs to undertake it for him. When it is remembered
that these things are not accidental, but under certain
circumstances matters of course, the social status that
is thereby represented must be justly discredited.
“ Stony ” : Jones, who works in the same
office as myself, is a family sort of man, and lives
near by a public hall. He came on the scene of
his labors this morning with a tale of wrath and
woe. It seems that there is a certain Professor of
Cooking (no less) perambulating the suburbs of
Sydney, and he came to the hall adjacent to
Jones. On the day of the Prof.’s lectures, a pert,
pink-faced female arrived at the Jones dwelling,
and asked Mrs. J. for the loan of a skillet. The
skillet was lent. During the afternoon the student
came back at short intervals to demand an
enamelled pot, a dough-board, firewood, matches,
three plates, a pound of flour, a piece of butter,
some sugar, and a washing-up dish. This young
woman offered no security whatever for the return
of the goods, and Mrs. J. remained in a state of
nerves right through the afternoon until
the things were brought back, all more or less the
worse for wear, particularly the flour and butter,
which the Professor ultimately handed over with
his compliments and thanks, in the form of a
calamitous-looking wedge of cake or bun. Jones
intends to put in the whole of next Saturday
afternoon wire-netting his home, in order to keep
the professor and his myrmidons off the ancestral
door-mat; and, with that object, has already
written J. Carruthers, demanding that 14| miles
of the recently-smuggled article shall be forwarded
to him instantly. Jones lives and votes in St.
George.
Thus “Mate,” sadly :
The British seaman —as distinct from the officer—is
fading out—partly because the pay and conditions are so
much worse than on shore, partly because there are so
few ships that will carry him. You can wander all day
round the docks in any big English port without finding
a ship that has an Englishman for’ard. Where they are
not Lascars or Chows they are Dagoes or Dutchmen. As
a sample—one Glasgow firm, trading to the Mediter-
ranean, had a standing rule that none but Dagoes were to
be employed. The mate who took Britons did so at the
risk of his billet. Principal reason was that the British
crew went on day wages in their home port, and conse-
quently had to be paid overtime for shifting at night.
The Dagoes lived on board all the time, and would turn
out at any hour. About three years ago the whole fleet
was put under the Greek flag, and now carries Dagoes fore
and aft.
In the last 12 months Richmond just
beat Footscray as the Victorian railway station
that shipped off the biggest number of passengers
(leaving Melbourne out of the count). They were
the only two stations whose outward passengers
passed the two millions; but South Yarra was
close up with 1,884,529. Melbourne (Flinders-
Street) record was 4,681,720 for suburban stations
and 377,720 fpr the country—an average of 13,865
a day.
4 4 Coalopolis ” : Apropos of 44 The Beale
Book” in the November Lone Hand. The
article recalls to mind unpleasant experiences I
had with a company who make it a payable,hobby
to palm off electric belts as cures for everything.
I answered one of their ads., and asked for their
free book ; in reply got one, also tons of letters.
I dropped the subject as quickly as I took it up ;
but they weren’t to be shaken off, and kept on
writing, and not only that, handed over, or sold,
to some brother company my good name, and also
my address. No more quacks for me! The
Lone Hand is to be thanked for its article,
otherwise I, for one, would not have, probably,
ever heard of “ The Beale Book.”


B. SEPPELT & SONS, LIMITED.
VIGNERONS, DISTILLERS, CORDIAL AND
VINEGAR MAKERS, SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
A MAMMOTH INDUSTRY.
THE LARGEST WINERY IN THE
WHOLE WORLD.
The Seppeltsfield Estate —one of the show places
of South Australia —is situated near Greenock, 45
miles north of Adelaide. Mr. J. E. Seppelt, one
of the pioneers, settled there in 1851, and subse-
quently planted a small vineyard, and excavated a
Cellar 24 feet by 36 feet. This was the beginning
of what has become the premier winery of
the world. To-day the extent of the premises
simply astonishes visitors to this industrial centre.
The S.A. Register recently, in commencing an
article descriptive of the concern, said, “It is no
easy matter to take up the pen and write about
Seppeltsfield. The magnitude of the establish-
ment simply paralyses you.” To convey some
idea of achievements we give the following facts :
The holding capacity of the receptacles for liquids
is 1,370,000 gallons. The stock of wines reaches
the great total of 1,200,000 gallons.
Grape Brandy and other grape spirit, 52,000
proof gallons ; and vinegars, 90,000 gallons.
* * » *
Grapes are purchased yearly from about 2,570
acres of vineyards.
* * *
The two stills are capable of producing 480
gallons of spirit per day. Eleven coopers are
employed permanently on the premises, but
periodical contracts are made with outside coopers
to meet unexpected requirements.
The tonnage of goods in and out of Seppeltsfield
for the year totals about 8000.
•■* * »
If, immediately after a vintage, the liquids in the
cellars and other buildings were bottled, and
the bottles (there would be 8,220,000) placed
end to end, they would reach a distance of 1557
miles, so, if placed along the line of railway, they
would extend from Adelaide to Toowoomba, in
Queensland.
* * * *
The Seppeltsfield of to-day is a monument to
the dominant skill and indefatigable energy of Mr.
•Benno Seppelt, the present head of the proprie-
tary.
When Mr. P. B. Burgoyne, the London wine
merchant, was in Adelaide, he publicly remarked
regarding Seppeltsfield :
“You may go the world over and not find more
intelligence thrown into an industry or branch of
branch of commerce, nor will you find greatel
perfection in wine establishments of any country.
Mr. Seppelt is one of the most useful members of
your community, and an example of success
which follows where interest in one’s work is the
dominating influence on the life.”
* * * *
Viscount des Garets, a vigneron of Epernay,
France, visited Seppeltsfield some time ago, and,
when interviewed by representatives of the press,
remarked :
“I think Seppeltsfield the most wonderfur
winery in the world. I shall never forget the
kindness I received there, and the magnitude of
the establishment amazed me. I had the oppor-
tunity to sample wines and brandy. The wines
were really excellent and far ahead of what I
tasted in other States, and the brandy was some
of the finest I have ever tasted. The fact that I
am taking a number of cases of these goods home
with me is the best proof of what 1 think of them.”
* * * *
Prof. Perkins, the South Australian Government
Viticulturalist, in writing on this subject, stated: —
“ I know of no cellars, either here or in Europe,
so perfectly conceived from the general plan down
to the minutest detail for handling economically
enormous bulks of wine.”
Notwithstanding the complete descriptions, Sep-
peltsfield must be seen before its industrial move-
ments there can be fully grasped.
* # * *
It is a township in itself, and one wonders how,
in the comparatively short space of half-a-century,
such a magnificent concern has evolved from
so tiny a beginning. It is simply the
old story of the splendid oak tree from
the little acorn. But the growing tree was
tended by one who determined, in his early life,
that a vigorous growth should continue until his
ideal was reached. At the earliest stage of the
industry Mr. Benno Seppelt planned the Seppelts-
field of to-day, and progression went on bit by
bit until it became what it is. To achieve an
output of the great bulks of liquids produced, com-
mercial branches of the business are established in
Adelaide, Brisbane, Fremantle, and Broken Hill,
with a capable staff in each.
The Commonwealth requires more individuals
of Mr. Seppelt’s type. Industries will make a
nation when legislation fails to do so.
Prices of wool are still sky-high, and
growers and agents and buyers are making big
money, but up to now the ordinary storemen, the
men who toil in the smelly, greasy, unpleasant
stores round Circular Quay, Sydney, pressing and
humping the wool, have got no benefit from all the
fatness. Indeed, the decrease in their wages made
in the old drought times has never been wiped
out. The storemen work 70 hours a week, less
meal hours, and are paid poor wages. This is
during the season. For months in the year they
are out of employment. The reason of their
troubles is that they are “free laborers,’’ and
without organisation. But at last they are going
to form a trades union, and demand an eight-
hour day for a start. The wool-selling agencies are
aghast, of course, and are raising the good old
bogey, “ the prestige of the port.” But the men
have got used to that hifalutin’ now, and are cool
enough to reply that if prestige depends on
starvation pay, the prestige had better be emptied
out into the harbor.
By this paper’s Oldest Inhabitant:—
An historic building at Newtown, St. Stephen’s
schoolroom, has just been demolished to make room for
a new structure. This was the church of the suburb
before the finer edifice in Church-street was erected. It
was consecrated on Sept. 9, 1845, by Bishop Broughton,
then known as Bishop of Australia. The church was
crowded at that old-time consecration, and amongst the
congregation were Lady Gipps (wife of the Governor),
Maurice O’Connell (Commander of the Forces), and the
Mayor of Sydney (George Allen). The ground for the
church was given by the trustees of Mrs. Bligh, wife of
Governor Bligh, who had a big grant here. The last
minister of the parish, Mr. Taylor, just dead, was 41
years in charge. One of his immediate predecessors was
Rev. C. C. Kemp, who married the widow of William
Toogood, who at various times kept Toogood’s Hotel, on
the S.E. corner of Market and Pitt streets, the Golden
Fleece, on the N.E. corner of King and George streets,
and the Rose and Crown, on the N.E. corner King and
Pitt streets. Mr. Kemp induced the widow when she
became Mrs. Kemp to refuse a continuance of a publican’s
license for the Golden Fleece corner, which was her pro-
perty. As a result, the City Bank became the tenants,
and kept the corner for years.

HALF-YEARLY REPORT oT the Directors of the BANK of AUSTRALASIA, with the
Accounts for the Half-Year to 15th April, 1907.
Presented to the Proprietors at the Half-Yearly General Meeting held on Thursday, 3rd October, 1907.
REPORT.
The Directors submit to the Proprietors the balance-sheet as at 15th April last, with the profit
account for the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and
colonial rates and taxes, and for all bad and doubtful debts, and after writing down investments to
market price or under, the net profit for the half-year, including some exceptional recoveries from old
accounts, amounted to £227,463 7s. 4d. .
To this sum must be added £16,861125. 7d. brought forward from the previous ha f-year making
a total of £244,324 19s. lid., out of which the Directors have declared a dividend for the half-year at
the rate of 12 per cent, per annum, or £2 Bs. per share, and a bonus of Bs. per share, free of income tax.
The dividend and bonus will absorb £112,000, and the Directors have further appropriated £lOO,OOO
to the Reserve Fund and £15,000 in reduction of the cost of bank premises. There will then remain
£17,324 19s. lid. to be carried forward to the next account.
The dividend and bonus will be payable in London and in the colonies on 4th October next.
For the vacancy on the Court of Directors, already reported, Mr. Charles Edward Barnett and
Mr. William Howard Smith are candidates, and the Directors have much pleasure in recommending
the election of Mr. Barnett.
C. W. FREMANTLE, Chairman.
4 Threadneedle-street, London, 23rd September, 1907.

STRANGE
PREDICTIONS
Planetary Professor Foretells
Events, and Quickly Reads the
Lives of People, though Thou-
sands of Miles Away .
Sends Letters to the Rich and Poor
Alike, in which he advises them about
BUSINESS, MARRIAGE, SPECULATION,
LOVE AFFAIRS, WEALTH, ETC.
Offers Readings to all who write and
send Date of Birth, Sex, Married or
Single, with 1/- to cover cost of
postage, etc.
IN his office in Melbourne, surrounded by
Charts and Dials of strange design, Prof.
Henri De Astro studies daily over the lives
of men and women who have written him for
advice on affairs of business, love, speculation,
travel, marriage, health, and the important
events of life. The following letter gives an
idea of
MR. DE ASTRO’S ABILITY.
My Advice Brought Money.
Brisbane.
I am proud to tell you that I have profited greatly
by following the advice contained in my Horoscope.
I had about given up taking a journey, but when you
advised it so strongly, I reconsidered and took the
journey, passed my examinations, and am now in a
position to earn from £2O to £25 per month. I would
not take £lO for the advice you gave me.
I believe every young person starting in life should
by all means secure a Horoscope from you. Nothing
I ever received has given me such power to control
conditions and circumstances as your Horoscope.
Wishing you every success, I am, yours truly,
B. H. RANDALL.
THE accuracy of recent predictions made by
this eminent Astrologer has caused many
of his friends to believe that he possesses
a supernatural power, but he modestly asserts
that his predictions are due alone to a scien-
tific understanding of natural laws. The many
thankful letters Prof. De Astro has received
from people who have benefited by his advice
furnish ample proof that he is sincere in his
work and has a kindly feeling towards
humanity.
Readers of The Bulletin can obtain a
Reading by addressing a letter to Prof. De
Astro, 366 Bourke Street, Melbourne. Simply
say you wish a Reading of your Life, stating
your birth date, sex, and whether married
or single, enclosing Is. Stamps or Postal Note
to pay postage etc., and the Reading will
be promptly sent.

A CURED WOMAN
Relates her Intense Sufferings—how Doctors
Failed to do her any Good. She Finally Tried
Clements Tonic
Which Makes Permanent Cure.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
Here is a narrative which will assuredly put many a reader on the right track—supplied to us by
Mrs. Annie Young, of Myrtle-street, Leichhardt. It was at Mrs. Young’s present address, where
she has been living for the past eleven years, that our special correspondent was granted the inter-
view which appears hereunder : —-
“It is thirteen years since I had the illness you refer to. It doesn’t seem half so long ago, for
everything is as fresh in my memory as though it was only yesterday.”
“Were you experiencing any physical pain ? ”
“Oh ! don’t talk about it. Have you ever had neuralgia in the head ? I hope not, for it was
something dreadful. Right across the forehead, through from one temple to the other, and up to the
top of my head, the most acute pain imaginable harassed me. The agony was so great that often I
imagined something was pressing on my brain, and I really used to think I would go mad with it.
It had such an effect on me, indeed, that even my sight went bad, and, as for the feeling in my eyes
—well, they felt as if they were double the size they should have been. Ah ! what desolate, melan-
choly days they were ! I’m sure I felt myself to be about the most unhappy woman in the world,
and I think I had occasion to be, in view of the fact that the outlook was so black,”
“Do you mean that your prospects of getting strong again seemed remote?”
“ They did, indeed, for I had been attended to by several doctors, and the most they could do for
me was to give just a little relief now and then ; but there was nothing lasting about it, and if I felt a
trifle better one day, I was sure to feel ten times worse the next, and the medicine, besides being so
ineffective, was always disagreeable to take. A strange thing was that I felt always so worn-out,
drowsy and exhausted—as if I was only fit to go somewhere and lie down and die—yet, when I made
an effort to sleep, do you think I could ? Not a bit of it. I’d twist and turn nearly the whole night
through, and when at last I dosed off the dreams I had were fearful.”
“ And you couldn’t get permanent relief from these symptoms through the medicine prescribed
for you ?”
“ No, unfortunately. But finally I got something to do me good. Clements Tonic it was, and
the best remedy I ever had in my life, too. Goodness, what a show it made of everything
else I had taken, for instead of only easing me a little occasionally, it went right to the base of my
infirmities, and lifted them out by the roots ! In addition to what I have already told you, I must
state that I was highly nervous—nobody could be more so, for I was actually afraid to stay in my own
house by myself, and was continually worrying myself about things that I imagined were going to
happen. Trouble was always hanging over me, and I couldn’t get the idea out of my head—not until
I had been taking Clements Tonic for a space of time, when, quite mysteriously, I ceased brooding
over such silly things, and my mind became as happy as the day. Oh !it was wonderful what an
altered woman Clements Tonic made me. Instead of being anxious and timid all day, I got as happy
and cheerful as possible—nothing frightened me, and my nerves were steady. It is because I know
so many nervous women that I deem it my duty to say what a splendid remedy Clements Tonic is for
that complaint, though, for that matter, it was equally beneficial to me in other ways. I used to get
pains down the small of my back and about my shoulders, also palpitation and pains in the region of
my heart, which made me think that something very serious was wrong with me there; but Clements
Tonic showed itself to be such a pain-removing medium that I can faithfully recommend it to all
sufferers. Its regulating influences on the heart were extraordinary, and for making one’s sleep sound
and peaceful the same remedy was the only one to meet with my requirements. I wish I had used it
months before, for plenty of people told me what a grand medicine Clements Tonic was. The least I
can do is to assist my neighbors to throw off their afflictions, so you may publish my experiences in
any manner you like.
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, ANNIE YOUNG, of Myrtle-street, Leichhardt, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission
to publish in any wry my statements—which I give voluntarily, without receiving any payment ; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the. same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oarha
Act, 1900. (Sgd.) ANNIE YOUNG
Subscribed and declared at Petersham this fifth day of September, one thousand nine hundred and four
before me, H. EVANS, J.p. ’
Take a Trip!
Get away from the counting-house
and from business cares.
Go into the country, among the
mountains.
And you’ll come back with fresh
vigor and a brand-new set of ideas for
another twelve months’ work.
To make sure of a good holiday, go
to the “ Hydro-Majestic,” Medlow Bath.
It is situated high above the sea-level,
3,500 feet up in the clear air, surrounded
by the most beautiful scenic effects that
the world of Nature has produced.
There your tired system may re-
cuperate. There your every wish is
anticipated and provided for in such a
way as to make life really enjoyable.
The meals are served in a large and
well-aired dining room, which opens out
on to a beautiful garden surrounding the
Hydro. All provisions are fresh and good,
being supplied direct from the Hydro’s
Own farm in the Kanimbla Valley.
The days may be spent in Walking
Tours, Motor or Coach Trips, exercising on
the Golf Links or at Tennis and Croquet,
whilst the evenings pass merrily away to
the accompaniment of first-class music,
games, cards, and other entertainments.
And although the arrangements for
your comfort are much superior to those
procurable elsewhere, the terms are not
excessive, as enquiries at the Hydro City
Office, 38 Castlereagh St., City, will show.
Telephone 4349, or write direct to
Hydro-Majestic,
Medlow Bath.
