One Muskerry—a Ber-ritish nobleman —
shocked the House of Lords (19/7/’O7) by the fol-
lowing deplorably commonsense observations :
The grievances of British ships’ officers included nig-
gardly pay, inferior, insufficient and perhaps insanitary
accommodation, no guarantee of continuous employment,
dismissal for trifling reasons or none at all, unjust sus-
pension of certificates, no leave from very arduous
duties, continuous labor from day to day and night to
night, and frequent Sunday labor, long absence from
friends and home, and want of proper rest owing to in-
sufficiency of officers engaged.
The Victorian Inspector of Explosives has
returned to Melbourne after inquiries in Europe.
According to his report the British pattern of
cordite is dangerous. He was told that the firing
of a big gun
results in a large accumulation of residual gases within
the gun after discharge. When the breech is opened,
these gases, issuing into contact with the oxygen of the
air, burst into flame, hence what is known as “the back-
flash.”
Cordite, being intended to be burned in a close
vessel, should contain such an amount of sup-
porters of combustion that all its inflammable
constituents will be burned while the shot is
passing along the bore of the gun. Further
Experts are now agreed that the War Office and the
Admiralty will have to make some change in their pre-
sent powder.
And the Commonwealth will have to meekly wait
until that change is made before it can get safe
cordite. If we had our own high explosive factory
we might by this time have got at least to the
German level of safe cordite, perhaps to the
Japanese pitch of something a shade better than
cordite.
“ Billy T.” writes :
London Paunch has printed a cartoon deriding the
Australian tariff so far as the preference to Britain is con-
cerned. The picture represents the tariff wall—an old
wall with a new tier of bricks on it. On top sits the sturdy
male figure of Australia. The Ma Country, a very portly,
elderly person holding tip her skirts, is approaching the
wall, at the foot of which is a stool marked “ 5 per cent.
Preference.” “ I shall never get over it,” gasps Ma
Country. “That’s all right, Mother, I’ve put an
eight-inch footstool for you,” replies Australia.
Melbourne Argus reprinted this cartoon with a great
deal of exultation, .and Australians are invited to feel
contempt for themselves, because of this ungallant con-
duct towards an adipose old lady. It strikes me that
the allegory cuts the other way. If the Ma land is too
beefy, unwieldy, scant of breath, and lacking in the capa-
city to climb, even with the special assistance extended
to her, surely it is not for us to lower our wall. Rather is
it a matter of urgent necessity for Mrs. Bull to go into
training, shake off her superfluous scrap fat, and hustle till
she is more fit for encountering obstacles. A bulky
Britannia like the ex-washerwoman in the Paunch cartoon
could hardly climb over 2d. in coppers.
“ Yedette ” : In walking around the nar-
row railway line leading to the Barren Jack Moun-
tain, where the work of building the big barrage is
in progress, one is struck with the immensity of
the scheme. Great masses of rock have to be cut
through, to build the diversion channel, into
which the Murrumbidgee will be forced, so that
the natural bed of the river may be cemented.
Below the railway cutting the river roars and
surges in a narrow gorge, sending up showers of
spray. Above the seething of the water sound
the clatter and throb of the big steam drilling
machines as they pierce the granite floor of the
river bed. The “swish, swish” of the mighty
centrifugal pump next breaks upon the ear, as it
sucks the water out of the diversion channel to
enable the work of blasting to proceed. To either
side the big mountains raise their towering forms,
and on Black Andrew a hundred men are working
to cut a platform to support the cable towers.
Now and again a huge rock will be displaced and
clatter and tear down the precipice into the river.
Now a load of cement comes down the “ flying
fox” cableways extending hundreds of feet in
length, flying past like four generations of
chain lightning. The whirring of the heavy
engines, as they supply the motive power for all
this work, re-echoes amongst the rocks and crags.
The immensity of the work impresses. Will it
make the Western Plains blossom with verdant
splendor as is claimed ?
Law as laid down by the Botany Bay
Bench of the early days was really the cause of
most massacres of the aborigines in the past.
Judge Advocate Atkins, in a letter to Governor
King, in 1805, alleged that the blacks had no
position before the law, and could only be dealt
with when they did wrong as people deal with
brutes. The letter referred to ran:—
The object of this letter is to impress the idea that the
natives of this country, generally speaking, are at present
incapable of being brought before a criminal court, either
as criminals or as evidences ; that it would be a mockery
of judicial process and a solecism in law, and that the
only mode at present when they deserve it is to pursue
and inflict such punishment as they deserve.
That letter was written just after a slaughter of
blacks on the Hawkesbury, where, according to
the official account, Dunn “ killed one,” Andrew
Thompson “a considerable number,” Obadiah
Ikins “many,” and so on. Atkins’ argument
against bringing blacks to trial was that they were
incompetent to plead, chiefly because they did not
understand the oath. Atkins’ idea became the
law of the time and was carried west and north
by adventurous white men, producing “dis-
persals ” as a natural consequence.
“ Wang ” : I arrived on the scene just in
time to witness an unrehearsed performance, the
other day. Leisurely pedalling his bike up
Swanston-street was a cyclist. Suddenly an ex-
cited yelp cut through the atmosphere, followed
instantly by its owner, a dog of nondescript breed.
It rushed straight at the cyclist, who saw it
coming and sprinted. Too late. The man made
a wipe at the canine with a parcel he carried,
and missed. The two-penn’orth of meat
turned to avoid the blow, and somehow his
fly-flicker found its way between the spokes of
the bicycle wheel near the axle. Then a sur-
prised dog found itself being wound up.
The tripe hound made frantic endeavors to get
away ; so did the cyclist. But the dog displayed
great affection for him. It caught him by the
trousers and hung on. The cyclist dismounted
ungracefully, and in great haste, on his hands
and knees, and, giving a cow-kick, got away
■ minus several square inches of trousers leg.
Mongrel began to make things hum then. His
tail piece was immovable, and he attacked the
bike-wheel with great gallantry. A sizzing sound
came from the tyre, where he had mouthed it. On
the foothpath an animated conversation took place
between the tyke’s owner and the bikers owner.
The Law came hurrying up. Rushing to a. build-
ing in process of erection, the Law seized a
scaffolding pole and made a smite, which stunned
the tyke and wrecked the bike. Then, as he
extricated the animal from the machine, two loud
and angry owners demanded compensation.
Several amateur lawyers pleaded their cases
vociferously. But the Law was equal to the occa-
sion. With a flourish of his hand he commanded
them to move on. “Gib now,” he said, “or I’ll
run you in for creating a disturbance.” Then the
cause of all the trouble opened its eyes, and,
noting a clear track, hurried away with a scared
look and a badly-mangled tail.

WHAT IT MEANS
to Have your body contain an abundant supply of Electricity. It means strength, vigor, vitality, freedom from pains and
aches, steady nerves, self confidence, ambition and success, for success cannot be reached without these things. It means that if you
suffer from Nervousness, Weakness, Debility, Rheumatism, Kidney Trouble, Sciatica, Asthma, Indigestion
OP Constipation, that if you will stop dosing yourself with useless and dangerous drugs, and fill your nerves, blood, muscles
and tissues with the electric strength they lack, that you will soon have as sound and strong a body as Nature intended you to have.
No weakness or disease can exist in a body fully charged with Electricity—the fact that it does exist is the strongest proof that the
body lacks Electricity. Science has proven that a strong, vigorous, healthy body contains a large amount of Electricity.
WHAT THE FOREMOST MEDICAL SCIENTISTS SAY
Prof. O. S. FOWLER
Prop. O. S. Fowler, in his work on the treat-
ment of nervous diseases, says:— “Sufferers
little realise how far they are restorable, yet
they seek relief in wrong directions, for medi-
cine camiot cure this class of disease ; but, on
the contrary, they almost universally prove in-
jurious. They are no more adapted to reach these
cases than a dose of ipecac or jalap is to assuage
a mother’s grief for the loss of her darling babe,
for the disease—being largely local —the restora-
tives must be applied directly to those prostrated
parts, and since electricity is undoubtedly the
instrumentality of all life, it necessarily follows
that this element constitutes Nature’s own most
potent remedy in these diseases when rightly
applied.”
Prof. EDGAR S. LARKIN
Pros. Edgar L. Larkin, the eminent scientist, discussing “ The
New Physiology,” in an article dated June 24, 1905, says:—“The
trend of science is toward electricity on all sides. Discoveries made
hour by hour point to one grand conclusion—the substratum of
Nature is Electricity. Life, force and mind are intimately related
with it. All animals are merely electrical machines. A man,
brain and body, is a battery, and nerves correspond to wires.
It is now certain that transmission of sensation over them is
electric. Nerves have been cut and a galvanometer inserted ; the
needle moved, proving that nerve power is electric. The human
system is an electro-chemic battery, and the life principle is
electrical. The electric age is here. Drugs are hard hit on
all sides, and an intelligent physician tells me that he has almost
entirely stopped their use. The word health now means a normal
supply of Electricity in the body, and the word disease means an
insufficiency of that power.”
Prof. JACQUBS LOEB
Prof. Jacques Loeb, of California University,
who is undoubtedly one of the world’s fore-
most authorities on electric currents as ap-
plied to the human body; after much experi-
menting has made this announcement : “ A
part of the chemical energy of food-stuffs is
transformed into electrical energy, which in
turn gives energy to the muscles and or-
gans of the body. . . . Electricity, and
not heat, is the life of the body. These
discoveries are going to upset the theories of
medicine as administered by the physicians
of to-day, as it is settled that Electricity is
the basis of human vitality.”
The most advanced scientists in the world having advocated the use of electricity in preference to drugs, it only remains for you who suffer to obtain the best and
most advanced method of applying it. This is only to be found in the improved Dp. McLaughlin’s Electric Belt, which is the standard electric body
battery of the world and the only appliance adapted for self treatment. You wear it for a few hours a day or at night while you sleep. Used as we advise, with special
attachments for all parts of the body so that the current can be directed to the weakened or ailing part, it sends a strong current of life-giving electricity into the
weakened nerves, tissues and muscles, forcing into them a strong circulation ot hot blood charged with electricity. That is how it cures and strengthens
permanently —by forcing the blood to make strength —and that is why a permanent cure is established in from one to six months according to the conditions.

The Maoriland Compensation Court has
awarded Randall Johnston £106,502 for the Te
Arai Estate, Poverty Bay, taken for closer settle-
ment. Mr Johnston claimed £127,000.
“ Cogna ” : Re society par. (Bulletin
27/6/’O7) on Germans standing the Union Jack on
its head. I've sailed under it for 33 years, and the
only way I know of finding out which way to
hoist it is by keeping the toggle on top and the
lanyard down. You must mean the ensign of
the red variety. That, reversed, is the distress
signal.
“ Mate ” : He shortage of mates, the
phenomenon is explicable mainly on the grounds
of shortage of romance. The ships we used to
take such pride in—regular traders to Australia
and India —have gone out. Their apprentices
could count on a month or more at home ever} 7
year. They lorded it over their late schoolmates
in fascinating caps and brass buttons; talked
largely of the girls of Sydney and Melbourne, and
the bazaars of Bombay and Calcutta; spun
delightful yarns full of sea boots and oilskins, the
reefing of top-sails and club-hauling in a gale,
accompanied by much inaccurate technicality.
The returned seafarers slurred over the cleaning
ot brass, and chambermaiding of fowls and pigs.
Zu th , ey mention salt horse and pantiles, or
the absence of butter and milk—well, those were
incidentals and fitted in with the rest. Then-
yarns induced an atmosphere of thrill that was
priceless as a mate-supplying factor. Now-a-
tnnes the sailing-ship apprentice is generally away
two or three years on his first voyage. At the end
thereof he’s thinking of the more sordid side of
things and taking an interest in mates’ wages.
Also a somewhat stiff exam, is looming in the
near future, and he doesn’t, inconsequence, recruit
worth a ticky. The steamer apprentice—if he
gets home at all—has at most a week of a spell.
He recalls the trimming of coals and cleaning of
brass that lately occupied him, and the recollection
doesn t make for big talk. If he’s in a swagger
line he s introduced to the higher mathematics of
navigation and kept doing interminable sums.
He can t spin attractive and blood-stirring yarns
about logarithms and star positions. And his
associates sympathise but aren’t anxious to follow
his example. The shipowner will have to make
the game worth while in pay and conditions. If
he trusts, as he has done so far, to the Romantic
Gall of the Sea, and the Adventurous Spirit of
British Boys, he’ll be a very sad shipowner in the
near future, or else he’ll get Chow mates, and the
country will be even sadder than he.

Contentment.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OMAN, perplexed, but aye persistent sower
Of seeds whose growth you often may not
wait !
Pained parent of posterity, bestower
On ages, yet unhandseled, of their fate !
Of all the moral senses in your giving,
Why do you in such sparsity hand down
The thing of things to make life worth the living,
Contentment, Virtue’s diamond-crested crown ?
Without contentment none are truly wealthy,
And with it never any one was poor.
It germinates in systems that are healthy,
And fruits alike in city or on moor.
Environment does not suffice to grow it.
But mental strength its roots should fertilise;
And fools and feather-heads shall scarcely know it,
For patience is the treasure of the wise.
The finest word that anywhere is spoken
Is Equanimity, the Buddha finds ;
To own it means felicity 11 . broken,
For happiness endures m peaceful minds.
And neither can elation nor resentment
Disturb the even tenor of their ways
Who worship in the Temple of Contentment,
And dwell within its shadow all their days.
And Sadi, in his meek and simple fashion,
Has wondered much why Adam’s children must
Be harborers of arrogance and passion—
They sprang from, and they all return to, dust.
All equal at their birth and at their dying,
No transitory chances should prevent
Philosophy’s contentment from applying
Its balance to their level settlement.
The pauper, when his end is good and cheerful,
Fares better than the King who dies in woe.
Who nothing has, of nothing need be fearful,
And lightly as he came, so shall he go.
Contentment much prefers to patch its tatters,
Or treat its ventilation as a joke,
To forfeiting the only thing that matters,
Its self-respect, by begging for a cloak.
But, were the lords of plenty only given
An equitable vision, there might be
No poverty, to mendicancy driven,
Less discontent with life’s allotted fee.
The “madness of comparison ” might leave us,
The nurture of bad feeling cease at last,
False pride’s misunderstandings never cleave us,
And egoism moulder in the past.
And so I supplicate the Gods of Kindness—
For kindly gods there somewhere surely are—
To help our struggling peoples in their blindness,
And draw their veil from Truth’s revealing star.
Then man, and that still vainer thing, his woman,
No longer swayed by envious desire,
May quit an emulation all unhuman,
That treads the weak and luckless in the mire,
To turn their thoughts and aims on something
better
Than steps on artificial planes of rank ;
To throw aside the money claims that fetter,
And wake discordant echoes with their clank ;
And, bringing out the best in one another,
In communable brotherhood to try
To make this world as fair as that far other
We dream about achieving when we die.
N'.S.W.
TALAFEKAU.
The Rising of the Court.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE same dingy court room, deep and dim,
like a well, with the clock high up on the
wall, and the doors low down in it ; with
the rather shoddy throne-bench, the Royal arms
above, and the little witness-box to one side,
where so many pitiful, pathetic and noble lies are
told by pitiful sinners and disreputable heroes for
a little liberty for a lost self, or for the sake of a
fiiend of a “pal’' or a “cobber.” The same
overworked and underpaid magistrate trying to
keep his attention fixed on the same old miserable
scene before him ; the same row of big, strong,
healthy, good-natured policemen ; and the police-
court solicitors (those awful police-court solicitors)
wrangling over misery for a crust, and making
sordid “jokes,” in the hope that the newspapers
will notice them.
Opposite the Bench the spiked dock, divided
by a partition, with the women to the left and the
men to the right. (They bring children here no
longer.) There are shaking, wild-eyed drunks and
disorderlies, and a decently-dressed but trembling
and conscience-stricken little wretch up for petty
larceny, or something. (His bosses have sent a
solicitor to prosecute, and a ‘ £ manager ” or some
understrapper to give evidence.)
Over there, on a form to one side of the bench
opposite the witness-box—and in a patch of sun-
light from the skylight, as it happens—sit the
“ Prisoners’ Aid,” “ Prison Gate,” and “Rescue”
people (one or two of the women in nurses’ uni-
forms), who are come to help us and to fight for us
against the Law of the Land.
* * * *
Mrs. Johnson, of Red Rock Lane, is here, and
her rival, One-Eyed Kate, and Cock-Eyed Sal,
and one or two of the other aristocrats of the
alley. And the weeping, bedraggled remains of
what was once, and not so long ago, a pretty,
slight, fair-haired and blue-eyed Australian girl.’
She is up for inciting One-Eyed Kate to resist
the police. Also Three-Pea Ginger, Stousher,
and Wingy, for participation in some row
amongst the aforementioned. (Wingy is a natty
little one-armed man, whose case is usually
described in the headline as “A ’Armless Case”
by one of our great dailies. ) Their pals are
waiting putside in the vestibule—Frowsy Kate
(The Red Streak), Boko Bill, Pincher and his
“piece,” etc., getting together the stuff for the
possible fines, and the fee for the lawyer, and
ready to swear to anything, if called upon.
I, myself, on bail—charge Plain Drunk. It
was “ drunk and disorderly,” by the way, but a
kindly sergeant changed it.
Yet I am not ashamed—only comfortably
dulled and a little tired—dully interested and
observant, and hopeful for the sunlight presently.
We low persons get too great a contempt for
things to feel much ashamed at any time ; and
this very contempt keeps many of us from “ re-
forming.”
Let us go back a bit, and suppose we are still
waiting for the magistrate, and think of Last Night.
The whispering, shuffling, clicking of the court
typewriter ceases, the scene darkens, and the court
is blotted out. And —
* * • •
Drink—lurid recollection of being “searched”
■—clang of iron cell door, and I grope for and
crawl on to the slanting plank. A period of
oblivion. The soul is away in some other world.
Clang of cell door again, and soul returns in a
hurry to take heed of another soul, belonging to a
belated drunk on the plank by my side. Other
soul says :
‘ ‘ Gotta match ? ”
So we’re not in Hell yet.
We fumble and light up. They leave us our
pipes, tobacco and matches ; and presently one
knocks with his pipe on the iron trap of the
door and asks for water, which is brought in a
tin pint-pot. Then follow intervals of smoking,
incoherent mutterings that pass for conversation,
borrowings of matches, knockings with the
pannikin on the cell door wicket or trap for more
water, false and fitful starts into slumber, perhaps
—or wild attempts at flight on the part of our
souls into that other world, that the sober and
sane know nothing of. Gradually, reason (if
this world is reasonable) comes back.
“What’s your trouble ? ”
“ Don’t know. Bomb outrage, perhaps.”
“ Drunk ? ”
“Yes.”
“ What’s yours ? ”
“ Same boat.”
Presently he is uneasy (and I am getting that
way too, to tell the truth). After moving about,
and walking up and down in the narrow space as
well as we can, he “rings up ” another policeman,
who happens to be the fat one who is to be in
charge all night.
Wot’s up here ? ’*
‘ ‘ What have I been up to ? ”
“ Killin’ a Chinaman. Go to sleep.”
Blankets are thrown in. My mate wonders
whether he asked them to send to his wife to get
bail, and hopes he didn’t.
They have left our wicket open, seeing that we
are quiet. But they have seemingly left some
other wickets open also, for from a neighboring
cell comes the voice of Mrs. Johnson. They say
that Mrs. Johnson was a “lady” once, like many
of her kind; that she is not a “bad woman,”
but gets money sent to her from somewhere —
from her “family,” or her husband perhaps. But
when she lets herself loose —or, rather, when
alcohol lets her loose—she is a terror in Red
Rock Lane, and it is only her fierce, practi-
cal kindness to her unfortunate og poverty-
stricken sisters in her sober moments that keeps
her tolerated in that classic thoroughfare. She
can speak “ like a lady ” when she likes—not like
a “ woman of the world,” but as one who knew
and knows the world, and is in Hell. But now
her language is the language of a rough shearer in
a “ rough shed,” on a blazing hot day.
After a while my mate calls out:
“ Oh, for God’s sake give it a rest ! ”
Whereupon Mrs. Johnson straightway attacks
him and his ancestry, and his mental, moral, and
physical condition —especially the last. She
accuses him of every crime known to modern
Christian countries and some Asiatic and ancient
ones. She wants to know how long he has been
out of gaol for kicking his wife to pieces that time
when she was up as a witness against him, and
whether he is in for the same thing again ? (She
has never set eyes on him, by the way, nor he on
her.)
He calls back that she is not a respectable
woman, and he knows all about her.
Thereupon she shrieks at him and bangs and
kicks at her door, and demands his name and
address. She is a respectable woman, and
hundreds can prove it, and she is going to make
him prove it in open court.
He calls back that his name is Adolphus Reginald
Grainger, and he also tells her his club, and gives
her the address of his solicitor.
She bangs and shrieks again, and states that
she will get his name from the charge sheet in the
morning and have him up for criminal libel, and
have his cell mate up as a witness—and hers too.
Just here a policeman comes along and closes her
wicket with a bang and cuts her off, so that her
statements become indistinct, or come only as
shrieks from a lost soul in an underground
dungeon. He also threatens to cut us off and
smother us if we don’t shut up.
We settle down again, but presently my fellow-
captive nudges me and says : “ Listen ! ” From
another cell comes the voice of a woman singing—
the girl who is in for “inciting to resist.”
“Listen !” he says, “ that woman could sing
once !” Her voice is low’ and sweet, and plain-
tive, as of a woman who had been a singer, but
had lost her voice. And what do you think it is ?
The story of Bartimeus :
The crowd in accents hushed reply
“Jesus of Nazareth passeth by.”
Mrs. Johnson’s cell is suddenly silent. Then,
not mockingly, or scornfully, but as if the
girl is a champion of Jesus of Nazareth, and
is hurt at the ignorance of the multitude—and
pities Hhn :
Now who is this Jesus of Nazareth, say ?
The policeman, coming along the passage, closes
the wicket in her door, but softly this time, and
not before we catch the plaintive w'ords again :
The crowd in accents hushed reply—
“ Jesus of Nazareth passeth by.”
My fellow-prisoner throws the- blanket from
him impatiently, sits up with a jerk, and gropes
for his pipe.
“ God !” he says. “ But this is red hot ! Got
ennuther match ? ”
I think about the Nazarene, and sleep for
awhile. I wonder whether they’ll give us time
in the next world, so we’ll be able to sleep some
of our sins off, in the end, as we sleep our drink
off here.

FekirKsdl
A Lifelong Sufferer
Cured.
Few people nowadays are free from
Biliousness in some of its many
forms. It is an ailment caused by
derangement of the functions of
the liver. That wonderful organ
manufactures bile to assist in the
digestion of food, and at the same
time filters all waste and * armful
matter from the blend. When the
liver’s action is imperfect, the bile
becomes misdirected, and the in-
evitable result is Biliousness.
In the most obstinate cases of
Biliousness, Bile Beans are an
unfailing Cure.
“ Ever since childhood,” writes Mrs. M. J. Fairhall, of Hackett Terrace,
Richmond Hill, Charters Towers (Q.), “ I have been subject to Biliousness.
Among the many symptoms which troubled me were windy spasms, poor
appetite, spots before the eyes, and a terrible pain between the shoulder-
blades, for which I had to take morphia to relieve me. The climax
was reached in an illness which the doctors said was caused by my
liver being congested. The agony I suffered was awful. I was com-
pelled to move down south to Newcastle for a
change, with all my family, and it was in this
town I came across Bile Beans. Even from the
first few doses of this medicine I felt a great
improvement in health, and a box and a half
entirely cured me of my distressing complaint.
This is four years ago, and I have not been
troubled since, which proves that Bile Beans cure
surely and permanently.”

Then daylight and “The Paddock,” which is
the big drunks’ dormitory and dining cell at the
‘ ‘ Central. ” The men borrow and lend and
divide tobacco, lend even pipes, while some break
up hard tobacco and roll cigarettes with bits of
newspaper. If it is Sunday morning, even those
who have no hope for bail, and have a long, hor-
rible day and night before them, will sometimes
join in a cheer as the more favored are bailed.
But the others have tea and bread and butter
brought to them by one of the Prisoners’ Aid
Societies, who ask for no religion in return. They
come to save bodies, and not only to fish for souls.
The men walk up and down, and to and fro, and
cross and re-cross incessantly, as caged men and
animals always do, and as some uncaged men do
too.
“Any of you gentlemen want breakfast?”
Those who have money and appetites order;
some order for the sake of the tea alone ; and
some “shout” two or three extra breakfasts for
those who had no money when they were run
in. We of the under world can be very kind to
each other in trouble.
But now it’s time to call us out by the lists,
marshal us up in the passage, and draft us into
court. Ladies first. (But I forgot that lam out
on bail, and that the foregoing belongs to another
occasion. Or was it only imagination, or hear-
say? Writers have got themselves run in before
now in order to see and hear and feel and smell
for themselves—and write.)
“Silence! Order in the Court!” I come
like a shot out of my nightmare, or trance, or
what you will, and we all rise as the magistrate
takes his seat. None of us noticed him come in,
but he’s there, and I’ve a quaint idea that he
bowed to his audience. Kindly, humorous Mr.
Isaacs always gave me that idea. And,
while he looks over his papers, the women
seem to group themselves, unconsciously, as it
were, with Mrs. Johnson as front-centre, as
though they depended on her in some vague way.
She has slept it off, and tidied, or been tidied
up, and is as clear-headed as she ever will be.
Crouching directly behind her, and supported and
comforted on one side by One-Eyed Kate and on
the other by Cock-Eyed Sal, is the poor, be-
draggled resister of the Law, sobbing convul-
sively, her breasts and thin shoulders heaving and
shaking under her open-work blouse —the girl who
seemed to pity Jesus of Nazareth last night in her
cell. There’s very little inciting to resist about
her now. Most women can cry when they like,
I know, and many have cried men to gaol and
the gallows ; but here in this place, if a woman’s
tears can avail her anything, who dares raise a
sneer ?
His Worship looks up.
Mrs. Johnson (from the dock) : “Good morn-
ing, Mr. Isaacs. How do you do ? You’re look-
ing very well this morning, Mr. Isaacs.”
His Worship (from the Bench) : “ Thank you,
Mrs. Johnson. I’m feeling very well this
morning. ”
There’s a pause, but there is no “ laughter.”
The would-be satellites don’t know whom the laugh
might be against. His Worship bends over the
papers again, and I can see that he is having
trouble with that quaintly humorous and kindly
smile, or grin, of his. Pie has as hard a job to
control his smile and get it off his face as some
magistrates have to get a smile on to theirs. And
there is a case coming by-and-bye that he’ll have
to look a bit serious over. However—
“ Jane Johnson.”
Mrs. Johnson is here present, and reminds the
sergeant that she is.
Then begins, or does begin in most courts, the
same dreary old drone, like the giving out of a
hymn, of the same dreary old charge—“ Yo—
u d —-With —Being Drunk —And—
Disorderly —- In —Such —And —Such —A—Street
—How —Do —You Plead —Guilty— Or Not
.—Guilty? ” But they are less orthodox here. The
“ disorderly” has dropped out of Mrs. Johnson’s
charge somehow on the way from the charge
room. I don’t know what has been going on
behind the scenes, but anyway it is Christmas
time, and the Sergeant seems anxious to let Mrs.
Johnson off lightly. It means anything from 24
hours or ss. to three months on the Island for her.
The lawyers and the police —especially the lawyers
—are secretly afraid of Mrs. Johnson.
The Sergeant: “This woman has not been
here for six weeks, your Worship.”
Mrs. Johnson (who has him set and has been
waiting for him for a year or so) : “ It’s a damned
lie, Mr. Isaacs. I was here last Wednesday !”
Then, after a horrified pause : “ But I beg your
pardon, Mr. Isaacs.”
His Worship’s head goes down again. The
“laughter” doesn’t come here, either. There is
a whispered consultation, and (it being Christmas
time) they compromise with Mrs. Johnson for
“ ss. or the risin’,” and she thanks his Worship
and is escorted out —rather more hurriedly than is
comportable with her dignity, for she makes re-
marks about it.
The members of the Johnsonian sisterhood have
reason to be thankful for the “ lift ” she has given
them, for they all get off lightly ; and even the
resister of Law-and-Order is forgiven. Mrs.
Johnson has money, and is waiting outside to
stand beers for them ; she always “shouts” for
the boys when she has it. And —what good does
it all do ?
Ginger and Wingy get off with the “ option,”
and, though the fine is heavy, it is paid. They
adjourn with Boko Bill, and their politics are
lurid.
Squinny Peters (plain drunk —five bob or the
risin’), who is peculiar for always paying his fine,
elects to take it out this time. It appears that
the last time Squinny got five bob or the risin’,
he paid up like a man, and the Court rose, imme-
diately, to Squinny’s intense disgust. . lie isn’t
taking any chances this time.
Wild-Flowers Charley, who recently did a
fortnight, and has been out on bail, has had a few
drinks this morning, and, in spite of warnings from
and promises to friends, insists on “ making a state-
ment,” though by simply pleading guilty he might
get off easily. The statement lasts some five
minutes. Mr. Isaacs listens patiently and politely,
and remarks :
“ Fourteen days.”
Charley saw the humor of it afterwards, he says.
But what good does it all do ?
HENRY LAWSON.
Mafficking.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DEVINE pulled Gordon into a quiet corner,
and spoke feverishly.
“ I’m in a devil of a mess, Gordon. You
remember Mafelcing night. Well, that night I
was away from home—the only time I ever remem-
ber being away a whole night. My wife, though
we were only married then six months, didn’t
ask me any questions about it till a week ago.
Then one of those dear old schoolgirl friends of
hers (damn ’em all) came to visit us, and fainted
when she was introduced to me.”
“ That was awkward,” said Gordon.
“ While she was coming round she talked, and
now there’s Hades to pay. Now, you’re coming
up to lunch with me, and be introduced to my
wife ; and you’ve simply got to remember how
you met me in Flinders-street by appointment.
We went to the Opera House for an hour—yes,
we did. Shut up till I’ve done. Then we
picked up a few old chums, and had a real
good maffick. Don’t you remember how
we stole a cab and drove to Spencer-
street, then stole another, and drove back
to Spring-street? Yes, we did. We upset
a tram, and called at every pub we knew and a
thousand more we didn’t know. Then we all got
run in till morning, but got out early, and got off
leniently on account of the intense loyalty of the
period. Now,” anxiously, “have you got all
that in your brain pan ? You needn’t laugh.
I’m dashed serious. The wife wants an explana-
tion, and she’s got to get it.”
Gordon got a word in.
“ Look here, Dev. I’m quite willing to help
you ; but, hang it, it’s such a poor yarn. Besides,
the old schoolmate may talk some more.”
“No fear,” said Devine. “She laughs, and
says it was temporary confusion of mind and all
that kind of thing. But you know what women
are. I want that alibi , and you’ve got to provide
it. By the way, I suppose you did maffick that
night ? ”
“ Did I what ? ” Then Gordon smiled dreamily.
“And, by Jove, you remind me. I did have a
time. She was the neatest thing in pink chiffon
and openwork stockings I ever saw. I’ll swear
she was looking for some one. But after a while
she seemed to settle down and—well, she was
particularly neat. But I never found out who
she was. Eh, oh yes. I’ll come out and
do my best, but I’m a poor actor. Yes, I’ve
got it all down in my mental ledger : stolen
cab—no, cabs ; tram upset —I saw that myself,
the pink vision was with me at the time ; pubs,
etc. All right, Devine. Come on and introduce
me, and we’ll start the Ananias stakes.”
* * * *
Letter from Mr. Devine to Mr. Gordon
Dear Harry,—Just a hasty note. Lettie has not men-
tioned the subject since you explained the niylit
before last. But I must say I was much disappointed in
you. A bad actor ! Caesar ! you were worse than bad.
From the moment I introduced you you stumbled and
mumbled, and positively gibbered at the finish. She
didn’t believe you ; that I saw plainly. Yet she accepted
the whole yarn. I can’t quite understand her attitude in
the matter, but I suppose I must not complain, since she
has dropped the subject altogether since you left. In-
deed. she seems quite anxious to avoid it. Well, “all’s
well,” etc.
Thanks for your assistance, though you are, as you say,
a dashed bad actor. Yours faithfully,
J. Devine.
Gordon almost wept at this lack of appreciation.
“ A bad actor ! And I got away without Devine
even dreaming that his wife was my friend in pink
and openwork stockings—the one that mafficked !
And I managed not to fall down dead when I
saw her ! And I managed to tell her with
my eye that it was all right, and
she needn’t faint ! And I gibbered out
that story somehow, when I knew it was a
lie, and Devine knew it was a clumsy whopper,
and his wife knew it was an invention, and his
wife’s friend knew it was a yarn. And there was
that dawning comprehension on the friend’s face
—she realised that Mrs. Devine had mafficked
that night ! Yet I only broke one flower-pot
coming away, and fell down two steps ! And
I’m a bad actor ! ”
KU KLUX.
Me.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I AM the publican’s pretty daughter. I was
educated abroad. I have notions, and the
young men who sprawl over my father’s
counter do not appeal to me in the least. They
leave me very cold. Sometimes I also leave them
cold.
How would you like to be singing a most
enchanting song of Brahms’, and to have a rude
interruption in the shape of a battered-looking
head (with a bleary eye) showing round the
corner of the door, and a cultured voice murmur-
ing “ Say, Miss, can’t yer give us ‘ Stick to your
Mother, Tom ’ —something with a bit of chune
in it ? ”
I merely stop dead, with a freezing request to
the intruder to kindly close the door after him.
In spite of my fluffety hair and my soft eyes, I
can be most awe-inspiring when I like. But one
night two intruders were not to be so easily dis-
posed of. They were gawky, muddy-faced youths,
who wriggled about in chairs (while I warbled),
and would not listen to hints. They were in a
maudlin state, and ever and anon nudged each
other feebly.
I raised a proud head, and poured out French
songs in a rich voice, because I knew they de-
tested them.
In desperation, I jumped rapidly from one song
to, another.
V She’s like a hee —from flower to flower, you
. know,” tittered one, trying to work his decrepit
old* compliments on me. But in this very phrase
f suddenly spied a loop-hole of escape.
“B— What ! How dare you hint at profane
language in my company ! Will you walk out, or
will my father help you ? ”
Dumbfounded and blanker of eye than ever,
they stumbled out. I patted down my hair,
smiled roguishly at my pleasing reflection in the
mirror, and gathering my forces, swooped into a
Liszt rhapsody.
